The author of this book says: When I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is a part of medical science in its entirety, and have reached the utmost in clarity and explanation, I saw fit to complete it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the practical work.
Because practical work is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that its knowledge is almost extinct and its effect has ceased. And only a few vestiges of it remain in the books of the ancients, which have been spoiled by hands and marred by error and confusion, until its meanings have become obscure and its usefulness has diminished.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this treatise on it in a way that is explanatory, clear, and concise, and to include pictures of cauterizing irons and other surgical instruments, since this adds to the clarity and facilitates what is needed.
The reason why there is no skilled craftsman in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy and its practitioner must first train in the science of anatomy as described by Galen. until he understands the functions, forms, temperaments, connections, and separations of the organs, and the knowledge of the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, origins, the arteries and veins, their locations and origins.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of surgery, and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
Because whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have mentioned concerning anatomy will inevitably fall into error and kill people thereby, as I have witnessed many times among those who have presumed to know this science and claimed it without knowledge or training.
And that is because I saw an ignorant physician who incised a pig-like tumor in a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled until she fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who proceeded to remove a stone from a man who had been wounded in the bladder; the stone was large, so he acted rashly and removed it with a piece of the bladder substance. So the man died after about three days, and I had been called to remove it; I saw from the size of the stone and the state of the patient that I could have done that.
And I saw another physician who earned his living practicing medicine for some of the brothel keepers of our city.
Then a black boy came to him with a fracture in his leg near the heel, along with a wound.  The physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and splints very tightly, and did not leave the wound any room to breathe.
Then he gave free rein to his desires, and left him for days and told him not to loosen the bandage until his leg and foot swelled up and he was on the verge of death.
So I was called to him, and I quickly loosened the bandage; he was relieved and free from his pains, except that the corruption had become firmly established in the limb and I could not prevent it.
Then the corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
And I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, which ulcerated after days until his patient's affliction greatly increased.  That is because if a cancer is purely from black humor, then it should never be touched with an iron instrument at all.
Except if it is in a limb that can be entirely excised.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual operations are divided into two parts: an operation accompanied by safety and an operation accompanied by harm in most cases.
And I have warned in every place that comes in this book about the operation that contains risk and fear, so you should avoid and reject it.
Lest the ignorant find a way to criticize and attack, take for yourselves caution and circumspection, and for your patients gentleness and deliberation.
And use the best method leading to safety and a favorable outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases that are difficult to cure.
And purify yourselves from what you fear might cast suspicion on your religion and your worldly affairs, for this is more lasting for your reputation and higher in this world and the next for your status.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, "Do not treat a bad disease, lest you become a bad physician.
This chapter has been divided into three sections.
The first section.
On cauterization with fire and cauterization with strong medicine, methodically arranged from head to foot, with images of the instruments and cautery irons, and everything needed for the work by hand.
And the second section.
On incision, puncturing, bloodletting, cupping, wounds, and the extraction of arrows and the like, all methodically arranged, with images of the instruments.
And the third section.
On setting, reduction, and the treatment of dislocations and the like, methodically arranged from head to foot, with images of the instruments.
The first section.
On cauterization.
And before we mention its use, it is necessary to mention the manner of its benefits and harms, and in what temperament it is used.
So I say that the discussion of the manner of the benefit of cauterization and its harms is a long discussion and a precise science and a hidden secret, and a group of wise men have spoken of it.
And they differed in it, and I have shortened from their speech the little [amount], for fear of lengthening.
So I say that cauterization generally benefits every bad temperament that is with matter and without matter, except for two temperaments, and they are the hot temperament without matter and the dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed in it.  Some of them said that cauterization is beneficial in it, and others said the opposite of that, that cauterization is not suitable in a disease that is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
And it is impossible to be cured of a hot dry disease with a hot dry medicine. And he who says the opposite of that said that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot dry disease that occurs in the bodies of people. Because when you add the body of man and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the body of man cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has revealed that to me many times, except that one should not imagine that matter except one who has trained and practiced in the matter of cauterization a thorough training. And he has understood the difference in the temperaments of people and the state of diseases in themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and their duration.
But as for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially the cold and moist diseases. All physicians have agreed upon them and have not differed in the benefit of cauterization in them.
And know, O my son, that of the secret of treatment, cauterization with fire and its advantage over cauterization with a burning medicine, is that fire is a simple element whose action does not exceed the member that is cauterized, and it does not harm another connected member except a little harm.
And cauterization with a burning medicine may extend its effect to what is beyond the members, and perhaps it produces in the member a disease difficult to treat, and perhaps it kills.  And fire, because of its nobility and the excellence of its essence, does not do that except if one is excessive.
And this has become clear to us by experience, through long service and attention to the craft and understanding of the realities of the matter.
For this reason I have dispensed with lengthy discourse.  Were it not unbecoming of this book of mine, I would have presented to you concerning fire a hidden secret and the manner of its action in bodies and its expulsion of diseases with philosophical, demonstrative discourse too subtle for your understanding.
And know, O my sons, that they have differed concerning the time suitable for cauterization, and they have considered the best time to be springtime.
And I say that cauterization is suitable at every time, because the harm occurring from the time is consumed by the benefit which is brought about by cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for strong, urgent, necessary pains which do not tolerate delay, because of the fear that what follows from them may be a calamity greater than the slight harm incurred from the time.
And let it not occur to you, O my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization which cures a disease will never have a recurrence, and make it a necessity.
And the matter is not as they suppose, because cauterization is only like a medicine which changes the temperament and dries the humors which are the cause of the pains.
Except that cauterization is preferred to medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the intensity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return after a time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength of his disease, and what may be prepared in his body from the gathering of impurities in it, and his neglect of himself in acquiring them from foods and the like of such causes.
Except that the disease in which cauterization is used is a mild disease and in a member with few impurities and humors. Like cauterizing a tooth for pain and the like, for it may be possible that that pain does not return, and that is in the least.
And as for the common people's saying also that cauterization is the last of medicine, it is a correct saying, not to what they go to, for they believe that no treatment is beneficial with medicine or otherwise after the occurrence of cauterization, and the matter is otherwise.
And the meaning of cauterization being the last of medicine is only that when we use types of treatment in a disease and those medicines do not work, then we use cauterization as the last thing, and it works, so from here it happened that cauterization is the last of medicine. Not in the meaning to which the common people and many ignorant physicians went.
And the ancients mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they only said that because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and they said that the place of cauterization does not suppurate, and that is not absolutely so.
Because I have tried that and found that it only does that in some bodies, not others, and cauterization with it is better and preferable to iron, as they said, except that if you heat the cautery iron in the fire, made of gold, you will not know when it is heated to the degree you want because of the redness of the gold.
And because it quickly becomes cold, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and is spoiled, and the craftsman falls into trouble because of that. Therefore, cauterization with iron is quicker and closer to the truth for us to use, God willing.
And I have arranged this chapter on cauterization into sections which I have organized from head to foot, so that it may be easy for the student to find what he wants, God willing.
The first section.
In cauterizing the head, one cauterization.
This cauterization benefits from the excess of moisture and coldness on the brain, which are the causes of headache, and frequent colds from the head to the area of the eyes and ears, excessive sleep, and toothache and throat pains. And in general, for every illness that arises from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and catalepsy, and similar illnesses.
The method of this cauterization is that you first order the patient to vomit with a purgative medicine that cleanses the head for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, his age, and his habit.
Then you order him to shave his head with a razor, then you seat him before you cross-legged, having placed his hands on his chest.
Then you place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes; where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery, whose image is this.
Expose the bone to the extent of the head of a date pit or the size of a carob seed. Then raise your hand, otherwise reapply your hand with the same instrument, or another, if it has cooled. Until you see of the bone what I mentioned to you. Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a cotton swab in it; apply it to the place and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in butter to it and leave it there until the eschar from the cautery separates. Then treat it with the quaternary ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they have said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it discharges, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have mentioned cauterizing the skin to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the thickness of the bone is burned, then removing what was burned from the bone, and then treating it.
And others said that one should exaggerate with the cautery until it strongly affects the bone, until it falls from the bone in the shape of a carat or a small disc.  And they claimed that this vents the vapors of the head from that place, and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals. And I do not see these two types of cautery at all except in some people, and in a haphazard way.  Leaving it [untreated] is better in my opinion, and for safety. If it [the cautery] is done, then the head weakens when its natural connection is broken, as we have seen in other members.
Especially if the head of the patient is naturally weak. The first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and it is what I use, so use it and you will be safe, God willing.
Chapter Two.
Also on cauterizing the head.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head, and it lasts for the patient, and poultices, collyria, snuffs, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if a single cauterization as we have described has been performed and nothing has benefited him, then look. If the patient's head is naturally strong and not weak, and he feels intense cold, cauterize him again a little above the previous cauterization. Then cauterize each horn of his head until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is exposed. And cauterize him in the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this and do not expose the bone, for the patient will find this very painful, unlike the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head, and I will mention this cauterization in its place.
The cautery with which the horns of the head and its back are cauterized should be gentler than the cautery with which the middle of the head is cauterized.  This is its image.
Chapter Three.
Cauterization of non-chronic migraine.
If pain occurs in a fissure of the head along with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then let the patient vomit with purgative medicines for the head, and use all the treatment that I mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If that does not work, then cauterization is done in two ways. Either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides. Then, incise the place of pain in the temple with a wide scalpel until there is a space under the skin that can accommodate the clove.
Then insert it under the skin until it disappears.  Then bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it for about fifteen hours. Then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Apply a cotton pledget soaked in butter until the place suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.  If you wish, you may do this with some of the burning medicines which I gave in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is in this manner: heat the cautery, which is of this form:
It is called the nail cautery because its head is like the head of a nail; it has some perforation and in its middle a small projection.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand, turning the cautery little by little; the amount you burn should be about half the thickness of the skin. Raise your hand so that you do not burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding. Then soak cotton in salt water and apply it to the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with butter, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this migraine with the knife-like end of the cautery, taking care not to cut the artery in this chronic migraine, especially.
Chapter Four.
Cauterization of chronic migraine.
If you treat the migraine with the aforementioned treatments and what we mentioned in the classifications of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and you see a cause for which the first cauterization with medicine or fire cauterization is not sufficient. Then you should heat the knife-like cautery until it glows white, after you have marked the painful area with a line half a fingerbreadth long or so, and you should withdraw your hand once while pulling it so that you cut the artery and reach toward the bone.
However, you should be careful to avoid touching the jaw, which moves when chewing, lest you burn the muscle or its motor nerve, which would cause spasms.
And be cautious and watchful of bleeding from the artery that you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who is ignorant of what he is doing and is not a skilled and experienced practitioner; leaving it alone is better.
And the treatment of bleeding that occurs from the artery will be mentioned in its place in the book, God willing, if you see something in the condition that this cauterization cannot manage.
And you saw that the patient's body could tolerate it, cauterize in the middle of the head as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery that we mentioned in the chapter on opening the artery with the double-bladed cautery, for it is a better and more effective cautery than this.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing earaches.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold and is treated with purgatives and other treatments that we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not go away, then heat the cautery iron called the point, whose image is this.
Then, after heating it, apply it to the area around the whole ear as it goes around, or around both if the pain is in both. And keep the cauterization a little distance from the base of the ear, after marking the spot with ink.  The cauterization should be about ten points in each ear or thereabouts. Then treat the areas until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Cauterization of Paralysis.
The paralysis treated by cauterization is that type which arises from phlegm, as I have mentioned in the classification of diseases.
And one should avoid cauterizing the type which arises from dryness and spasm of the nerve.
When you have treated this type of paralysis with plasters, snuffs, and gargles, and your treatment has not been effective, then it is necessary that the patient be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the root of the ear, the second a little below his temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
And make your cauterization on the opposite side from the diseased side, because the relaxation occurs on the side which appears healthy.
The method of cauterization is to cauterize with a cautery opposite the upper tip of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the temple, its length being the length of the thumb.
Lower your hand with the cautery until you burn about half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery iron; it is a type of scalpel, the image of which has preceded, but it is a little gentler, as you see. The scalpel should be a little thicker than usual. Then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
If the apoplexy becomes chronic and you treat it with what we have mentioned, and your treatment is not effective, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize him in four places. One cauterization on each temple, one in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one at the back of the head as previously described.
The application of the cautery is as previously described.  It may also be cauterized on the stomach, which will be more effective, then treat as before.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
If it is caused by phlegm, the patient should first be given strong purgatives and brain-cleansing pills.
Then his entire head should be shaved, and a mustard plaster, described in the chapter on plasters, should be applied to the back of his head repeatedly, for this is a kind of cauterization.
Do this in the same manner as I described there; if he is cured by this, well and good; otherwise, perform three cauterizations on the back of his head, arranged in a row from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Make the distance between each cauterization the width of a finger. Then treat the cauterization as described above. If you wish to increase [the treatment] and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle area.
If you wish to increase [the treatment], cauterize the temples, then treat it until it heals. The cautery should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterizing paralysis and general body relaxation.
It is necessary that you first proceed to purify the head with the purgatives and what we have mentioned. Then shave the patient's head, then cauterize: one cauterization in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck. If, in the case of relaxation of the body, you need more than that, and the patient can tolerate it, and the disease is strong and persistent, then cauterize four times on the vertebrae of his back. And continue cauterizing until you burn most of the skin, then lift your hand, then treat him as previously mentioned until he is healed, God willing.  The cautery should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is caused by phlegm should be cauterized.  First, his brain should be cleansed with strong purgatives and with all the other treatments we mentioned in the classification, if the patient is an adult and able to take medicines.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then use gargles and mouthwashes that cleanse the brain many days beforehand, along with improving his diet.
Then shave his head, and cauterize him with one cauterization in the middle of the head, as described previously, and another cauterization at the back of the head. And one cauterization on each temple. If the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, cauterize him with the cauterizations mentioned for the person with paralysis and body relaxation, on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back; and let the cautery be olive-shaped, as described previously.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauterizations we mentioned for the paralytic.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined toward black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge him so that it cleanses his brain, as was previously explained in the classification.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tightly woven cake of linen like a circle, then place it in the middle of his head while the patient sits cross-legged, holding it from each side, then take one pound of old sheep tallow.
Then heat it over a fire with moderate heat, as much as the finger can tolerate when inserted into it, then pour it into the middle of his head in the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this for the patient once every week, along with all other good treatment, until he is cured, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures without holding the cautery iron in your hand but rather by sprinkling, for this type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply to it cotton soaked in tallow or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
On cauterizing a running eye.
If the onset of a running eye becomes apparent to you with the signs I mentioned in the classification, then act quickly and purge the patient of whatever fouls his head, and purify him of all humors, and sweat him in the bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize him in the middle of the head, then cauterize him twice on the temples if the running is from both eyes, or on one side if it is from one eye.
Then cut with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin, and let the cauterizations be lengthwise across the temples, and guard against bleeding.
If you see any of it, cut it on the spot with any remedy you can, and I will come to the wisdom in cauterizing and cutting arteries and guarding against bleeding.
And he may be cauterized in the nape of the neck under the two bones with two strong cauterizations.
Chapter Thirteen.
Concerning the cauterization of chronic weeping sores.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and are from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, from outside the head, and you are certain that this is from cold, thick, phlegmatic superfluities, then cauterize it with the cauterization that I described in the beginning for the descending water. A cauterization in the middle of the head and two cauterizations on the temples and two cauterizations in the nape of the neck under the two bones. And if you need more, then cauterize one cauterization on each side of the corner of the eye at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter Fourteen.
Concerning the cauterization of a foul-smelling nose.
If you treat it with what we mentioned in the section and the treatment is not effective, then immediately purge the patient with colocynth for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle with an olive-shaped cautery. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery, two cauterizations above the eyebrows, a little below the hair, and be careful of the artery, do not cut it. And this is the image of the nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the cauterization of eyelid ptosis.
If an eyelid is relaxed due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize a little above the eyebrows, two cauterizations on each side, keeping them distant from the temples, and let the length of each cauterization be the length of the eyebrow.
And do not exaggerate with the cautery, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and the shape of the cautery is in this manner.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its margins turn inward so that it pricks the eye, then cauterization is of two types. Either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a caustic medicine. As for cauterization with fire, you should order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes alone, if he is one who plucks them, until they grow long and even.
If they prick him while they are growing, then tie his eyes with a bandage lest they move until they grow. When they have grown and become even, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of an ivy leaf.
The beginning of the mark should be near the margins. Then place under the eyelid a piece of cotton soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a lizard. Then heat a cautery iron, its shape being this.
Then cauterize according to the mark you made, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin, which is in the shape of the ivy leaf, is completely burned, especially its surface. A sign of the success of your work is to see the eyelid wrinkle and the hair rise from the flesh of the eye.
Then lift your hand and leave it for three days. Then apply cotton with butter to it until the scab is removed. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If some of the hair returns later and the eyelid relaxes,
repeat the cauterization in that place as you did before. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural place and does not prick the eye. As for cauterization with a caustic medicine, it is that you tell the patient to leave the margins until they grow long and even.
Then make from paper the shape of an ivy leaf. Then take of the usual soap and of unslaked lime, of each one a drachm weight or so. Crush them both well together, and do this quickly lest it dry. Then spread some of it on the paper you made in the shape of an ivy leaf, and place it on one or both eyelids.  Place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
And place your index finger on top of the medicine and press it gently, while you move it whenever the patient feels the sting of the medicine, for he will feel a burning sensation. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the burning stops, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And look: if you see that the eyelid has risen as it rises when cauterized with fire or by cutting, otherwise repeat the medicine on the place where the medicine did not take effect and did not blacken, until you complete your work and the eyelid is cauterized. Then place cotton with butter on it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or another until it heals.  It is necessary for you during the operation to be extremely careful that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye. If the eyelid relaxes after several days, and something is suspected in the eye,
then repeat the medicine on that place of the eyelid which has relaxed, specifically as you did initially, then treat it until it heals. This is the image of an ivy leaf.
Know that people's eyes differ in size, so your cauterization should be according to that. The correct method is not hidden from one who has experience in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the polyp that appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the polyp with what we have mentioned in the sections on diseases and your treatment is not effective, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
You should order the patient to place his head in your lap, and a servant should hold his head between your hands in such a way that it does not move or shake.
Then you place a cotton swab moistened with egg white or saliva on his eyes, then you heat the cautery, whose image is this.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather from one end, which is the cauterizing end. And if you wish, it can be perforated at the other end; and if you wish, it can be solid like a gimlet, but this hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then you squeeze the polyp if it is open and remove its contents and dry it; or if it is not open, you open it and remove its pus. Then you apply the cautery to it while it is very hot, holding it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose during cauterization, lest your hand waver or the patient be disturbed, and the cautery fall into the conjunctiva and damage it. If you reach the bone with the first cauterization, that is well and good; otherwise, reapply the cautery a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton with butter to it and treat it with drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and treat it according to what will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a polyp, mentioned by some of the ancients: you aim at the location of the polyp, incise it, then place a thin cone in the incision itself; this is its image.
And you pour into it an amount equal to the weight of a dirham of molten lead, and hold your hand to the cone firmly and steadily, and the patient must not move at all, lest the molten lead flow into his eye.  It is necessary to place cotton soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
For the lead will burn the site of the polyp and cure it miraculously. If the polyp heals with the cauterization and treatment we have mentioned, that is well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to use the perforation of the nose and return the polyp to the nasal passage, as will be described in its special place.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, which is called a harelip, especially in the lips of children; it often happens. If you treat this cleft with what we mentioned in the classification and the treatment is ineffective, then heat a small, knife-shaped cautery in this manner.
Its cavity should be as fine as a knife; then quickly apply it, while hot, to the cleft itself, until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft. Then treat it with the kyrioti, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a polyp occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling appears at the root of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the molars, then it suppurates and bursts, and from the running pus a fistula results, and you treat it but the treatment is ineffective, then you should heat a cautery to the extent that it fits in the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterwards with what we have mentioned of treatment until it heals, God willing.
If the material is cut off and it heals, well and good; otherwise, it is necessary to examine the place and remove the diseased bone, according to what will come in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On cauterizing loose molars and gums.
If the gums are loose from moisture and the molars move, and you have treated them with medication without effect, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, whose image comes after this, after you have placed the tube on the tooth.
And insert the hot cautery iron quickly, and hold your hand a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then raise your hand, then return the cautery iron several times as you wish, then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
And let him hold it for an hour and then spit it out; for the loose tooth will become firm, and the loose gum will tighten, and the putrid moisture will dry.
Chapter twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is from cold, or if there are worms in it, and medication has not been effective, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fat, or cauterization with fire.
As for cauterization with fat, you take cow's fat and boil it in an iron spoon or in a shell, then you take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe, then dip it in the boiled fat and quickly apply it to the aching tooth and hold it until it cools.
Then repeat it several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  If you wish, you may dip wool or cotton in cold fat and apply it to the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterization with fire, take a copper or iron tube, somewhat thick in its body so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron, whose shape is shown, and place it on the tooth itself, holding your hand until the iron cools. Do this repeatedly.
Then the pain will go away, either that same day or the next. After the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good fat and hold it for an hour, then spit it out. This is the shape of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, according to what is possible. This is the shape of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing polyps.
If the polyps are due to phlegm and cold humors and do not respond to ripening medications, and you desire a quick ripening, heat a hollow cautery iron, which has this shape, perforated at both ends.
So that the smoke exits from the other end during cauterization. Apply it gently to the tumor once and again if necessary, until you reach the depth of the tumor.  If the tumor is small, make the cautery iron the size of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply cotton soaked in butter to it until what was burned by the fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and tents until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors prevail in the trachea, especially if this is accompanied by a cold temperament, then the patient should first be purged with purgative medicines, then cauterize with a cautery in the hollow of the neck at the root of the throat in the lower place.
Beware of reaching the throat with the cautery and do not burn more than half of the skin. Then cauterize another cautery at the joint of the neck at the end of its last vertebra, and let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described.
Then treat him with what has been mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors and the patient has no fever or consumption, and the disease is chronic, cauterize two cauteries above the clavicles in the low, soft, empty places, and another cautery in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
And let the cautery be nail-shaped in the manner previously described; and if you wish, let your cautery be punctate with the cautery called the point, the image of which has been previously shown in puncturing earache, and let the points number about thirty.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he is healed. You may also make a cautery with three branches in this manner, and hasten the cauterization with it, for you cauterize three cauteries at once with it.  This is the image of the cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated because of slippery humors, or if it does not remain in place when reduced from its dislocation, so that it becomes habitual for it to be relocated then dislocate at the slightest movement, as we have witnessed, then it is necessary to replace the joint first. Then the patient lies on his back or on his healthy side, then the skin inside the armpit is lifted upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inwards.
Then heat the cautery with two points, of which this is the image.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization will be four cauterizations; and it may be cauterized with a cautery with three points, so the shape of the cauterization will then be six cauterizations, and the points should be delicate.
And this is an image of the cautery with three points.
And this number may be increased by one, so that the cauterizations become eight; then apply to the cauterization finely ground balls with salt, then have the patient rest and not move the limb for a while until it strengthens, and if the dislocation is upwards, which is rarely the case.
Then cauterize above the shoulder with one good, nail-shaped cauterization or numerous cauterizations in dots, for the joint will become firm, the moisture will depart, and the patient will heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If coldness and excessive moisture occur in the stomach, to the extent that it is deranged from its temperament, and if many catarrhs occur in it, and it is treated with various treatments without success, then the patient should lie on his back, extend his legs and arms, and then be cauterized with three cauterizations. One cauterization below the sternum, the size of a finger, with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauterizations lower down on either side of the first cauterization, so that the shape of the cauterizations is triangular, and farther apart so that they do not merge if they suppurate. The depth of the cauterization should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and this should be the shape of the cauterizations.
In this manner and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery with which you perform this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
And the stomach may be cauterized by puncturing it, for one who is apprehensive of this cauterization.  This is done by marking points on the stomach with ink to the extent desired, then cauterizing them with a punctate cautery, and then treating it with the previously mentioned treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the cold cauterization of the liver.
If pain arises in the liver from coldness and dampness, or from a subtle wind, so that it departs excessively from its natural temperament, and the patient is treated with what we mentioned in the classification, and this does not benefit. Then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, and three cauterizations are marked with ink in this shape and this exact amount on the liver, below the diaphragm, where the elbow of a person ends.
And after each cauterization, a cauterization will be [made] the thickness of a finger, and the cauterization will be straight along the body, and do not press the iron firmly.
And let the amount of thickness of the skin that you burn be half, no more, and let the patient stand on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him lie down with his legs extended and his arms raised.  And this is the image of the cautery iron.
And you may cauterize these cauterizations with the scalpel-like cautery iron if you have gentleness and skill in the craft, and be cautious lest you overdo the cauterization and burn all the skin, burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines, for the skin there is thin; so know this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cauterizing a swelling of the liver.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know if that swelling is in the liver's flesh or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will find heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be intense sharpness with the pain, and you see that the physicians have been exhausted in treating it, then it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, then you mark the swollen area with ink.
Then heat the cautery in the fire; it is the cautery that resembles a hoe, and this is its image. And cauterize it with one cauterization until you burn all the skin and the cauterization reaches the peritoneum, until all the matter comes out, then treat it with the treatment of abscesses until it heals.
And this type of cauterization should not be used except by one whose training in the craft of medicine has been long and who has repeatedly experienced these diseases; then he may undertake such a work, but leaving it is better in my opinion.
Chapter twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the roots of rhubarb for a cold fistula in this manner: that you take one long, dry root of rhubarb, the longest you can find, and it should be about the thickness of a finger, then you dip it in oil and roast it in the fire. Then you cauterize with one cauterization between the connection of the clavicle and the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the jugular veins, slightly inclined towards the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two cauterizations also between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backwards, and another cauterization in the middle of the chest and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations from behind: one between the shoulders and two on the sides of the spine, lower than the cauterization which is between the shoulders.
And you should not deepen your hand with the cauterization, but it should be superficially on the skin.  Some of the ancients mentioned that some people used a cautery of iron like a probe; they would heat it and insert it between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And it expels the pus as we mentioned in the tumor of the liver.  And in this method of cauterization there is danger; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula forms in the area which will not heal.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you treat the disease of the spleen with what we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective, then cauterization is in three ways, all correct. One is to cauterize it with three or four cauterizations arranged lengthwise along the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations whose shape we presented, and between each cauterization and the next is the thickness of a finger or a little more.  The nature of the cautery is the same as that which we mentioned in cauterizing the liver. Do not deepen your hand with the cautery and the patient's position is lying on his back.
The other method is in the armpit, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left armpit ends, and you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left armpit ends, and your lifting of the skin is across the body so that the cauterizations fall along the body.
Then you insert the two cautery irons, very carefully, until you pass them through the skin on the other side, then you remove the cautery iron, so the cauterizations are four. And if you wish, you can cauterize with the other cautery iron with the three points.
Then you treat the place of the cauterization after you leave it to discharge pus for many days; this is more effective than all the treatments mentioned previously.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On cauterization for dropsy.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascites specifically, if you treat the patient with dropsy using the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize four cauterizations around the navel and one cauterization on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, and a cauterization on the spleen, and two cauterizations on his back between the vertebrae, one opposite his chest and another opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately the thickness of the skin. Then you leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
And do not neglect the patient after cauterization with what is necessary to gather the humors for him so that healing may hasten to him. The shape of the cauterizing iron with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as its form has preceded.  That with which the abdomen is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, then it is necessary to cauterize on the back of the foot in the depression between the little finger and the ring finger.
And place your hand with the cauterizing iron and do not bend it, then raise your hand and do not return it at all, for the yellow water will ooze out, and the cauterizing iron is of this shape.
Then cauterize on the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, and the cauterization is to be with the sharp end of the cauterizing iron, and the cauterization is to be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and another below it towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh, two cauterizations.
And leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time so that the water may ooze out, then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and dampness to the point of weakening the retentive and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines, and this has been treated with various treatments and it has not healed, and you see the patient able to withstand cauterization and strong, then cauterize him with a large cauterization on his stomach. As preceded in cauterizing the stomach with the circular cautery iron and four cauterizations around the navel gently with the gentle nail-shaped cautery iron and a large cauterization on the coccyx with cotton or two cauterizations.
If you see the dampness abundant and the patient able to withstand it, then cauterize him on the pubis and cauterize on each flank, and perhaps add two small cauterizations to the stomach near the large one, for it is a successful treatment whose benefit does not fail, God willing.
Chapter thirty-four.
On cauterizing the hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are chronic, numerous, or single hemorrhoids in the anus, and they are from thick, cold humors or corrupt dampness, and they have been treated with what we mentioned in the division but the treatment has not been effective, then cauterize the patient with three cauterizations on the lower part of the sacrum under the table slightly, triangularly.
And a cauterization below the navel by the length of two fingers. And if you judge that his stomach has become cold and his food does not digest, and you see his face swollen, then cauterize him on the stomach with a large cauterization as mentioned before, and a cauterization on his liver. And another on his spleen with a nail-shaped cautery. And leave the cautery open for a time, then treat him until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and persevere with your hand until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which the blood flows. Do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with appropriate ointments until they heal. And if you cauterize it with a large cautery also on cotton, it will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula which is in the anus and its surroundings.
If the patient does not consent to the incision and the procedure which we described in its place and shrinks from that, then perhaps he will heal by cauterization.  If a fistula occurs and pus and foul moisture flow from it for a long time, then the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then know its depth with the probe, then heat the cautery which resembles a needle, then insert it hot into the fistula itself, along the course of the fistula and the extent to which the probe entered, and repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt tissues are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your need.
And beware of burning a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula leads to the body of the bladder or to the body of the intestine.
Therefore, beware of all these places.  And you do this only if the fistula is in a fleshy place and you estimate that it is not penetrating. Then treat the place until it heals. If the place is sealed and the matter ceases from it and remains so for a time, then know that it has healed completely.
And if the matter does not cease from it, then know that it is penetrating, or that in its depth there is a corrupt bone or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter thirty-seven.
On cauterizing the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys due to cold or a strong wind, and the patient's sexual function is diminished because of it, then it is necessary to cauterize him on the loins, on the kidneys themselves.  Cauterize each kidney with the nail-shaped cautery mentioned previously; and perhaps you will cauterize a third time in the same place.  So three cauterizations, lined up, will be more effective.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize him with one cauterization below the navel, on the bladder where the pubic hair begins, and one cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
The cauterization on each side should be the size of a thumb joint, and cauterize one or two times in the lower back if necessary. The cautery should be nail-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter thirty-nine.
On cauterizing the womb.
If a substance forms in the womb due to cold and dampness, and because of that it prevents conception and its flow is corrupted and its courses are difficult, or pain occurs upon its arrival, then it should be cauterized with three cauterizations around the navel, as we mentioned in cauterizing the bladder. One or two cauterizations on cotton in the lower back, and the cautery should be nail-shaped.
Chapter forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Humoral substances may be attracted to the hip joint, causing it to dislocate.  The sign is that the leg appears longer than the other when comparing them, and you find an empty space at the site of the dislocation.  Therefore, the patient should be cauterized with a semi-circular cauterization on the hip joint itself.
After marking with ink around the joint, in a circular fashion, so that the joint itself falls in the center of the circle, use the cautery whose image is presented in the cauterization of the stomach. If you do not have this cautery, use three cauterizations with the large olive-shaped cautery.
Make the cauterization deep enough to reach the entire thickness of the skin, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter forty-one.
On cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint, and the cause is cold and dampness, and you have treated the patient as mentioned in the classification, and the treatment has not been effective, and this has lasted for some time, then it is necessary to evacuate the thick humors from the patient with  bitter apple or prickly pear, or something similar.
Then shave it and apply cauterization in two ways: either cauterization with burning drugs or cauterization with fire. Cauterization with fire can be done in many ways; one of them is to cauterize on the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
Make them of a suitable depth, and the distance between each cauterization should be the width of a finger. The cautery iron should be olive-shaped.  You may also cauterize one in the middle on the head of the hip itself, making four cauterizations. And if you wish, shave it in a circle as previously mentioned in cauterizing a dislocated hip, with a single cauterization.
So that the circle encompasses the entire hip and burns the entire thickness of the skin. This is the manner of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two cauterizations on the thigh at the place where the patient indicates pain, and one cauterization above the heel, four fingers towards the lateral side slightly. The cautery iron should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be only the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize where he indicates with a pointed cautery iron, three or four times, or more if necessary. If he indicates pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with one knife-shaped cauterization.
And be careful in all your cauterizations not to burn a nerve or a large artery, lest you cause the patient a bad or lasting harm.
And I have seen one and another who was cauterized above the heel, and the cauterization was excessive, so that the leg became gangrenous until the gangrene reached the foot and perforated it entirely, and the whole leg became corrupted, then diarrhea and death followed.  If the pain is in both sides, cauterize both in the same manner.
Some learned physicians have mentioned for cauterizing the hip a cautery of this description:  A cup-like instrument is made of iron, its diameter half a span, and at its mouth it should be the thickness of a date pit or slightly less, and inside that cup another cup, and a third cup.
And the distance between each cup should be the width of a thumb's joint, and the cups should be open on both sides, and their height about a joint or two joints, and a handle of firmly attached iron is made for them.  And this is its image.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, then it is placed on the hip joint, the patient leaning on the sound side, and you cauterize with three round cauterizations at once. Then you leave it for three days, and dress it with butter, and leave the wound open for several days.
Many, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The author of this book said: We have seldom used this type of cautery because of its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, and because few are found who can endure it, except that it is a good cautery for whomever endures it and its location is correctly hit.
As for cauterization with burning medications, it is that you make two cups similar to the rings in which the pulp of the pomegranate is placed, of copper or of iron.
Their rims will have a height of about two finger joints or so, and the distance between them will be the thickness of a finger, and they will be open at the bottom and open at the top, and they will be fastened to each other in this manner.
Then you lower it onto the hip joint, the patient lying on his sound side, and you support your hand firmly, then you pour between the circles some hot water, which is warmed a little by the fire, and hold it for the time of a sandglass, and the patient endures its stinging and burning, for he will feel a burning like fire.
Until the stinging subsides, then you raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the hot water from the whole hip with fresh water, then you leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn disappears, and you leave it for days so that pus flows from it, for that is more effective.
Then you treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, you make for him a mold to contain the water, according to what you made for the hip, the same. And this is the description of the hot water, and I have already described it in the chapter on the preparation of medicines.
Take of alkali salt and of unslaked lime, one part of each, crush them and place them in a new pot which has been pierced at the bottom with one small hole, just large enough for a stylus to pass through, and place under the bottom of the pot another glazed pot.
And pour over the alkali and lime enough fresh water to cover them by a finger's breadth, after you have pressed them well with your hand, and leave the pot until the hot water descends into the glazed pot below, then collect all that water and pour it onto more fresh lime and alkali.
For then it will be extremely strong and sharp, useful in many medical procedures and in cauterizing various members, because it acts exactly like fire.  Among the medicaments which also cauterize the hip are thapsia, linseed oil, and lime with soap, mixed together.
Galen, recounting the story of an ancient man, claimed a treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly praising it and claiming that no other treatment is needed, and that it cures with a single application.
To the point that someone might enter the bath carried and leave cured. This is done by taking green henbaneif green is unavailable, then take fresh dry henbanefinely grinding it with a little fat and applying it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg or thigh.
It is bound on and left for three hours, or however long the patient feels the burning cease. Then he enters the bath; when his body is damp, he enters the bath pool. Then, God willing, the pain will go away and he will be cured. If he is not cured, repeat the poultice after ten days.
Then, God willing, he will be cured. Know that no one should cauterize with these medicaments except after the body has been purged. Dioscorides mentioned that goat hair, when used to cauterize sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization is performed in this manner: wool is taken and soaked in old oil and placed on the hollow place between the thumb and the forearm, closer to the forearm. Then take dry goat hair.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes an ember, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then take another and continue doing this until sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip, and the pain ceases, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with goat hair.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the cauterization of back pain.
Pain in the back may occur from many causes, either from a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It may be from the effusion of a cold, moist substance, and cauterization is only done in this type alone.
That which is from the effusion of a cold substance, then after the patient has vomited with hellebore and the like, he should be cauterized on his back where the pain is, three rows across the width of the table itself, after marking the location with ink. In each row, five cauterizations or more, according to what you see of the patient's endurance and strength.
The cauterization should be with a pointed cautery; and if you wish, cauterize it with three or four cauterizations with a medium-sized nail cautery, in this manner.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a hump.
This ailment often affects young children.  The sign of its beginning in the child is that shortness of breath occurs upon standing and movement, and you will find at the end of the vertebrae of his back a bead that has protruded, standing out from the other beads.
If you see that and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery, in this manner. Let the cautery take hold on each side of the vertebra equally. And if you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery, in two or three rows, and let the points be close to one another.  Then treat the area until it heals, as we have mentioned.
And be careful not to use cautery in a hump that is from a spasm of the nerve.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are from cold humors that descend to any member of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, then it is customary for physicians to call that gout, especially.
If gout caused by cold is treated with the types of treatment we mentioned in the classification and the pains do not go away, then cauterization will remove them. This is done by cauterizing, after evacuation, around the joint of the legs with many cauterizations.  The cautery should be olive-shaped and medium-sized, in this manner.
And if you need to punctuate the surface of the leg, do so with a punctate cautery. If the pains ascend to the knees or to other jointsand this frequently happenscauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive-shaped cautery.
If you need more cauterization than this, cauterize, but do not deepen your hand with the cautery; rather, let it be about the thickness of the skin only. If the pains ascend to the hips or to the back, then use what we mentioned concerning cauterization in its chapter.
If the pains are only in the hands, punctuate around the forearms as if encircling two rows. If the pains remain in the fingers, punctuate each joint with a point and the metacarpus. If the pains ascend after several days to the elbows or to the shoulders,
cauterize them on each side. The patient should not lack good management and the taking of medicines; for if he improves his diet and evacuates phlegm, he will be cured with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the cauterization of fissures.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some of the intestine and fat descends to the testicle, and this is recent and beginning, then you should order the patient to abstain from food that day and to use laxatives to evacuate his bowels.
Then he should lie on his back before you, and you should tell him to restrain himself until the fat or intestine protrudes, then you should return it with your finger, then you should mark with ink below the hernia on the pubic bone a mark resembling a semicircle, its extremities towards the upper part of the body.
Then heat a cautery of this shape.
Until it becomes white and throws off sparks, then return the intestine or fat to the abdomen. Then a servant should place his hand on the place lest the intestine protrude, and you have spread the patient's legs and placed a pillow under him, and another servant should tie his legs.
And another on his chest holding his hands. Then you lower the cautery onto the same mark, and your hand with the cautery should be upright and straight, and hold it until it reaches the bone, or repeat it again if it does not reach the bone the first time.
And guard against the protrusion of the intestine while cauterizing, lest you burn it, which would cause the patient either death or a great calamity. And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed. And the cautery for children should be gentle according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then treat the cauterized area after three days with butter until the scab from the fire disappears, then treat it with other ointments until it heals. And let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound is closed.
And you should make his food during his treatment such as softens his bowels, lest the intestine protrude during straining and defecation. Then, when he wishes to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days, and let him reduce exertion and fullness from food and drink and loud shouting.
For if this treatment is used in this way, he will heal completely, God willing. And I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its chapter, God willing.  And as for the hernia which occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is incipient, if you wish it not to increase, cauterize a round mark on it according to its size. Let what you burn of the skin be about two-thirds of it, then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not increase, God willing. And some of the ancients mentioned that the hernia should be cauterized with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After you mark the hernia with ink, making the horizontal line at the upper side of the hernia and the other line downwards, and you make one nail-sized cauterization in the middle; the first cauterization is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter forty-six.
On cauterizing fistulas.
If pain occurs in some member from a fall or a blow, and that pain lasts a long time, and medication does not alleviate it, then it should be cauterized with a pointed cautery.  The cauterization of each member should be according to its size, whether small or large, and its weakness or strength.
And if the pain and fistula persist, then if it heals from the first cauterization, that is good; otherwise repeat the cauterization. For it is the habit of these pains to move from one member to what is near it; therefore you should follow them with cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter forty-seven.
On cauterizing leprosy.
As for lepers, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that comes from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize, look: if the leprosy is beginning, and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the classification, and it has not subsided nor stopped, and you fear that corruption will seize the whole temperament of the patient, then cauterize on the head five cauterizations. One in the usual middle of the head, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two on the temples, and one from behind on the hollow of the nape; and prolong your hand with the cautery a little until it affects the bone slightly.
And scales will be removed from it so that the thick vapors may easily escape from it.  And cauterize also on the spleen itself as before. But if the leprosy has continued on the patient and appeared clearly, then it is necessary to cauterize these cauterizations which we mentioned on the head. And a cauterization on the tip of the nose, and two cauterizations on the cheeks, and two cauterizations on the vertebrae of the neck, and six on the vertebrae of the back, and a large one on the coccyx at the end of the spine, and another above it in the same region, and two on the hips, one on each hip. And two on the knees, one on each knee, and two on the shoulders, and two on the elbows, and two on the breasts, and cauterize on each joint of the joints of his fingers and toes, one cauterization, and on each heel of his feet and forearms.
And beware of the nerve which is on the back of the heels lest you burn them. And perhaps a cauterization may be made on the pubic bone, and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver. And know that the more you increase the cauterization, the more useful and effective it will be.
And know that the patient does not feel pain from the cauterization as the healthy person does, because his body is numb.  And the cauteries should be of various sizes, large and small, according to the limbs and joints, as was previously described concerning the characteristics of the cauteries.
Then treat the cauterization with finely ground cress mixed with honey and other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness: when a limb becomes numb and is treated with medications, ointments, and bandages but does not heal, cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb, whether large or small. Let your cauterization be deep, penetrating slightly into the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  And it may be that some numbness affecting the hand and foot, in the vertebrae of the back at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, is cauterized, and the numbness will disappear.
And let none undertake this except one who is knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
Concerning the cauterization of vitiligo.
If vitiligo is longstanding and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it slightly, to the depth of the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil, and the leaves of verbascum, and pigeon's blood or the blood of sparrows; one part of each. Mix everything together, apply it to a cloth, and bind it to the place until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is incipient and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it grows. Some wise men have mentioned that it should be cauterized strongly in its middle; I do not see that, because I expect it to ulcerate, and I have witnessed that many times.
The correct thing is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we said, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the cauterization of hemorrhoids.
If a hemorrhoid occurs and is slow to mature, either because of the strength of the agent that causes it, or because of the age of the patient if he is old and anemic, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the hemorrhoid, cauterize around it with many small cauterizations in a punctate manner.
Then leave it, for it will hasten maturation. If you want to stop it with cauterization, take the cautery iron, the image of which is this.
And insert it into the middle of the hemorrhoid until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be from what is below, so that the pus may flow easily. Then treat it as appropriate until it heals.
Chapter fifty-two.
On cauterizing the canker.
A canker is a corruption that spreads in the member and consumes it, just as fire consumes dry wood. If you see a canker in a place that can tolerate cauterization with fire, take many small and large, nail-like cauteries, according to what is suitable for that place where the canker is.
Then cauterize it from every direction until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing of it remains at all. Then leave it for three days and apply to the cauterized areas powdered sulfur with oil until all the eschar and all the corruption is removed.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh. If after three days you see that the flesh grows healthy and without corruption, then do nothing. Otherwise repeat the cauterization on what remains of the corrupted areas.  The canker may also be treated with a sharp medicine.
For it acts in place of fire, more quickly beneficial. I have mentioned its treatment with a sharp medicine in the classification; take it from there when you need it.
Chapter fifty-three.
On the cauterization of inverted and non-inverted nails.
This condition frequently occurs on the soles of the feet; it is a rough thing that pains the person.  Cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a sharp liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, you heat a hollow cautery iron resembling an eagle's feather, made of iron, to a size that encompasses the nail on all sides. It should have a thin edge. Then you place it, hot, upon the nail, and then rotate your hand with the cautery iron around the nail until the cautery iron reaches the depth of the nail.
Leave it for three days, during which pus will form. Then dress it with pounded wild rue mixed with salt and leave the dressing on it overnight. Then it will be removed from its roots.  Then treat the wound site with a flesh-producing ointment until it heals, if the nails are not inverted.
This frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet.  You should take a tube of copper, iron, or an eagle's feather and place it over the nail or wart, then pour a small amount of sharp liquid into the tube.
Hold your hand while rotating it, gently probing with your hand so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the sharp liquid finds its way to the root of the nail. The patient should endure the stinging of the sharp liquid for a short time.
Then leave it, for the nail will be removed from its roots. Do this one by one until you have treated all of them on the body. Then treat the areas after they are removed with ointments that promote flesh growth.
Chapter fifty-four.
On cauterizing scabs.
If a scab occurs from a cold in the nerve or from a quartan fever or something else, it should be cauterized four or five times on the spine, with a cauterization between each vertebra, and one cauterization on the chest. And a cauterization on his stomach with an olive-shaped cautery iron, for the scab will subside and the cold illness will ripen quickly.
Chapter fifty-five.
On cauterizing pustules occurring on the body.
Ugly pustules may erupt on the body, resulting from cold, thick, corrupt substances. At their first appearance, each pustule should be gently cauterized with a cautery iron, the tip of which has been heated in the fire, or with a long cautery iron, or with a lenticular cautery iron.
And boils may be cauterized in this manner at their first eruption, so that their active principle does not increase, and the patient recovers from them; however, this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter fifty-six.
On cauterizing hemorrhage occurring from a severed artery.
Hemorrhage often occurs from a severed artery during a wound inflicted from outside, or when incising a tumor, or cauterizing a member, and so on, and it is difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery and press it firmly so that the blood is contained under your finger and none comes out. Then place several small and large olive-shaped cautery irons in the fire and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has burst, and apply the cautery iron to the same vein after quickly removing your finger, and hold the cautery iron until the bleeding stops.
If, when you remove your finger from the mouth of the artery, the bleeding resumes and the cautery iron is extinguished, quickly take another cautery iron from those prepared in the fire and continue to do this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve that is there, lest another affliction befall the patient. And know that if blood flows from an artery, it cannot be stopped, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cautery, as we said; or by amputation if it has not already been severed; for if it is severed, its ends contract and the bleeding stops; or it may be tightly bound with threads; or medicines that stop bleeding may be applied to it, and it may be firmly bandaged with a compress.
And as for anyone who tries to stop it with a ligature or by tightening with cloths or by applying cauterizing substances and the like, it will not be stopped at all except rarely.  If this happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not available, let him quickly place the index finger on the mouth of the wound itself as we described.
And let him press it very hard until the blood is contained and flows from above the wound and onto the artery, and the finger should not be removed from it, constantly using very cold water until the blood clots, thickens, and stops.  In the meantime, consider what may be needed in the way of cautery or medicine.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, punctures, bloodletting, wounds, and the like.
Khalaf said: In the first chapter we mentioned every disease suitable for cauterization with fire and cauterizing medicine, and its causes, reasons, instruments, types of cautery irons, and I made that into sections from the head to the foot.
And I follow in this chapter the same method, to make it easier for the student to obtain what he seeks.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more pitfalls than the first chapter on cautery.
And because of that, warning should be stronger in this, because the work in this chapter often involves the loss of blood which sustains life when opening a vein, or incising a tumor, or lancing an abscess, or treating a wound, or removing an arrow, or incising a stone, and the like, all of which involve danger and fear, and in most of them, death results.
And I advise you against engaging in what is doubtful to you, for there may come to you in this craft various kinds of people with various kinds of illnesses, some of whom are weary of their illness and death is easy for them.
Because of the severity of what he feels from his illness and the length of his affliction, and the illness is established in a way that indicates death. And some will give you their money and enrich you with it hoping for health, and his illness is fatal. So you should not accept at all those who come to you with this description.
