Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with anything of that unless after certain knowledge is established with you about what leads to a good outcome.  And use in all the treatment of your patients a foreknowledge and warning of what leads to safety.
For in that you have help in acquiring praise, glory, mention and thanks. May God inspire you, my sons, with His guidance and not deprive you of correctness and success. Indeed, that is in His hand. There is no god but He.
And I have arranged this chapter in sections, as preceded in the chapter on cauterization, from head to foot, so that what you want from it may be easier for you, God willing.
The first section.
In the treatment of the water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often affects children at birth if the midwife presses the child's head without gentleness, and it may also occur from a hidden cause that is not known.
And I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than children, and all of them whom I have seen have quickly approached death; therefore I have seen [it best] to leave working on it. And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day. Until the child could not sit up because of the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture either gathers between the skin and the bone, or it gathers under the bone on the membrane.  The procedure in this case, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, is that one should make an incision in the middle of the head, a single transverse incision, the length of the incision being about two fingerbreadths, so that the moisture flows out.  This is the image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling is larger, then make two intersecting incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the boneand its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side, and the water subsides if you press it with your hand inwards, and this is not hidden from youthen it is necessary to make three incisions in the middle of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you bind the incisions with cloths and bandages. Then you dress it from above with a drink and oil until the fifth day. Then you untie the bandage and treat the wound with lint and ointments. Do not neglect to bind the head moderately, and feed the patient with all dry food, low in moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals. Another description of the incision is that you look where the bone of the swelling and the gathering of water appear, for it may be more in the back of the head, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left.
Then you aim with the incision where the swelling and the fullness of water appear to you, so you incise it as much as you can, and be careful not to cut an artery, lest bleeding occur and the patient die from that bleeding along with the evacuation of the moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears known as the "al-assayn".
When someone suffers from acute attacks in the eyes or chest, and this persists and treatment with medications does not work, then the best treatment in that is to cut these two arteries.
Then, if you wish to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery, then tighten the patient's neck with the excess of his garment, then look where the pulse beats and their locations, the two low-lying locations behind the ears, and they rarely hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then cut them with a sharp scalpel, cutting to the bone. This should be done across the head, and if you wish, insert the scalpel from under the artery and cut upwards. The length of the cut should be about two joined fingers.
If the artery is cut, the blood will come out in a pulsating manner, leaping forward and throbbing continuously. If the artery is not felt, you should measure three fingers from the ear, then mark it with ink and incise to the bone.  What should be...
sent from the blood six ounces on average, and perhaps more or less will be sent, all according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries that appears to you. Then look at the wound; if there remains anything of the peritoneum on the bone, cut it.
Lest a hot swelling appear, then bind the wound with a thread of linen cloth, then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
In ligating the two arteries in the temples.
If a person experiences chronic migraine or acute attacks due to sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or severe chronic headache and the like, and he was treated with various medical treatments which did not work, then we have tried in these diseases ligating the arteries of the temples or cauterizing them as we have described.
And the method of ligating them is that the patient should have the hair shaved from the temples, and aim for the artery apparent in the temple, for it will be evident to you from its pulsation, and it rarely hides except in a few people.
Or, in case of intense cold, if it is hidden from you, let the patient tighten his neck with the excess of his garment, then you rub the area with a cloth or compress the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly and evidently. Then take the scalpel, whose picture is this. Then you incise the skin gently with it until you reach the artery, then you cast a hook into it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the membranes underneath it from every side; if the artery is thin, then twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut a portion from it to the extent that its ends are separated and constricted and do not cause bleeding; for if it is not severed and cut off, the blood will not flow at all.  Then evacuate from six ounces to three of blood.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong doubled thread; and let the thread be either of silk or of lute strings, lest decay quickly reach it before the wound heals, causing bleeding.
Then cut what is between the ligatures; do this at that time or after another time. And if you wish to cauterize it with a knife-like cautery to the bone until its ends are severed, this will take the place of this very action, or better, as we said.
Unless the patient has a fever or a hot temperament, for cauterization is among the things that help to consume the humors, so it is more certain in its benefit.  After removing the arteries, it is necessary that the place be packed with old cotton and firm bandages be applied; after healing, treat it with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow and with lint, until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your work, hasten to cut it off, either by cauterization, or by filling the area with vitriol and tightening your hand until the blood stops. If you do not have any of that, put your finger there until the blood clots.
And wash the area with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and bind it as necessary.  And what is easier and simpler than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after you mark the two places with ink.
And bring it down very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that what is between its ends is separated, for it will not reunite with this cauterization at all. And this is the image of the cautery.
The blades should be sharp, resembling small scalpels, except that they should be much less sharp than a scalpel, because if they are as sharp as a scalpel, they will cool more quickly and the flesh will not be severed quickly. If there is some thickness to them, the heat of the fire will be retained in them.
And it cuts the flesh quickly. This method is better than any other method, and it is easier and simpler.  Let the distance between the blades be about the width of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of the constant flow of hot tears into the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the scalp from the outside, and nothing from medical treatment with drugs has benefited, and you see the patient's face is red. And he feels a crawling sensation in his forehead like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, his eyelids are eroded, and his eyelids are clinging together from the intensity of the tears.  Therefore, the ancients in treating it resorted to this procedure, and they call it treatment with the sword. It is that you order the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions in the forehead along the length of the forehead. The length of each incision is about two fingers, one incision parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second a little distance from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third from the other side.
And be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the joining of the jaws.  Let the distance after each incision be about three closed fingers. And let you have prepared pieces of sponge or many dry cloths to wipe away the blood.
Then insert the double-edged scalpel, whose image is this. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and with it, you should separate all the skin between the incisions along with the membrane on the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the third incision. Then remove this scalpel. And also insert into the first incision another instrument called a sharp, one-sided scalpel, smooth and not sharp on the other side. And this is its image:
And place its sharp side upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone. And push it until you reach the middle incision, and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, without the cut reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do this in the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood flows, press the areas of clotted blood. Then place in each incision a cotton wick and place over it a dressing moistened with a syrup and oil, or vinegar and oil.
So that no hot swelling occurs. On the third day, remove the bandage and use plenty of washing with lukewarm water, then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and whatever you treat wounds with until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharges to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When someone experiences many severe, sharp, persistent discharges, and you see his eyes weak, small, and his vision weakened, and his eyelids ulcerated and shedding their skin. And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and consecutive sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins, and the best treatment is this treatment, which is that you order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain in the depth of the head and consecutive sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep veins, and the best treatment is this treatment, which is that you order the patient to have his forehead shaved, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or a little higher across, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, and the incision should be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the muscles of the temples which move when chewing, until when the bone is uncovered and all the blood is dried with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a linen thread, then bandage from above with compresses, and soak the compresses with syrup and oil lest a hot swelling occurs. And when you untie them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, then you should scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins in it.
Then treat it with a drying preparation that grows flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and preparing an ointment from them, and using it in the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or a semi-stone like iron and glass; or a vegetable seed like chickpea and kernel and the like; or a flowing thing like water and vinegar and the like.
Or an animal.  When a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear which does not grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun. Then if you see the stone, instill some violet oil or cerate into it, then try to remove it by movement of the head or sneezing with hellebore. And close the nostrils at the onset of sneezing after you place a ring of rags or wool around the ear and extend the ear upward. Often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out otherwise, then try to remove it with a gentle probe, whose image is this.
If it comes out with the probe, otherwise try to remove it with a fine, slightly curved, blind hook. If it does not come out with that, then make a tube of copper and insert the end of the tube into the hole of the ear gently.
And seal what is around the tube with pitch softened with oil, lest there be a way for the air other than the tube. Then draw it with your breath strongly, and often it comes out with that. If it does not come out with what we described, then take a little of the gum of Nabataea or of the prepared gum which is taken by birds, place it on the tip of a probe.
After wrapping a tightly-wound cotton around it, then insert it into the hole of the ear gently after you have dried the ear from moisture. If it does not come out by all that we described, then proceed to incision before the hot inflammation or spasm occurs.
The description of the incision is that you first bleed the patient in the cephalic vein and remove blood from him according to his strength. Then you seat the patient before you, turn his ear upward, and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its lobe in the lower part of it.
And let the incision be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then quickly stitch the incision and treat it until it heals.
And if the thing that fell into the ear is one of the seeds that grow and swell, then try to remove it with what we mentioned. If it does not respond to removal, then take a thin, delicate scalpel in this manner.
And try with it to cut that type of seed that fell into the ear. You only do this if you are certain that that seed has become moistened by the vapor of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe or with a soft swab or by suction.
As we mentioned, then it is easy to remove them. As for the water that enters the ear, the patient should first use sneezing with hellebore, having filled his ear with some old cotton while lying down on the ear in which is the water. If it comes out with that,
otherwise, take many small, smooth, flat stones along the length of the fingers, warm them slightly with fire, and the patient puts one of them into the hole of his ear and stands on one leg on that side and strikes the stone in the ear with another stone.
He continues to do this with one stone after another until all the water comes out.  The water may also come out by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather and inserting one end into the ear, and bringing the other end to the fire until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as was mentioned previously with the stone. As for removing a creature that has entered it, look: if it is small in body like a flea or similar, treat it as mentioned in the classification, but...
if its body is large and perceptible to the touch, try to remove it with forceps and hooks; this is easier than anything else that becomes lodged in the ear. As for removing worms that develop in the ear, if you treat it as mentioned in the classification in the chapter on fumigations and your treatment is not effective,
you should look into the ear in the sun; if you see anything of the worms, remove them with forceps or delicate hooks. If you do not see anything, take a tube of this shape.
narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, and insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate. Then suck strongly with it; do this repeatedly until all the worms come out. If they do not respond to this, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you with the stone.
If they do not come out by what we have mentioned, use the fumigations which I have mentioned, and which the ancients have tried in killing worms; you will find this in the chapter on fumigations.  Pouring oils and medicines into the ear is done with this instrument, and this is its image:
As you see, make it of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. If you wish, let the nozzle inside the tube be of tightly fitting copper; if you wish, take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert the reed with the cotton from above and squeeze it moderately with your hand until the oil is driven into the interior of the ear and is felt.
The patient feels it entering. And let what you pour into the ear be warmed a little in the fire, and beware that what is instilled is neither too cold nor too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of obstruction occurring in the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear openings not perforated.
Obstruction in the ear canal may also occur in some people from a wound or from  flesh growing there; and this obstruction may be deep within the ear opening, beyond sight.
It may also appear slightly outside the opening and be visible; that which appears deep within the ear and is not visible is in most cases difficult to cure, and that which is visible, the patient's ear should be placed in the sun. And look into it; if you see the obstruction apparent, open it with a delicate scalpel, which has this shape:
Its tip should be somewhat broad, limited on one side, and the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides so that it does not injure the ear; if the obstruction is from flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a delicate hook and cut it gently and thoroughly until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a smooth, slender probe, warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal; if you feel the obstruction, along with the patient's feeling of heaviness in hearing, gently remove it.
And be careful not to injure the membrane of the ear, then insert into the ear a wick the size of the opening, which you have dipped in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are sure the wound has healed, or take a wick, moisten it with water, and sprinkle powdered vitriol on it.
And use it; if you see that the ear has swollen with a hot swelling, it is necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another wick dipped in prepared quince, made with rose oil, until the hot swelling subsides, then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear, and use the other treatments that stop bleeding, mentioned in the chapter on cauterizations.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts which appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry; it is necessary to grasp them with forceps or a hook and cut them from their roots with a scalpel, then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
If it bleeds, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cautery; cauterization is better after cutting.  It often returns if anything remains of its roots; if it is cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and it does not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of the cold affecting the eyelids.
Often something resembling a cold in its intensity and hardness appears in the eyelids.
Therefore it is called a cold, and it is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids. The procedure is that you should look; if the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves to every direction, then its treatment is easy; make a transverse incision on it.
Then gently peel it from every side until it is freed, then hang it with a hook and cut it. If it is not possible for you to cut it except after you have perforated the eyelid with the cut, this does not harm the patient at all. If the incision is large, then join it with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, then there is no harm from it, for the ointment will heal and join it. If the cold is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, then turn the eyelid and hang the cold with a hook without needing to make an incision, and cut it from every side.
If it perforates the eyelid with the cut, that does not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting the cold with salt water and treat the area with something that joins it until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of the growth that appears in the eyelid.
The growth is a fleshy excrescence that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid. This mostly affects children; it weighs down their eyes, causing them to suffer from colds at night, and they are unable to look at sunlight. Because the tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under the eyebrows are moist, swollen, and the growth is clearly visible.  When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the growth between them. The procedure is to have the patient place his head in your lap.
Then you take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick, large enough to surround the growth on all sides. Then you place it on the growth and press with your fingers from all sides to gather the growth in the center of the circle.
Then you make a transverse incision in the middle of that swelling with a lancet, and the incision should not be larger than an incision made in bloodletting. As for depth, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the growth.
In most cases, the growth protrudes from the incision as a piece of white fat. You pull it out with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, rotating your hand right and left until it is removed. Beware of enlarging the incision, lest the lancet reach the eye and injure it.
If the growth does not appear in the first incision, you should gently enlarge the incision a little until the growth appears. Then pull it out as we said, then dip cloths in vinegar and water and apply them to the area and bind it with a bandage. Some people crush salt.
And they place it inside the incision to dissolve what remains of that swelling. Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs in the area, treat it with soothing compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On types of eyelid tumors.
If excessive membranes grow on the eyelid, contrary to the natural course, beneath the natural membranes and eyelashes, then they harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, whiteness, and thickening, to the point that this causes blindness.
Eye tumors are of four types: cauterization with fire, application of a strong medicine as previously described in the chapter on cauterization, excision and suturing, or treatment with reeds, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand; if it does not turn, then insert a needle threaded with a suture from beneath the eyelid, passing the needle and thread from above, near the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Turn it with a probe, then incise the inner eyelid below the excess hair with a sharp scalpel, from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus. Then remove the suture, place a small pledget of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of a laurel leaf, except that the shape should be according to the amount of eyelid elevation you desire, for this varies among people; some may require a suitable amount of eyelid to be cut off according to the degree of eyelid relaxation.
Some may need less to be cut, all this according to the relaxation of the eyelid. Then, incise with the scalpel along the two lines you marked, beginning from the larger canthus to the smaller canthus, each incision being near the natural hair, about the thickness of a probe.
Then insert the hook into one corner of the skin and remove it all. Then, using a needle and fine woolen thread, suture the edges together. Wipe away the blood and fasten the remaining threads to the eyebrows with some adhesive substance.
If you wish to do so, otherwise it does not matter.  Then leave the sutures and threads in place for about three or four days, then treat it.  If you wish, you may leave the wound without suturing and treat it with drying and astringent substances, for the eyelid will rise when the wound heals and unites; however, suturing is better.
This method of blepharoplasty was mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient; it is, however, a good procedure and without danger.  Another method of blepharoplasty is to mark a shape on the eyelid like a laurel leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then cut away the excess eyelid with small scissors, evenly, in this manner:
If you cannot hold the hooks steady, take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger canthus and a third thread near the smaller canthus.  Gather the threads evenly between your fingers, then raise them gently.
Raise all of the marked skin as described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If hot inflammation occurs when you cut or incise, treat this inflammation with qir or similar emollient ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its edges inverted; in this case, you should use what we mentioned: cutting, suturing, cauterization with fire, and application of a caustic medicine. Cauterization with fire or caustic medicine is more effective and gentler on the patient than cutting and suturing; use cutting and suturing as necessary.
Cauterization with reeds is done in this manner: invert the eyelid and make an incision on the inside as described. Then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the length of the eyelid, and their width less than the width of a scalpel.
And you should trim their ends on both sides where the threads are attached. Then gently bring them together over what remains of the eyelid and firmly tie the two reeds on both sides, and leave it for some days. The tightened skin then dies, turns black, and sloughs away.
Until it falls off by itself; if it is slow to fall off, trim it with scissors, then treat it until it heals. When it is healed the eyelid will be raised and the eyelashes will not be inverted. This is an image of the two reeds, showing how they are made in terms of this shape and size in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
On raising inverted eyelashes from the eye with a needle.
If there is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a fine needle and thread it with fine, smooth silk, then bring the ends together and tie a very small knot. The length of the thread should be about a span.
Then attach another fine thread to the loop, shorter than the first, and tie its ends if you wish. Then have the patient place his head in your lap, and let it be near the sun so that the work may be clear, for it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the loop into the root of the superfluous hair.
And quickly pass it through the eyelid until you bring it out above the natural hair. Then pull the thread up through the loop and draw the hair or hairs into the loopnot more than three.
Then pull it with your hand until the hair comes out with the loop in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull the loop and the hair does not come out with it, pull the loop downwards with the thread you attached to it until the loop comes out from the upper hole.
Then return the hair into it and pull them; do this until it rises in the upper hole and you see it clearly. Then remove the loop and the thread and take them out, and bind the eye and leave it bound for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from the natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of the sty that occurs in the upper eyelid.
That which this sty is exposed to is called a harelip, and this sty is either natural or accidental. The accidental one is from the healing of a wound or a cut or cautery and the like.
And the method of treatment is to incise that adhesion and separate its lips and put a linen wick between them and bind it until it heals, and it is not advisable to use things that dry and constrict in its treatment.
For if you do that, the sty will return more severely than it was, but use things that relax, such as fenugreek and lint with water in which althea and flaxseed have been boiled, and diachylon ointment melted with something of fats, and smear the wick with it and treat it with it.
And the key to its treatment is to try by every means not to reunite in the way it was at first, and this treatment which we have mentioned is only some improvement of the sty, not that the appearance of the place returns exactly as it was at all.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of the sty which occurs in the lower eyelid.
This sty which forms in the lower eyelid is that which is truly called a sty, and it is natural and it is accidental.  The accidental one is from a wound or incision or cauterization and the like.
And the method of working in it is that you take a needle with doubled thread and pierce it into the flesh and pass it from the left canthus to the right canthus until the thread is at both ends of the flesh, then you stretch the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is proper, otherwise you take a probe and place it on the site of the incision and turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, and the ends of the incisions are from the corners of the cut which you cut until they meet so that an angle is formed from them. Until when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape:
Which is the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp side is below what is next to the eyelid, then you gather the separated parts with two stitches, sewing it with a wool thread, then you treat it with what we mentioned of the relaxing and softening medicines until it heals.
And if the fistula appeared from an incision, suture, or cauterization, it is necessary to make a simple incision without the hair of the labia as well, as previously mentioned, then separate between the lips with a probe as we mentioned.
In short, in the treatment of fistula, whether from above or below, you should perform the procedure according to what is apparent to you of the appearance of the fistula, for it may differ greatly in form, and the skillful surgeon devises a solution in whatever way is possible for him.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the disease itself indicates the appropriate procedure and instrument in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur to many people, such that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, preventing it from easy movement and hindering its natural functions; it is necessary to insert the end of a probe under the eyelid.
And raise it upwards or stretch it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a delicate scalpel, not sharp like cutting scalpels but rather somewhat blunt, lest the patient be disturbed during the procedure and the scalpel slip, injuring the eye with its sharpness; and your cutting should be as if you are peeling off that adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed; then pour into the eye salt water to wash it, or instill into it a solution of vitriol dissolved in water, then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
And place on top of the eye wool moistened with egg white, and after the third day use the astringent eye drops until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cutting the pterygium and the fleshy excrescence of the conjunctiva.
The pterygium is of two types: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a thick white moisture which, if touched by iron or attempted to be grasped with a hook, is torn.
And a hook has not been fixed in it. Both types begin from the greater canthus until they gradually spread until they cover the pupil and obstruct light and movement of the eye. The procedure for cutting it is that the patient should place his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes and you lift the eyelid with your hand, then you grasp the pterygium with a slightly curved hook and stretch it upwards, then you take a needle and thread it with a horsehair or oxhair and strong thread, and you bend the point of the needle slightly and pierce it into the middle of the pterygium.
And you pass it through with the needle and tie the pterygium with the thread and stretch it upwards and dissect with the hair the side of the pterygium next to the pupil, as if you are spreading it with the hair to its end, then you cut the remainder at the root of the canthus with a fine scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the canthus.
So that continuous tearing does not result from its cutting.  The pterygium may be stretched with the hook alone or with the thread as we said, then the smooth-edged scalpel, whose image is this, is used to dissect.
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, lest it cause a wound and injure the pupil. Then, after cutting, put some powdered salt or vitriol into the eye and leave it until the next day, then treat it as is appropriate until it heals.
If the pterygium is not fibrous and you are unable to insert a needle into it or attach a hook to it, this type of pterygium is often treated with medication and heals as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a fine, smooth scalpel with a weak edge, like this image:. And gently scrape the pterygium from above with it. If you see that it is loosening and crumbling and the scraping has affected it, then instill into the eye some vitriol, or red vitriol, or powdered salt from your case, and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until it is all gone, unless a hot inflammation intervenes in the eye; then leave it and treat the hot inflammation until it heals, then resume the procedure of scraping until it heals. As for the fleshy excrescence of the canthus
If the excrescence causes excessive harm to the eye, then hook that excrescence with a hook and cut some of it off; do not cut too deeply, lest it cause a flow of tears. Then instill red or white vitriol into the canthus until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
Concerning the excision of warts and the removal of excess flesh that grows in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, a red, superimposed flesh may grow until it covers the pupil, or comes close to it, or overflows onto the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids may turn outward, and it resembles a rose.
The procedure is that the patient should place his head in your lap, then open his eyes, and you should grasp all that red flesh with hooks suitable for that purpose.
Or grasp it with forceps or tweezers, then cut the flesh little by little until you remove it all by cutting. Be careful of the eye so as not to injure it during the procedure, and your cutting should be either with the scalpel we described for cutting the membrane or with small scissors. Like those with which one plucks hairs, as its image will follow in the chapter which follows this chapter.
When you have finished cutting and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with powdered salt, or drop into it some collyrium or similar caustic medication, and apply externally to the eye cotton soaked in egg white to prevent inflammation.
If some of the growth remains and the bleeding overcomes you and you fear inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with what reduces inflammation, then return to the procedure until it heals.  Do the same with excessive flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any eye weak by nature or by circumstance with cautery, and do not undertake any of these operations until you see that the organ can tolerate the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
Concerning the removal of foreign bodies from the eye.
Pterygia are red vessels that grow over the eye, preventing vision and weakening the eye over time.  First, you should observe; if the eye in which the pterygia are present is strong and has no other disease besides the pterygia,
then removal is indicated. You should instruct the patient to place his head in your lap, then you should hook those vessels with one or two hooks, according to your skill. The hooks should be delicately curved in this manner:
Or they may be two double hooks in one piece, in this manner:
Then, with a delicate scissors, gently remove those vessels, wiping away the blood from time to time until you see that the vessels have been removed from the eye and the bleeding has stopped. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and perform this procedure midday, facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work so that you do not cut anything other than those vessels. When you have finished, instill green or red vitriol into the eye to cauterize any remaining pterygium. If you are unable to remove it all at that time, dress the eye with something to soothe the pain.
Leave it for a few days until the pain subsides and the inflammation ceases, then repeat the procedure in the same manner until it heals.  This is the image of the appropriate scissors.
Leave it [and let the patient's head be in the student's lap, while another wipes away tears and examines the front and back of the eye, and you] the essential thing in this operation is that you open the eye with a fixed or triangular speculum held by a student.
Sharp, and let the patient's head be in the student's lap, while another wipes away tears and examines the front and back of the eye, and you have the choice of beginning to remove it from the top or bottom of the eye, but it is preferable to remove two strands at the upper margin, one at the end of the eyebrow and the other at its beginning.
Grasp it between the middle and index fingers, then hook it from below opposite them, then grasp it between the little and ring fingers, and lift your hand gently until you see all of the pterygium elevated. Cut it from its thinnest part, if you wish, with scissors or, if you wish, with a fine scalpel.
And you introduce the thread onto a needle and you lift all of the pterygium until it is raised above the conjunctiva, and do not cut the threads, for the tears and blood will obscure your view.
Then cut with scissors what extends beyond the cornea from above and below, so that nothing remains attached except to the cornea.  If you can lift it from the cornea by pulling the thread, that is best, and this may be possible in some people.
And when you have cut it, let its place be cauterized for a few days until its pain subsides and the hot swelling is safe, then resume treatment.
Chapter Nineteen.
On returning the nose to its place.
Physicians call a deviated nose a polypus. If you treat it with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine as described above and it does not heal, there is no remedy except to incise the swelling when it is ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed.  If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, remove it with an instrument of this shape:
The rasp, which is made of Indian iron, is called the head. Its head is round like a button, finely engraved with the pattern of a file or a rasp. You place it on the corrupted part of the bone, then turn it between your fingers, applying slight pressure with your hand, until you know that the corruption has been removed.
Do this several times, then dress the wound with drying, astringent medicines. If the wound closes and flesh grows over it, and the discharge stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, suppuration, or any incident,
know that it is healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy except to return the fistula to the nasal opening in this way:  Expose the bone again with an instrument or a sharp medicine. When the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The point of the instrument is triangular, and its shaft is conical, tapering to the point, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the ethmoid and slightly away from the eye. Then rotate the probe with your hand until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
He will feel air escaping from it if he holds his hand over his nose. Then dress the wound with the drying astringent medicines as I have taught you. If flesh grows and the area hardens, then the matter that was discharging outwards will return to the nose, which will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning the protrusion of the eye.
If the eye protrudes entirely, and no damage or deficiency occurs in the vision, then you should bleed the patient, and then apply cupping to the back of the head without any condition, and perform gentle suction.
Then apply a poultice made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and  bdellium. Then bandage the eye from above with many soft pledgets.  The method for making the pledgets is to take many soft rags and fold each one into four layers, making many of them.
Then place them one on top of the other, as much as the eye needs, then bind it tightly with a firm cloth. Repeat the cupping with suction, without condition, then remove the cupping and leave the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened gently. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise repeat the application of the poultice, pledgets, and tightening, and the cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cutting the uvula.
If a hernia occurs in the uvular layer, then it protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids like a grape, and the person's appearance becomes ugly because of it, and you want to cut it, then the procedure is as I describe.
It is that you insert a needle into the base of the uvula from below upwards, then insert another needle with doubled thread from the side of the palate and pierce it, and leave the first needle as it is.
Then you cut the place where the doubled thread bends and tie some of the uvula around the needle with it and pull it gently, then you remove the needle and place moistened wool with egg white on the eye and leave it until the threads fall with the uvula, then you treat the eye with what strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  You may pull the uvula from every direction with a circle of cloth until the eye protrudes gently, then you incise with a thin scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye, so the white moisture flows and the eye descends and withers in place, then you pull it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of canker sores.
This ailment called canker is a fluid that gathers in the eye resembling flowing water, but it is not.  The procedure is that you have the patient sit on a chair upright, then you take his head in your hands from both sides.
And move it until you see the fluid descend to the lower part of the eye, then it will remain and not move, and the light will be released, and the patient will see things as he used to see them. If it does not descend to the lower part, we know that it is water. If the descent of the fluid is not achieved by what we have mentioned,
otherwise, seat the patient before you, then take a thin lancet and make a small incision with it above the cornea at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the limbus until the material comes out.
When it comes out, instill in the eye warm water in which honey has been mixed, or water in which fenugreek and honey have been boiled, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which of them is suitable for cupping, with a detailed and explained description, so take it from there correctly. Then you should seat the patient before you, sitting squarely facing the light near the sun.
And you bind his healthy eye and tie it tightly, then you lift the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the eye in which the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the cupping instrument near the cornea, with the thickness of an awl in the same white of the eye, towards the smaller canthus, then you push the cupping instrument forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel with the cupping instrument that it has reached something empty.
And it should be that the extent to which the cupping instrument goes into depth is the extent of the distance that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness, which is the cornea, for the copper will be seen in the same view of the observer as the eye, due to the clarity of the corneal membrane, then you move the cupping instrument upwards to the place where the water is.
Then press it downwards, time after time. If the water descends immediately, then the patient will see what his sight has been opened to immediately, and the cupping instrument is in his eye, then it will settle a little. If the water rises, then lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
If it settles and does not rise, then remove the cupping instrument gently while twisting it with your hand little by little. Then dissolve in the water a little pure Andarani salt and wash the eye with it from the inside. Then apply to the outside of the whole eye a compress or wet wool.
With rose oil and egg white, and you bind it with the healthy eye. As for us and the people of our time, we place on it crushed cumin with egg white. If the cupping instrument does not enter the eye due to its hardness, for some people have very hard eyes, Then you should take the scalpel called the *bard*, whose image is this:
Then pierce with it only the adherent part and do not prolong the piercing. It is only that you should make a slight opening for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as we have mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm reclining position in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room.
And he abstains from all movements and from coughing. Make his food that which softens his constitution, and he does not move his head to the right or left at all. The bandage remains in place until the third day, then you untie it in that dark room and test his sight.
Show him things, then reapply the bandage until the seventh day. It is not proper to do this during treatment or after cupping at your hour, but rather you should avoid that before the water rises quickly with intense looking.
If a hot swelling appears, then you should untie the eye before the seventh day and treat that with what calms the swelling, until when it subsides, then at that time you release his sight and place a kerchief on his face so that his sight may exercise underneath it for days while he is in that dark room, then...
He gradually leaves the house and manages its causes. And know that in cupping, the learner cannot do without observation many times, then afterwards he proceeds to work. And it has reached me from some Iraqis that he mentioned that he makes in Iraq a cupping instrument that is penetrating, with which he draws out the water.
And I have not seen anyone in our country who made that, nor have I read it in any book of the ancients. It is possible that it is a recent innovation. And this is an image of types of cupping instruments so that he who is ignorant of them may see them:
It is made especially from copper, and its tip is of this very thinness, triangular-tipped and sharp.
Chapter twenty-four.
In the treatment of the flesh that grows in the nose.
Various kinds of flesh may grow in the nose, some of which resembles a many-legged scorpion, and some of which is cancerous flesh, hardened and dark in color, and some of which is soft flesh, not dark in color.
If any of this flesh is soft, not malignant nor cancerous, then the patient should sit before you facing the sun, and you should open his nostril and insert the snare into that flesh, then pull it outwards, and then cut what you have grasped with a sharp, delicate scalpel from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone; if any remains that you were not able to cut, remove it gently with one of the delicate instruments, so that none remains.  If the bleeding overcomes you or a hot swelling appears,
treat it as is appropriate.  If it is one of the malignant tumors, act quickly to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then place vinegar and water or a potion in the nose after the cutting. If the nose opens and the moisture passes to the throat,
know that he is healed. If the moisture does not flow as it should, know that inside there is flesh growing on the upper, weakened bones which the instrument did not reach during cutting. Then it is necessary to take a linen thread of some thickness.
and tie many knots in it, making the space between each knot the width of a finger or less. The patient should then skillfully insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe or whatever is possible after he has fashioned it like a button, and pull his breath until it reaches the nasal passage.
and it emerges at his throat.  Boys often do this in school, and it is easy for anyone who wants to. Then bring together the two ends of the thread, the one that came out of the mouth and the other that remained in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to saw the flesh, doing this until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knots of the thread. Then remove the thread and, after wiping away the blood, place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment in the nose. Do this for three days or more.
until the ointment consumes all the remaining flesh. Then, finally, place a lead tube in the nose for several days until it heals. If it needs drying treatment, use that.
This is an image of the instrument with which oils and medications are dripped into the nose:
It is made of silver or copper, similar to a small, open oil lamp, and its duct is likewise.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed, and the spout of the instrument flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when you heat oil or whatever you want of juices or liquid things in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Frequently a wart grows on the tip of the nose, and it grows larger and increases with the days until its appearance becomes ugly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and to excise it completely, then apply to the place either cautery or a burning medicine which acts in place of cautery.  If you fail to cut it off until it becomes large, then look; if it is hardened, solid, dark-colored and insensitive.
Then do not apply iron to it, for it is a cancerous tumor; and I have often seen that the cutting of this tumor resulted in great harm to its owner.  But if the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and you see that its excision is possible completely, then empty the patient's bowels and cut it without hesitation or delay, and treat the place with what dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection is separated by a wound or something similar, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, the treatment rarely benefits except in some people.
Then if something like that happens to someone, see if the wound is fresh with its blood, gather the edges of the wound with sutures, then treat it until it heals. And if the separation of the connection has separated its edges and each part has become sound, then it is necessary to scrape each part to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then gather the edges with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle on them mastic and frankincense, powdered, and place on top of the sutures a plaster of walnut ointment or another of the healing ointments, and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it.
And change the medication and leave it until the threads separate themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals. The method of suturing is to gather the separated connection, either with needles as we described in suturing the abdomen, or with thread as we explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots which appear in the lips.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips; some resemble the seeds of carob, some are smaller and some larger.  It is necessary to evert the lip and incise each nodule, and to extract it with a hook.
And cut it from every side, then pack the area after cutting with powdered verdigris until the bleeding stops, then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the area with a styptic until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting away excess gum tissue.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called epulis. It should be hooked with a hook or grasped with forceps and cut at its base. Let the blood flow, then apply powdered verdigris to the area.
Or one of the astringent, drying powders. If that flesh returns after treatmentfor it often doescut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On extracting teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly crusts may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, inside and out, and between the gums.  These may blacken, yellow, or green, even causing damage to the gums and making the teeth unsightly.
You should seat the patient before you, his head in your lap, and clean the tooth or molar showing crusts and something like sand, until nothing remains.  Do the same with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and anything else, until you have cleaned it.
If the substance is removed by the first cleaning, well and good; otherwise, repeat the cleaning on another day, and a second, and a third, until you achieve your goal. Know that a molar requires many different shaped and formed instruments, depending on what is needed for your work.
Because the instrument used to clean a molar from the inside is not the same as that used to clean it from the outside, nor that used to clean between the molars, which is of another form.  These are several forms of instruments which you should have all prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat the aching tooth by every means and delay its extraction, for there is no replacement for it once it is extracted, because it is a noble substance, except when there is no alternative to its extraction.
If the patient is determined to have it extracted, you should be certain that the aching tooth is the one to be extracted. For often the patient mistakes the pain and thinks it is in a sound tooth, so he has it extracted, and then the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have seen this done repeatedly by barbers.
If you are certain about the aching tooth itself, then you should incise around the tooth with a strong scalpel, until you loosen the gum from every side. Then move it little by little with your fingers or with delicate forceps until you loosen it.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps, and hold the patient's head between your knees, having steadied it so that it does not move.  Then pull the tooth straight so that you do not break it. If it does not come out otherwise, take one of those instruments and gently insert it under it from every side, and then move it as you did before.
And if the tooth is perforated or decayed, you should fill that hole with a rag and pack it firmly with the end of a thin probe, lest it crumble when you pull on it with the forceps.  You should carefully incise around the gum on every side.
And guard against breaking it, for if some of it remains, it will bring the patient a worse affliction than his first pain. And beware of doing what the ignorant barbers do, in their haste and recklessness in extracting it without using what we have described.
For they often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or it may be extracted with part of the jawbone, as we have repeatedly witnessed. Then, after its extraction, let him rinse his mouth with a drink or with vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the siteand this often happensthen crush some alum and pack the site with it.
Otherwise, cauterize it if the alum does not help. The image of the delicate forceps with which you first move the tooth: they should have long ends, short handles, and be thick so that they do not bend when you grasp the tooth with them. And this is the image of the large forceps:
They should be thick-handled, as you see, so that when you grasp them they do not bend or give way, and short-ended.  Let them be made of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel, with sharpened ends and teeth at the tip that interlock for a firm and secure grip.
The ends may also be made in the shape of a file, making them also strong in their grip.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the extraction of the roots of molars and the removal of fractured jawbones.
If, when extracting a molar, a root remains broken, you should apply a cotton pledget soaked in butter to the area for a day or two until the area relaxes, then insert a hook or forceps. whose ends resemble the beak of a bird called a "bulrja," and this is an image of the forceps:
They should be made like a file on the inside or like a pelican; if you are unable to extract it with these forceps, you should excavate the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert an instrument resembling a small lever, which is depicted here:
short and somewhat thick at the end, and not pointed, lest it break; if the root is extracted by this means, well and good; otherwise, use these other instruments, which are depicted here: the first has a triangular end, somewhat thick.
Another delicate triangular image:
And we may also use this two-pronged instrument, which is also depicted here:
And other instruments and tools previously mentioned in the extraction of molars; and we may also use this instrument which resembles a large hook, which is depicted here:
triangular, curved at the end, somewhat thick, so that it does not break; it should not be pointed. And know that the instruments for molars are numerous, as are all other instruments, they are almost innumerable; and the skillful and clever craftsman may invent for himself instruments according to what the work and the diseases themselves indicate.
For there are diseases for which the ancients did not mention instruments, due to the difference in their types; if a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth is broken or decayed, then investigate its location with one of these instruments and hooks that were mentioned for extracting roots.
And we use a forceps, its image is this:
It should be somewhat thick to grasp the bone so that it does not escape until the bone is removed; and we treat the area with appropriate medication. If the bone has slight decay, then clean it of its decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter thirty-two.
On the extraction of molars growing on top of others.
If molars grow other than in their natural course, their appearance is unsightly, especially if this happens in women and slaves.  It should be examined; if the molar has grown behind another tooth,
and its extraction or pushing back is impossible, then extract it. And if it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, the image of which is this:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron, very sharp at the tip.  Your cutting should be done over many days because of the tooth's hardness, and so that you do not loosen other teeth. But if it is protruding and you are able to file it, file it with an Indian file, the image of which is this.
It should be entirely of Indian [steel], and its cutting edge should be very fine, like the file used to make needles. File the tooth little by little over many days gently, so as not to loosen the tooth and cause it to fall out. Then file it again and remove it with some forceps.
And if a tooth is broken, and it hurts the tongue when speaking, then it should also be filed until the roughness of that break is removed, and it is even and smooth, and does not hurt the tongue or impair speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three
On binding loose teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose and move due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on anything to eat for fear that they will fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines but the treatment has not been effective.
The remedy is to tie them with a gold or silver thread; gold is better because silver tarnishes and decays after a few days, while gold remains unchanged forever. The thread should be of moderate thickness, according to what fits between the teeth.
The method of binding is to take the thread and insert its loop between the sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach a sound tooth on the other side by weaving.
Then weave back to the side where you began and gently and skillfully tighten it so that the teeth do not move at all. Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose. Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them, and hide them between the sound and loose teeth.
So that it does not injure the tongue. Leave it tied as it is for as long as it remains. If it becomes loose or breaks, tie it again with another thread; in this way, he will enjoy it for his whole life. This is an image of the teeth and the method of binding two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see:
A single tooth or two may be returned to their place after falling out, and bound as described, and remain.  A skilled dental craftsman does this. He may also carve a piece of ox bone to make it resemble a tooth and place it in the space where the tooth is missing. And it is tied as we have said, and remains, and he enjoys it.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament which appears under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament which appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a healed wound.
And the procedure is that you open the mouth of the patient, and his head is in your lap, and you raise his tongue, then you cut that nervous ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its constraint.  If there is some rigidity and complexity, and that is from a healed wound, then insert a hook into it and split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knot is undone.
And beware that the incision is not deep in the flesh, lest you cut an artery there and bleeding results. Then, after the cutting, let him rinse with rose water or with vinegar and cold water, then place under the tongue a pledget of linen which the patient holds in place every night.
Lest it reunite. If bleeding occurs, place powdered alum on the place; and if the bleeding overcomes you, cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery suitable for that. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
In the extraction of a frog generated under the tongue.
A tumor resembling a small frog may occur under the tongue, preventing the tongue from its natural function, and perhaps it may grow large until it fills the mouth. The procedure is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and look at the tumor.
If you see it dark in color or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not interfere with it, for it is a cancer. But if it is inclined to whiteness and is moist, then insert a hook into it and incise it with a fine scalpel and free it from all sides.
If blood overwhelms you during your work, then apply crushed vitriol until the bleeding stops, then return to your work until you remove it completely, then let him rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with other appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And what grows in the throat from other tumors, glands may appear inside the throat similar to those that appear outside, called tonsils; if you treat them with what I mentioned in the section and they do not heal,
look; if the tumor is hard, dark in color, and insensitive, do not approach it with iron.  And if it is red in color and its base is thick, do not approach it with iron either, for fear of bleeding, but leave it until it matures, then either you may lance it, or it may burst of its own accord.
And if it is white in color, round, and its base is thin, this is what should be cut.  The procedure is that you should look before operating whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided or lessened somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth; and take an assistant to depress his tongue with an instrument of this shape:
Make it of silver or of copper, thin like a knife. When you depress the tongue with it and the tumor is revealed to you and you see it, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil and pull it outwards as much as possible without pulling anything of the membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument of this shape, resembling scissors except that its ends are curved, and each of its points is very sharp, made of Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:
sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or with vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or myrtle leaves or similar astringents have been boiled until the bleeding stops, then treat him until he recovers.  Other tumors besides the tonsils may grow in the throat; cut them as I described for the tonsils.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a polyp, somewhat insensitive, and nearly blocking her throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and was prevented from eating and drinking water.
She was near death if she remained a day or two.  Two branches extended from the tumor until they emerged from her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserted a hook into one, and pulled; a suitable piece was drawn out.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril.  I did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut some of it. Only a little blood flowed.
The woman's throat opened, and she immediately began to drink water and eat. We continued to cut away portions of that tumor repeatedly over a long time, the tumor replacing what was cut, until it became protracted for both of us.
Then I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside the throat with a cautery. It stopped growing, then she traveled away, and I did not know what God did with her afterward.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On cutting a tumor of the uvula.
That which is called a grape, if a catarrhal discharge descends to the uvula and swells and is white and elongated, then it is called a column; but if it is thick below and round, then it is called a grape. If it is treated with what we mentioned in the classification, and the treatment is not effective,
and I saw that the hot tumor had subsided from it and it was thin, then it should be cut, and whatever part of it is gathered together and round, and does not have length, and is bloody or the color of a clot, or black, or insensitive, then it should be avoided cutting it.
For there is danger to the patient.  Therefore, if you see it in the manner which I described, of its whiteness and length, then seat the patient next to the sun and compress his tongue with the instrument previously described, then pierce the grape with the hook.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in cutting the tonsils. And it is necessary that you cut only that which exceeds the natural state without excess, for if you cut more of it you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after cutting, use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is afraid of cutting it, then it is necessary to use a method for cauterizing it without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, and that is to place the head of the patient in your lap.
Then compress his tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take from the sharp water which I mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unquenched lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of this instrument with it, and this is its image:.
Its end, in which you place the medicine, has a concavity like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lying on his side so that the saliva flows from his mouth from inside his throat, lest anything descend from it to his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine while you squeeze it on the uvula for about half an hour until you see that it has blackened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. And if you wish, take cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, and wet the cotton in the medicine.
And insert the probe with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula, then leave it; it will wither and fall after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, reapply it.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter and wipe away the medicine around it. Then gargle with cold water and treat from the outside with lotions and from the inside with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cautery and cutting; it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take foeniculum, hyssop, thyme, rue, wormwood, chamomile, and absinthium, and similar herbs; gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
And boil [the herbs], the pot being plastered with philosopher's clay, and there should be a hole in the middle of the lid onto which a hollow instrument is fitted, made in this manner, of silver or copper:
And insert the end which contains the pomegranate into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube until the uvula is thoroughly steamed. Then repeat this several times until it withers. And beware of performing this treatment at the first onset of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling. It is only appropriate to do this when its hot swelling subsides. If you do not have this instrument, take a reed and fit an eggshell to its end, lest the patient's mouth be burned.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the vapor from burning the mouth, and this is a good treatment along with its safety.
Chapter thirty-eight.
On removing thorns and other things that lodge in the throat.
Often a bone or fishbone or something else lodges in the throat. It is necessary to remove what is visible to the eye after you have pressed down the tongue with an instrument towards the uvula.
So that you may see what is in the throat, and if it does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, then it is necessary to induce vomiting in the patient before his food is digested in his stomach, for perhaps the lodged thing will come out with the vomit, or the patient may swallow a piece of beet or a lettuce root.
Or he may swallow a mouthful of dry bread, or you may take a piece of soft sea sponge and tie it to a string, then he swallows it, and when it reaches the place of the thorn, pull the string quickly; do this repeatedly.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out. If it does not come out by what we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument of this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than an awl, and at its tip a hook. The patient inserts it gently into his throat while raising his head upwards, and he must be careful not to touch his larynx lest it cause coughing, and he pushes out the bone or thorn; or the physician inserts it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it himself, for he knows the location of the lodged object, and he pushes downwards or pulls upwards with the instrument, all according to what he can manage, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If you treat the leech with what we mentioned in the section on medicinal treatment and it is ineffective, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight after you have depressed his tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a soft, well-made forceps; but if you cannot manage it otherwise, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, then insert a heated iron into the tube.
Do this repeatedly, and let the patient abstain from water for the whole day; then let him take a jug full of cold water, open his mouth into it, and rinse his mouth with it, without swallowing a drop, moving the water around from time to time with his hand.
The leech will fall out when it feels the water; but if it does not come out as described, fumigate the throat with urine and with asafoetida, using the instrument described for fumigating the tonsils; do this repeatedly, for it will fall out.
The method of fumigation is as follows: take a pot containing hot embers, the pot being covered with a lid having a hole in the middle; then fit the end of the instrument into this hole, and place the fumigant in it; then let the patient put his mouth to the end of the tube and close his mouth so that the fumigant does not escape until he knows that the fumigant has reached his throat.
Then the leech will fall out; but if it does not fall out, repeat the fumigation; let the patient endure thirst, and eat salt and garlic, but drink no water; the leech must come out by this means.  And this is an image of an instrument by which the leech is drawn from the throat when it is seen.
It resembles hooks, as you see, except that it has this curve which enters the throat, and its two ends resemble the beak of a bird; they have the roughness of a file, so that if they grip anything, they do not let go.
And another instrument, with which the tongue is compressed; it is a wide iron ring, large enough to encompass the mouth above the tongue; it is inserted into the mouth standing on its side, so that the mouth remains open without the patient's will, and he cannot remove it at all until it is taken away.
Description of the instrument with which the mouth is opened in this very way.
Chapter Forty.
It will deal with the cauterization and incision of tumors.
Tumors are numerous and varied in kind, as will be described one by one in this book, and they differ in their cauterization and incision in two respects: one, from the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
And the second type is due to the locations in the body where they occur, for a tumor occurring in the head is not like a tumor occurring in the anus, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy location is not like a tumor occurring in a joint; each has its own ruling in treatment.
And some tumors should not be cauterized except after the pus in them has matured and is complete, and some should be cauterized while they are raw and have not fully matured, such as tumors that are near the joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it corrupts what is around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus causing the dysfunction of that limb; or the tumor may be near a principal organ.
Because if you delay its cauterization until it matures, you will harm that principal organ; or it may be near the anus, so cauterize it raw.  It is necessary to cauterize a raw tumor that is not fully mature which is near the anus, lest it ulcerate and penetrate into the anus and become a fistula.
And it becomes something that will not heal. You should know the time for cauterizing tumors that have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever disappears, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor softens, and other signs appear.
And you should apply the cauterization in the lowest part of the tumor, if possible, so that the flow of matter is easier downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  The cauterization should be lengthwise if the tumors are in the hands or feet.
And the places of the muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, and in general, in all the flat places which do not bend. But those which bend, the cauterization should follow the shape of that place. And if the tumor is in fleshy places, it is best to leave it uncauterized.
Until its maturation is complete, as we have said, for if you cauterize it before that, the discharge of pus will be prolonged, causing much harm and filth, and perhaps its lips and base will harden.  Some tumors may be cauterized across the body, if necessary, or according to what the organ requires.
And you should use a single cauterization for small tumors, and for large tumors, a wide cauterization or many incisions, according to the size of the tumor.  There may be tumors which require the skin to be incised and cut if it has become like a rag.
And it has reached the point where that which is dead is like that which occurs in many abscesses and hidden tumors. Some of them are incised with a three-sided incision, and some are cut from it in the shape of an ivy leaf, like the inguinal tumor.
