And some use a circular incision, a crescent-shaped incision, and similar incisions.  And if it does not have a head, like smooth, flat tumors, then it should only be cauterized simply. And if the tumor is large and has gathered for a long time, and you cauterize it, you should not rush to remove all the pus at that time.
Rather, remove some of it, then dress the tumor until the next day, then remove some of the pus again. Do this repeatedly gradually until you remove all of it.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person, for the vital spirit often dissolves with the sudden removal of pus, so the patient may die and you do not realize it. Therefore, be extremely cautious in this matter.
After cauterizing these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look. If the wound of the tumor is small or the incision is single and simple, then use a lint of linen or old cotton.  If the tumor is large and the incisions from cauterization are many,
then you should insert a wick into each incision until some reach each other. And if some of the skin of the tumor has been cut or thinned, then you should pack it with old cotton or linen lint without moisture, and dress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the procedures, treatments, and remedies that we have described in many places in this book and in the divisions. If you are in winter and the location of the tumor is very nervous, then you should moisten the surrounding tissues with a warm potion and oil.
And place them on the area. If you are in summer and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply the surrounding tissues soaked in water and oil or in a potion and oil, all of it cold, until the third day, as we said. Then you should untie the tumor and wipe it.
And use something similar in its treatment until it heals. This is what you need to know about the general treatment of tumors. As for the detailed method, I have described how to treat each tumor, categorized and summarized.
Chapter forty-one.
On incisions for tumors that appear in the scalp.
Small tumors appear in the scalp; they are a type of boil, contained within membranes, and have characteristics like a chicken's pouch.  Their types are numerous; some are fatty, and some contain a moisture resembling pus.
Some contain a moisture resembling whey and broth, and so on; some are hard and calcified. There is no danger in incising and removing all of them, unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument whose image comes after this, which is called the *muddass*, so that you know what it contains. If what it contains is moisture, then incise it lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:.
Begin the incision from line B to line C. When the moisture has drained, excise the sac that contained that moisture and cut it all away, leaving nothing at all, for it often recurs if any of it remains.
Then dip cotton in yellow ointment, if available; otherwise, in salt water, and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day, for it consumes what remains of the sac. Then reapply the cotton with the yellow ointment a second and third time, if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the tumor contains a fatty substance, incise it in this manner:
Make a firm incision and place hooks in the lips of the wound and peel it from every side and exert yourself to remove the membrane which contains it. If an artery obstructs you, do as I described to you before; and likewise do in the tumor if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as I mentioned. The incision on the hardened tumor is easier because it contains little blood and moisture.  Some of these tumors which are in the head in some people have no moisture at all, and that is because I incised a tumor in...
the head of an old woman, and I found the tumor like hard, rough, white stone, unable to be broken, and if someone threw it, it would cut him. And it was not like the other tumors occurring in the head, except for these, like the tumors that appear in the heads of children.
And at the roots of the ears, incise them all with a simple incision and make their bottoms from their lower parts so that the flow of the matter is easy to the lower part, then treat them with what suits them of treatment.
Chapter forty-two.
Concerning the incision on the swellings which frequently appear in the neck. These tumors frequently appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins, and they may appear in the rest of the body. That which appears in the neck may be one or many, and some of them develop from others.
And every swelling of them is within a special membrane, as is the case in the abscesses and tumors of the head as we have described. And the types of these swellings are many; some are calcified, some contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
And I have not seen one of them in good condition to the touch, and its appearance was close to the color of the skin; it moved in every direction, and it was not attached to a nerve of the neck, nor to a vein, nor to an artery, nor was it sunken. Then it should be incised with a simple incision from the top to the bottom of the body in this manner, from line J to line B.
And peel it from every side, and spread the edges of the skin with a hook or two hooks, or many hooks if necessary, as we said in the case of tumors of the head. And remove it little by little, and be careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and let the scalpel not be too sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cutting, or the patient be disturbed, and you cut what you do not need to cut.  If you cut a vein or artery and it hinders your work, place powdered verdigris or some styptics in the wound.
And bind the wound and leave it until the intensity of the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes and begins to suppurate, for the bleeding will then stop. Then return to your work until you finish. Then examine with your index finger if anything remains, then other small worms, and cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the worm, then it should not be cut from its root, but it should be tied with a strong double thread and left until it falls off by itself without harm. Then stuff the wound with old cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it.
And if the entire worm is cut, then it is necessary to bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately after you know that no residue remains. If you see that a residue of the worm's bone remains,
then you should aim to cut to its root and base and use sutures and what we have mentioned. And as for the worms that contain fluids, apply a simple poultice where their ripeness appears, and place the poultice on the lower side as we said.
Then, after the poultice, use a dressing with Egyptian ointment or the like to consume what remains of the corruption, until you see that the wound has cleansed, then treat it with ointments that regenerate flesh until it heals.
Chapter forty-three.
Concerning the incision of the throat for a tumor that occurs inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it. This is the account of their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid incision of the throat, for they will not benefit from it.
Because all the veins and the lung will be weak. But those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the windpipe, then the incision of the throat is necessary to escape the danger of suffocation.
Then, if we want to do this, we should incise the throat below three or four rings of the windpipe, a small transverse incision between two rings, to the extent of the incision in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it. If the physician is timid, he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook, then incise the skin until he reaches the windpipe, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should incise the peritoneum which we have described.  The incision of the windpipe is indicated by the phlegm which comes out of it, along with what is excised, and by the cessation of the voice. The wound is left open for a time, and when the time when suffocation was feared has passed, the edges of the wound are joined from the skin.
And its outline is separate from the cartilage. Then use the medicines which cause the growth of flesh until it heals.  The author of this book said, the explanation of all this which we have related is only if they see the patient's throat obstructed by one of these tumors which have been mentioned.
And the patient was close to death, and his breath was about to cease. They proceeded to incise the throat so that the patient might breathe somewhat through the wound and be saved from death. And they ordered that the wound be left open until the severity of the illness is over; its severity being about three days or so.
Then they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it healed. What I witnessed myself is that a maidservant took a knife and passed it across her throat, cutting some of the lung windpipe. I was called to treat her, and I found her expiring like one who is slaughtered. So I uncovered the wound.
Then I found the blood which came out of the wound was slight, and I was certain that she had not cut a vein or artery.  And the breath was coming out of the wound. So I proceeded and sutured the wound and treated it until it healed. And nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in the voice, nothing more.
And after days she returned to her best condition.  Therefore, we say that incision of the throat is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-four.
Concerning the incision of the tumor which appears in the throat from the outside and is called the elephant of the throat.
This tumor which is called the elephant of the throat is a large tumor of the color of the body.  It is frequent in women and is of two kinds.
It is either natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it; as for the accidental, it is of two kinds: one resembling fatty swellings, the other resembling the tumor which results from the complication of the artery, and its incision is dangerous. Therefore, one should not at all apply iron to it, except for that which is small; if you probe and examine it with a probe and find it resembles a fatty swelling and is not attached to any of the veins, then incise it as you incise fatty swellings. And remove it with its containing sac, if it is in a sac; otherwise, remove it all completely, then treat the place with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
On the incision of various swellings.
Swellings are numerous in types, and I have mentioned all their types in the classification. It is necessary to explain here the difference between a swelling and an abscess, since it is difficult.  I say that with an abscess there is heat, fever, and frightening pains until the boiling of the matter subsides. And the corruption is completed, then the fever and intensity subside.  A swelling, however, has neither heat, nor fever, nor pain, and it is contained in a serous sac, which is its special covering. It is the color of the body, and its beginning is like a lentil, and it becomes like a blister, larger or smaller.
And it is of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture. The types of moisture are many, as I have mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you come to treat a swelling, you should probe and examine it first with the instrument called the probe, whose image is given in the chapter after this.
Assess the size of the tumor, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If fluid of any color comes out, incise it with a simple incision as I have described for other tumors.  If no fluid comes out after the probe, know that it is fatty.
Then make a crucial incision on it in this manner as I have taught you, and hook it with hooks.  Peel the skin from every side gently, and preserve the sac if you can, so that you remove it whole with the swelling.
If the sac is ruptured during the procedure and you cannot remove it wholeand this often happensremove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part, however small or large, remains, the swelling will most likely return.
If it overcomes you and a little remains, cauterize the wound at its edges with some sharp corrosive.  Then stitch the wound and place on top of it something that soothes hot swellings, and treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the swelling is large, suture its edges and treat it with what will unite them.
If you encounter a bleeding or non-bleeding vessel and bleeding occurs, quickly cauterize the area with powdered vitriol and leave it bound for two or three days until the wound becomes necrotic and the bleeding subsides.  Then return to removing what remains of the swelling.

Chapter Forty-Seven.
On the treatment of men's breasts.
The breasts of some people may swell at the time of puberty until they resemble women's breasts, remaining swollen and unsightly.  Whoever dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this manner.
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with cauterizing medicine, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the breast inclines downwards and relaxes from its bone, as happens to women,
it should be incised on its upper sides with two crescent-shaped incisions, each connecting with the other at their ends so that the larger line surrounds the smaller one, in this manner: From line B to line C, then you should remove the skin between the incisions and remove the fat and use what we mentioned of sutures and cauterization and whatever treatment is needed until it heals.
If you do not manage to cut what you need to cut because of the patient's anxiety or because of bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in caustic ointment and leave it until it eats away the remaining fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are a type of hard, cystic swellings, some of which contain fluids.  Those containing fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped incision in this manner.
From line B to line C.  If all the fluid in the tumor is evacuated, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with a suitable ointment until it heals. If the tumor is a type of nodular swelling, incise it as we mentioned in the incision on swellings. If the wound extends and pus develops, use cautery as we previously described.
Chapter forty-nine.
On incising a tumor that appears near an artery or vein, called aneurysma.
If an artery is injured and the skin over it unites, a tumor often results from this; similarly, a vein may also develop swelling and a tumor.
The signs by which one knows if the tumor and swelling are from an artery or from a vein are: if the tumor is from an artery, it is elongated, deep-seated in the body; and if you press the tumor with your finger, you feel as if it has a creaking sound.
That which is from a vein, the tumor is round and on the surface of the body; incision of these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many places in the body where they are very large; therefore, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those that are also in the extremities or in the head should be avoided.  As for those that are a swelling of the mouth of an artery, incise it in the skin lengthwise, then open the incision with hooks, then dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath and pass it to the other side, and tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I have taught you in the ligation of the two arteries in the temples; then incise with a scalpel the area between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
Then the tumor will resolve, and you use treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off; then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a severed vein, you should hold with your hand as much of the tumor as possible along with the skin, then insert a needle below the area you held with your hand and pass it through with a doubled thread until you bring it out from the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly with it, according to what I have mentioned to you in tying the uvula from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads will slip, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire tumor at the crossing point of the first needle.
And tighten your threads in four places, then incise the tumor in its middle until what is inside comes out. Then cut away the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then apply a dressing that you have soaked in a drink and oil. Then use treatment with cautery and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the tumor that occurs from the twisting of a nerve.
Just as a tumor appears in the artery and vein, so too does a tumor appear in the nerve if a blow occurs to it or it occurs from excessive exertion or the like.  This is most common in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a chronic tumor resembling in color the rest of the body. In most cases it is painless, and if pressed hard, the patient feels a kind of numbness. The tumor is not deep within the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but not forward or backward.  As for those in the joints, they should not be treated with iron, for it may cause gangrene. As for those in the head or forehead
Then incise the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its root. If it is large, hook it with a hook and peel it off, then remove it and close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter fifty-one.
Concerning the excision of warts that appear on the abdomen.
Many people develop warts on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies.  These are called fungal warts because of their resemblance to mushrooms. Their root is thin and their head thick; their lips may become inverted, and some are small while others become very large.
I once saw a man who had two warts on his abdomen that resembled mushrooms. They were thin and white at the root, their lips inverted and cracked, and moisture constantly flowed from them. I excised them; one weighed about eighteen ounces and the other about six.
In excising them, observe that if the patient is moist and the wart is white, moist, and thin-rooted, cut it with a broad scalpel. Keep cauteries at hand in the fire, for often much blood flows when they are cut.
If the bleeding is excessive, immediately cauterize it. If you find the patient cowardly and afraid of being cut with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart of this type. Leave it for two days, then increase the tightness of the lead, continuing to tighten it.
Keep tightening it until it affects the wart so that it separates and falls off by itself without any effort. If the wart has a thick root, there is some danger and uncertainty in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; however, it may be possible to cut half of it or part of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, and then treat it until the wound heals. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and unsightly, for it is a cancerous tumor; we will discuss cancer later.
Chapter Fifty-Two
In the treatment of umbilical hernias.
Umbilical hernias result from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum of the abdomen, from which fat or intestine protrudes, as happens in other hernias; or from blood emitted from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from wind that is impacted there.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of fat, then the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft, painless, and will appear in various positions. If it is due to the protrusion of intestine, its position will be, as we have described, more variable. If you press it with your fingers, it disappears and then returns; sometimes there is gurgling, and it becomes much larger upon entering a bath or with severe exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when pressed with your hand, nor does it increase or decrease. If it is due to blood, then along with these signs the tumor will appear dark. If it is due to newly grown flesh, the tumor will be firm, hard, and remain the same size.
If it is due to wind, it will be soft to the touch. The treatment is as follows: if the umbilical hernia is due to blood from an artery or vein, or to wind, you should refrain from treating it, for there is danger and uncertainty, as I have explained in the chapter where I discussed tumors that arise from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical hernia is due to intestine or fat, you should instruct the patient to hold his breath and stand upright, then mark with ink around the entire umbilicus. Then instruct him to lie on his back before you. Then incise with a wide scalpel around the umbilicus at the place you marked with ink. Then pull the middle of the tumor upwards with a large forceps, and then tie the incised area with a strong thread or silk tendon, tightly.
The knot of the ligature should be a slip-knot. Then open the middle of the tightened tumor above the ligature and insert your index finger, seeking the intestine. If you find that the ligature has grasped it, loosen the knot and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find fat, pull it out with forceps.
And cut away its excess. If an artery or vein obstructs you, ligate it carefully and return to your work. Take two needles and thread them with strong threads, and insert the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, crisscrossing them, then tighten the tumor at four points over the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the tightened flesh decays and falls off by itself, or cut it off when it softens and decays. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical hernia consists of flesh that has grown there,
or of moisture, you should constrict the tumor as we said, and remove the blood or moisture that you find within, then treat it with whatever closes the wound.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
In the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medication, and warned against treating it with iron lest it ulcerate; and we have mentioned cancer that develops in the uterus and warned against treating it.
And the ancients mentioned that whenever the cancer is in a place that can be completely excised, like cancer that is in the breast or in the thigh and similar accessible members, its removal in its entirety is possible, especially if it is small and beginning; but if it is old and large, then it should not be touched.
For I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve that.  The procedure, if it is accessible as we said, is to first purge the patient of black bile several times, then to bleed him if there is evident fullness in the veins.
Then you position the patient in a position that allows for the procedure, then you apply hooks suitable for the cancer, then you scrape it from every side along with the skin completely, until nothing of its roots remains; and let the blood flow and do not stop it quickly.
Rather, squeeze the areas and remove all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you can; and if during the procedure you encounter great bleeding from a severed artery or vein, then tie off the vessel until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter fifty-four.
On the treatment of elephantiasis.
We have already explained in the classification the types of dropsy, how the accumulation of water occurs, and the signs of each type and its treatment with medication. That which is treated with cautery is only the ascitic type, and the tympanitic type and the fleshy type are not at all approached with cautery.
For that is fatal, if you have treated this type of ascitic elephantiasis with medication and your treatment has not been effective, then see if the patient is extremely weak or has another illness besides elephantiasis, such as...
cough or diarrhea or something similar, then beware of treating him with cautery, for that is dangerous.  If you see that the patient is strong, has no illness other than elephantiasis alone, and is neither a child nor an old man, then the method of treatment is to have the patient stand before you. and an attendant behind him squeezing his abdomen with his hands and pushing the water downwards towards the pubic area, then take a lancet like this:
pointed at both ends, long, with a defined tip like a lancet, except that it has a slight curve so that it does not penetrate the intestines during the procedure and injure them. Then look, if the elephantiasis originated from the region of the intestines...
then it is necessary that you make the incision three fingers' breadth below the navel, directly above the pubic area. If the elephantiasis originated from liver disease, let your incision be three fingers' breadth to the left of the navel; if it originated from spleen disease, let the incision be on the right side by three fingers' breadth.
Also, the incision should not be on the side on which the patient intends to lie down, lest the fluids flow to that weak place. Then pierce the skin completely with the instrument, then insert the instrument into that incision and raise your hand.
with the lancet between the skin and the peritoneum, as if you are stripping it off, and the amount you strip off should be the size of a fingernail or thereabouts. Then pierce the peritoneum until the lancet reaches an empty space, which is the location of the water, then withdraw it and insert into the opening the instrument which has this form.
It is like a small tube made of thin cane, silver, copper, or smooth, polished lead. It has a small hole at its lower end and three holes in its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And its sharpened end may be in the shape of a pen nib, like this image:.
At its upper end is a ring; when the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately flow down the instrument, and a moderate amount of water will be evacuated at once. For if you evacuate more than is necessary at once, the patient may die from the dissolution of his vital spirit.
Or he may experience a faintness approaching death. But evacuate according to his strength and what the patient's condition indicatesthe strength of his pulse and the good color of his skinthen remove the instrument and the water will be retained, for it will be retained immediately.
Because of the skin that holds the hole in the peritoneum, which I have instructed you to stitch in that manner. Then you can reinsert the instrument another day if you see the patient able to tolerate it.
And you will also remove a small amount of water; do this for several days while carefully watching and guarding against error, until only a little water remains.  If you fear for the patient and leave a large amount of water, treat him by burying him in hot sand.
And profuse sweating in the bath and sun, and enduring thirst, and treating him with drying medications until he recovers.  And cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water as I described before.
Chapter Fifty-Five
In the treatment of children who are born with their urinary passages unperforated, or with a narrow or misplaced opening.
Some boys may be born with their urethra imperforate; you should immediately perforate it upon birth.
With a very fine scalpel, in this manner:
Then place a thin lead nail in the opening, bind it, and leave it for three or four days. When he wants to urinate, remove it, and then replace it afterwards. If you do not use lead, it is possible that the urine flowing over the area will not allow it to close.
As for him whose opening is narrow, treat it with lead as we have said for many days until it widens.  Those whose opening is misplacedsome are born with the opening at the end of the penis and are unable to urinate forwards.
He must lift his urethra upwards by hand;  this is because the semen cannot reach the uterus in a straight line, and this is a very bad condition. The way to treat this is for the patient to lie on his back.
Then stretch his penis with your left hand with a strong pull, and incise the tip of the urethra with a sharp blade or scalpel, like a pen nib, or as if you are carving something so that its middle protrudes, resembling a penis, and the opening is in the middle as it should be.
And be careful during your work not to cause bleeding, for this often happens; so stop the bleeding and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter fifty-six.
On the ulcer that appears on the foreskin and penis, and the blackness, corruption, and adhesion of the foreskin to the penis.
This ulcer often appears in the urethra; it is a rough fleshy growth, and there is benign and malignant.  The benign should be tied off with a fine ligature.
And cut it until you remove it all, then apply a cotton swab soaked in Egyptian ointment, then treat it afterwards with palm ointment until it heals. But if the ulcer is malignant and dark in color, then you should cauterize it after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised man, and part of the ulcer is inside the foreskin and part outside, then you should remove the ulcer that is inside first, until it heals, then treat it from the outside.
Because if you treat them both together, you do not prevent the foreskin from being perforated.  It may also appear in women, and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption; then you should excise everything that has blackened and threatens to corrupt or has already corrupted.
Then apply honey mixed with crushed and sifted pomegranate peel and acacia, then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If bleeding occurs, use cauterization with a crescent-shaped cautery iron in this manner:
For cauterization is beneficial for both conditions, I mean bleeding and the wound if it becomes corrupted. If the cautery eats away and disappears completely at some time, then you should insert a lead tube into the urinary canal so that the patient urinates through it. As for the adhesion of the foreskin to the cautery...
This adhesion only happens in those whose foreskin is healthy and who have not been circumcised.  It may also occur due to a wound or swelling. Then you should separate it with a blunt scalpel until the bond is loosened and the cautery is freed from every side.
If it is difficult to distinguish them completely, then you should remove some of the cautery and some of the foreskin.  For the foreskin is thin, and it may easily perforate due to its thinness.  Then separate the foreskin from the cautery with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water, lest it adhere again.
Then treat with an astringent drink until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
On the cleansing of boys and the treatment of what may befall them of harm.
Circumcision is nothing other than a separation of a connection like other wounds, except that since it is done by our will, and its use is especially in boys, it is necessary for us to describe the best procedure in it.
And the easiest path leading to safety.  I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because it was not used in their laws, but rather it is something we have acquired.
by experience.  And that is that I found the majority of surgeons and barbers using cleansing with the scalpel and with scissors, and they use the cautery and ligature with thread and cutting with the nail.  I have tried all these methods.
So I found nothing better than cleansing with scissors and ligature with thread, because cleansing with the scalpel often adheres to the skin, because the skin of the foreskin is two layers, so perhaps the upper layer of skin is cut and the lower layer remains.
Thus he is forced to cut again and renewed pain, and cauterization does not prevent cutting the end of the urethra, because it might enter its opening. As for cleansing with the nail, the skin might slip and your work is spoiled, or the boy's skin might be naturally short
For they are often born thus and do not need cleansing.  And I have seen this. As for cleansing with scissors and ligature with thread, experience has revealed its excellence to me, because the cutting of the scissors is proportionate, since the blade which is above is like the blade which is below.
So when you squeeze your hand with the proportion of the two blades, you cut to one measure and in one time, so the thread becomes like a wall for the skin of the urethra from all sides, with no mistake at all. The method of operation first is that you reassure the boy.
Especially if he is one who understands a little that you are only tying the thread in his urethra and leaving him until another day, then rejoice him and please him in every way you can and that he accepts with his mind, then stand him erect between your hands.
And let him not be sitting, and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the boy's eye does not fall upon it at all, nor upon anything of the instruments. Then you insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin and lift it upwards until the glans penis emerges.
Then you cleanse it from what gathers in it of filth, then tie the marked place with a doubled thread, then tie a second ligature a little below it, then grasp with your thumb and index finger the place of the lower ligature firmly and cut between the two ligatures. Then raise the skin upwards quickly and bring out the glans penis, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or finely powdered alkanet, for it is also excellent.
Then raise the skin upwards quickly and bring out the glans penis, then let the blood flow a little, for it is better and lessens the swelling of the urethra, then wipe it with a damp cloth, then sprinkle on it some ash of burnt dry gourd, for it is the best I have tried, or finely powdered alkanet, for it is also excellent. Then apply a dressing from above in a piece of fine linen, a cooked egg white beaten with fresh, sweet rose oil, and leave it on until the next day, then treat it with the other treatments until it heals.
And this is the image of the scissors which are suitable for circumcision:
They should be sharp, cutting, without curve, well-riveted, and the length of the two blades should be equal to the length of the handles. As for the mistake that occurs in circumcision, sometimes the inner skin is completely or partially inverted during cutting; it should be extended immediately with your fingernail before the place swells.
And cut it evenly; if you are unable to hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and what is under the glans penis remains swollen and inflamed, leave it until the acute inflammation subsides and then remove it gently.
And cut it as seems best to you, and be careful of the glans penis, for if something of the glans penis is cut, it does no harm; treat it with that which heals wounds from the plasters which we described in the chapter on plasters.
And if more of the skin than necessary is cut and it retracts upwards, that also does little harm; treat it as mentioned until it heals.
Chapter fifty-eight.
On the treatment of urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder is due to its obstruction by a stone, or clotted blood, or pus, or a growth, and the like.  If you treat that with what we have mentioned of the types of treatment described in the classification.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is due to a stone that has lodged in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that the patient sit on his knees, then one person mounts his back, extending his belly against his back, and the patient holds himself as much as he can.
Then the neck of the bladder will be forced to push the stone backwards, and the urine will flow. If it does not flow by what we have mentioned, and the matter becomes severe for the patient, then it is necessary that you use its removal with the instrument called a catheter, whose image is this:
It is made of silver, and it is thin, smooth, hollow like a bird's feather in thinness, long about a span and a half, having a gentle funnel at its head. The way to draw off the urine with it is to take a doubled thread and tie to its end wool or cotton firmly.
And you insert the end of the thread into the bottom of the catheter and cut with scissors if any of the wool remains so that it enters the tube and closes it like a button. Then you lubricate the catheter with oil, or butter, or egg white, and you seat the patient on a chair.
And you clean his bladder and his urethra with moist ointments or oil and lukewarm water. Then you insert the catheter into the urethra gently until it reaches the root of the urethra, then you bend the urethra upwards towards the navel, then you push the catheter inside it.
Until, when the urethra is fully bent, then you push the catheter inside until, when it reaches near the rectum, you incline the penis downwards with the catheter inside it, then you push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it has reached something hollow.
And it is made in this manner because the passage through which the urine passes is crooked. Then you pull the thread with the wool gently, for the urine follows the wool, then you remove it and the urine flows out, and you return the catheter.
You continue to do that until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
And the images of the instruments suitable for this are made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as pus, and you want to instill water and medicines into it, and this is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image:
It is made of silver or hollow ivory, having a long tube as fine as a hair, entirely hollow except for the tip, which is solid and has three holes, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
And the hollow place in which the propellant is, is only as much as will fill it without excess, so that when you draw something of the fluids with it, it is drawn, and when you push with it, it is pushed further, as is done by the squirt that throws oil in sea battles.
Then if you wish to put fluids into the bladder, you insert the tip of the syringe into the fluid and draw upwards with the plunger, then the fluid will be drawn into the body of the syringe, then you insert its tip into the urethra as we described in the catheters, then you push the fluid with the plunger.
Then that fluid will reach the bladder accordingly until the patient feels it, and this is the image of a fine clyster also with which the bladder is injected:
It is made of silver or lead, its upper end resembling a small funnel, and below it a groove in which the ligature is placed; then you take a camel's bladder and put in it the fluid which you wish to inject into the bladder, then tie it firmly between the grooves with a doubled thread.
And warm that fluid a little over the fire, then insert the tip of the clyster into the urethra, then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the fluid firmly until the patient feels that fluid has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it and pierce it all around, then insert a strong thread into those holes as you go around, then gather the circle as you gather a tablecloth after you have put in it what you want of fluids, ointments, and water.
Then tie it to the instrument and do with it as you did with the bladder, squeezing your hand until the fluid reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
In the extraction of the stone.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and mentioned the difference between the stone generated in the kidneys and the stone generated in the bladder, and the signs of all that, and mentioned the stone in which work with the hand and incision is done.
This is the stone generated specifically in the bladder and that which becomes lodged in the urethra, and I am describing that with clear explanation and brevity.
I say that the stone generated in the bladder most often affects boys, and among its signs is that urine exits the bladder similar to water in its thinness.
And sand appears in it, and he scratches his penis and plays with it, and often it hangs down, then spreads, and the anus protrudes with it in many of them.  And it is easy to cure boys of it until they reach fourteen years of age, and it is difficult in old men; as for young men, it is moderate between that.
And the one whose stone is larger, his treatment is easier, and the smaller one is the opposite. So if we come to treatment, it is necessary first that the patient be injected with an enema that removes all the stool in his intestines, for it may prevent the finding of the stone upon examination.
Then the patient is taken by his legs, and he is shaken and jolted downwards so that the stone descends to the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place repeatedly. Then you seat him between your hands upright, with his hands under his thighs, so that the entire bladder is inclined downwards, then you examine him.
And palpate him from the outside. If you feel the stone in the space, then immediately begin to incise upon it.  If it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary to lubricate the index finger with oil from the left hand if the patient is a boy, or the middle finger.
If he is a fully grown boy, then insert it into his anus and search for the stone until, when it falls under your finger, you move it little by little to the neck of the bladder, then press upon it with your finger and push it outwards towards the place which you want to incise.
And you order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and you order another servant to stretch with his right hand the labia upwards, and with his other hand to stretch the skin under the labia away from the place where the incision will be. Then take the sharp scalpel, whose image is this:
And incise between the anus and the labia, not in the middle, but towards the left buttock. The incision should be upon the stone itself, with your finger in the anus, and you press it outwards obliquely, so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, to the extent that the stone can exit.
Not larger, for perhaps the finger which is in the anus will press the stone at the incision, and it will come out without difficulty. And know that there may be stones which have angles and edges, and therefore their exit is difficult.
And some are smooth, resembling an acorn and round, so their exit is easy.  But if any have angles and edges, then increase the incision slightly. If it does not come out like this, then it is necessary to devise a means, either to grasp it with a firm forceps, the end of which is like a file.
To hold the stone firmly so that it does not escape from it, or to insert under it a delicate instrument with a hooked end. If you cannot manage it, widen the incision slightly. If excessive bleeding overcomes you, stanch it with alum.
If there are more than one, push the largest first towards the opening of the bladder, then incise upon it, then push the smaller ones afterwards.  Do likewise if there are more than two. If it is very large, it is foolish to make a large incision upon it, because two things will happen to the patient.
Either he will die, or he will have a constant dribbling of urine, because the place will not heal at all. But try to push it until it comes out, or devise a means to break it with forceps until you remove it in pieces.
When you have finished your work, cauterize the wound with costus, aloes, and myrrh, and bind it, and place over it cloths moistened with oil and wine, or with rose oil and cold water, to calm the hot inflammation. Then let the patient lie on his back, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
When it is loosened, wash the place with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with palm-tree ointment and basil ointment until it heals. If excessive hot inflammation, suppuration, or the like appears in the wound, such as blood clotting in the bladder and preventing urinationwhich is known from the appearance of blood with the urine
insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood; for if it remains, it will lead to corruption and decay of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of condition with its appropriate treatment until it heals.
In all the treatments, it is necessary to bind and bring together the thighs to secure the medicines placed on the wound. If the stone is small and has settled in the urethra and obstructed the flow of urine, treat it with what I have described before it necessitates incision.
I have often dispensed with incision by means of this treatment, which I have tested. It is to take a probe of steel, of this form:
The end is triangular, sharp, and fixed in a rod. Then take a thread and tie it around the penis below the stone, lest it return to the bladder. Then gently insert the probe into the urethra until the probe reaches the stone itself.
And turn the probe in the stone little by little, intending to pierce it until you pass through it to the other side; then the urine will flow immediately. Then press with your hand on what remains of the stone outside the penis, for it will crumble and come out with the urine, and the patient will be healed.
But if this treatment is not available to you because of some impediment, then tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone, then incise upon the stone in the penis itself between the two ties, and then remove it.
Then untie the ligature and clean away the clotted blood which has formed in the wound.  The reason for tying the thread below the stone is to prevent it from returning to the bladder; the other ligature is above so that, when the thread is untied after the removal of the stone, the skin may return to its place and cover the wound.
Therefore, when you tie the upper thread, you should draw the skin upwards so that it will return and cover the wound when you are finished, as we have said.
Chapter sixty-one.
On the removal of stones from women.
Stones are rarely formed in women; but if a woman should have a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One is that the woman may be a virgin; another is that you will hardly find a woman who will allow herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or one of the women of the family, and thirdly, you will not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially in manual work; and fourthly, the incision for the stone in women is far from the location of the stone, therefore requiring a deep incision.
And in that there is danger. If necessity demands it, then a skilled woman physician should be sought; but such are rarely found. If you cannot find one, seek a chaste and gentle physician, or procure a skilled midwife experienced in womens matters, or a woman who has some knowledge in this craft.
Then have her present and instruct her to do everything you command, beginning with the examination for the stone. That is to say, observe whether the woman is a virgin. If so, you should insert a finger into her rectum and search for the stone. If you find it and press on it with your finger...
then instruct her to make an incision. If she is not a virgin but a widow, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing firmly.
If she finds it, she should trace it from the mouth of the bladder downwards as far as she can reach, until she reaches the groin. Then, she should make an incision opposite the middle of the vulva, at the groin, on whichever side the stone is felt.
Her finger should not leave the stone, pressing on it. Let the incision be small at first; then insert the probe into that small incision. If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as is needed for the stone to pass through. And know that there are many kinds of stones.
Some are small, some large; some smooth, some rough; some long, some round; some branched.  Know their types so that you may thereby understand what you are doing. If you are overwhelmed by blood, sprinkle powdered verdigris on the site and leave it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone comes out.  And make sure you have with you the instruments which I mentioned for removing stones from men, to assist you in your work. If you are overwhelmed by bleeding and you know from the throbbing of the blood that it is from a severed artery, then apply styptics to the site.
And bind it tightly with bandages, and leave it; do not disturb it, and leave the stone; do not remove it, for the patient might die. Then treat the wound. When the severity of the bleeding has subsided after a few days and the site has suppurated, return to your work until you remove the stone.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On incision of the water bladder.
The water bladder is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum which is under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle, and it is called the scrotum.  It may be in a special membrane prepared for it by nature in a part of the testicle.
So that one might think it is another testicle. It is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum which we mentioned. This only rarely occurs.  This bladder is generated from weakness affecting the testicles, so this material settles there. It may also result from a blow to the testicles.
This moisture is of many colors: it may be yellowish, or bloody red, or black and purulent, or white and watery  and this is the most common. The signs by which it is recognized are:
Where the water collects, if it is in the white peritoneum that we mentioned, the swelling is round, slightly elongated, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane, then the swelling is round on one side of the testicle.
Therefore, one imagines that it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the skin of the scrotum and the white peritoneum, then it is palpable. But if you wish to know the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe, the image of which we have already presented.  Whatever comes out in the wake of the probe, you judge it according to what is inside.
If we proceed to treatment with the cautery, then you should order the patient to be bled if he is able and you see that his body is full. Then he should lie on his back on something slightly raised, with many cloths placed beneath him. Then you sit on his left.
And you order a servant to sit on his right, stretching his penis to one side of the scrotum and toward the groin. Then you take a wide scalpel and incise the skin of the scrotum in the middle lengthwise, almost to the pubis.
Make the incision straight, parallel to the line that divides the scrotum in two, until you reach the white peritoneum which contains [the testicle]; then peel it back, being careful not to tear it.  Your peeling should be done more from the side which adheres to the testicle, and you should extend the peeling as much as you can.
Then widely incise the peritoneum full of water and remove all the water. Then separate the lips of the incision with hooks and stretch the peritoneum upwards, without touching the containing skin of the scrotum, and cut the peritoneum however you can cut it, either all at once or piece by piece.
Especially its thin side, for if you do not completely cut it, you cannot be sure that the water will not return. If the testicle protrudes from its skin during your work, then, when you have finished cutting the peritoneum, return it [to its place], then join the lips of the scrotum's skin with sutures.
Then treat it like any other wound until it heals. If you find the testicle is already spoiled by another disease, then you should ligate the vessels in the spermatic cord for fear of bleeding, then cut the testicle from the spermatic cord and remove the testicle, then treat it as we have described.
If the collected water is in both sides, know that they are two hydroceles; incise the other side as you did the first, and if it is possible for you to do the work in one [operation], do so; then wipe away the blood.
And insert into the incisions wool that you have soaked in oil or rose ointment, and place on the outside other wool soaked in a medicament and oil, and spread it over the testicles and the flanks of the abdomen, and place folded rags over it, which are the pledgets. And tie them from above with a bandage which has six ends, in this manner:
Then two rags are taken, sewn together, and stuffed with fluffy wool in this manner, and the ends used to bind it are made of wet cotton or wool, and gathered tightly in the waistband of the trousers, and one of the ends is taken to the buttocks towards the back.
And the second is taken towards the pubis, and the two ends to the thighs from below, and the remaining two ends to the groins, and all the tightening is gathered in the waistband of the trousers.  This hydrocele can also be treated with cautery instead of incision with an instrument.
It is that you take a gentle, knife-like cautery and with it incise the skin of the scrotum while it is hot, as we described, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed. Take another cautery of this shape:
It resembles the Greek eye, then cauterize that peritoneum with it while it is hot until all the moisture comes out. Then stretch the peritoneum with hooks and peel off the containing layer with the cautery, cutting it as much as you can until you excise it all, and take care that the testicle is not touched by the fire.
Know that this method is safer and less prone to bleeding, and better than the method of incision. If you encounter a hot swelling or bleeding or something else during the incision or cauterization, then you should treat whatever you encounter with the appropriate treatment, as previously mentioned.
If the patient is cowardly and does not endure this operation because of its unpleasantness, then let him use cautery as I describe, if it is confirmed by the signs we have previously mentioned.
If the hydrocele is watery, then the patient should sit on a raised chair, then cauterize the lower part of the swelling with a wide scalpel, and apply the cautery along the length of the body. Make the opening wide so that all the water flows out, then place cotton on the area and bind it.
Leave it to drain the remaining water, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not come out during cauterization, and this is because the tunica albuginea obstructs the mouth of the wound and prevents the exit of water,
then you should insert a goose feather into the wound, or the instrument used to extract the water from the hydrocele, the image of which has been shown previously, or slightly enlarge the opening of the wound.
Know that the water may return and collect after six months or so. If it collects, cauterize it in the same manner, and the patient should take care of himself during those days.
Chapter sixty-three.
Concerning the incision on the fleshy membrane and its treatment.
Know that an incision on this membrane is one of the dangerous things leading to death in most cases; therefore, we see that it should be left alone and kept safe. I will, however, mention the procedure for treating it and the types of swelling.
I say that many swellings occur in the body, from which the two testicles are formed. This is due to many causes, either from a sharp humor flowing to the testicles, or from a blow. The color of the swelling is the color of the body, and it does not contain water.
The swelling may be soft, or it may be hardened, its color dark, and without sensation.  It may be from the complication and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of the veins.  That which is from the swelling of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the swelling.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be exposed at all. As for that which is from the swelling of the veins, nothing disperses from it when pressed with the fingers. This type may be incised, as well as other flesh.
And it is that you should incise the skin of the testicle, then extend the testicle upwards and bring it out from the white peritoneum, and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and ligate the vessels and cut the spermatic cord after freeing it from every side of the testicle; if the testicle has adhered to those growing tissues.
Then it is necessary to remove and cut the testicle. And if the adhesion is between some of the peritonea or between the vessels, it is necessary to free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular incision.
If the growth of flesh is in the place of adhesion which is from behind, it is necessary to cut all of it and remove the testicle as we said. When your work is completed, dress the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and syrup, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Four.
In the treatment of hydrocele with a hernia.
A hernia is a twisted tumor, somewhat twisted, similar to a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the testicles and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking.  The treatment of this condition is similar to what has been mentioned previously.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as the ancients did. I say that it is necessary that the patient sit on a high chair, then push the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards, then grasp the skin of the scrotum with your fingers along with the vessels which are close to the penis.
And let your assistant hold it and stretch it tightly, then incise with a broad, sharp scalpel an oblique incision along the vessels until the vessels are exposed, then dissect from each side as I described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the armpits, then insert a needle with a doubled thread.
And tie it at the first place where the hernia appeared and tie it also at its end, then incise it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body and remove what has gathered in it of foul, putrid fluids.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds that you want to heal so that the vessels that nourish one of the testicles fall off; there is no harm in that.  If the hernia affects all the vessels, it is necessary to remove one of the testicles along with the vessels, lest the testicle lack nourishment. before cutting the vessels, lest they wither and become useless.
Chapter sixty-five.
In the treatment of intestinal hernia.
This hernia occurs from a tear that appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen near the groins from the abdominal cavity, and the intestine descends from that rupture into one of the testicles.
This rupture is either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its extension, and these two types occur from many causes, either a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting something heavy, and so on.  Its sign, if it is from the extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs little by little over a long time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling is even towards the depth because the peritoneum constricts the intestine. Its sign, if it is from a tear in the peritoneum, is that it causes great pain from the beginning suddenly, and the swelling is uneven, apparent under the skin nearby.
That is because the intestine exits and goes outside the peritoneum.  The omentum may also exit with the intestine; this hernia is then called intestinal and omental. It may also be with gas.  Feces may flow into the intestine and become trapped there, resulting in the patient's death.
Because it causes severe pain and gurgling, especially if constricted. Treatment of these types of ailments with cautery is dangerous; one should be wary of resorting to it.  The procedure is to instruct the patient to return the intestine into his abdomen by hand if it is possible to return it.
Then he lies on his back in your hands and raises his legs. Then you stretch the skin next to the groin upwards and incise all the skin of the scrotum lengthwise. Then you insert hooks into the edges of the incision, as much as is needed to open the incision with them.
The incision should be large enough to allow the testicle to emerge. Then you dissect the membranes under the skin of the scrotum until the white, hard peritoneum is exposed on all sides. Then insert your index finger next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the skin of the testicle and the second peritoneum, and you release the adhesions behind the testicle. Then, with your right hand, you pull inwards on the skin of the scrotum, while with your left hand you stretch the white peritoneum upwards and lift the testicle with the peritoneum towards the incision.
You instruct the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you completely release the adhesions from behind.  You explore with your finger lest there be something of the twisted intestine in the hard white peritoneum; if you find anything, push it down into the abdomen.
Then take a needle with a thick thread twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin next to the incision. Then cut the ends of the thread loop so that there are four threads. Then you overlap them firmly and tie the peritoneum, which we said is under the skin of the scrotum, tightly on two sides. Then you also twist the ends of the threads and tie them tightly so that nothing from the vessels that nourish it can reach them. Lest a hot swelling result from that, and make a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers away from it. After these two ligatures, leave the amount of a finger bones width of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin.
Lest a hot swelling result from that, and make a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers away from it. After these two ligatures, leave the amount of a finger bones width of the peritoneum under the scrotum skin. And cut all the rest in a circle and remove the testicle with it. Then make an incision at the bottom of the scrotum skin from which blood and pus will flow, as we described before. Then use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound and use the ligature which we described.
And one may cauterize the white peritoneum which we mentioned after cutting it, cautiously, because of bleeding, for this often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself; if it is slow to fall off, then loosen it with warm water and apply something that will make it sweat until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the treatment of wounds until it heals.
Chapter sixty-six.
Concerning the windy hernia.  I have not seen anyone dare to treat this windy hernia with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as we described in the hernia with a sac, that is to say, that the vessels should be gently ligated below after the incision on it.
Then incise it in the middle and treat the tumor with what opens it until the vessels fall away, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter sixty-seven.
Concerning the hernia which is in the groin.
A hernia may appear in the groin, as we said, so that the place swells and nothing of the intestine descends into the scrotum; and if it descends, it is slight and returns at all times.
But if time passes, the fissure in the peritoneum increases until the intestine and epiploon descend into the scrotum, and this occurs from the extension of the peritoneum which is in the groin, as we said. That is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the place swells and protrudes, and its treatment is by cauterization, as I have previously described.
And it may be treated with the cautery in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, then you incise the swollen, protruding hernia horizontally to the extent of three fingers, then you separate the peritoneal membranes which are under the skin.
Until, when the white peritoneum which follows is uncovered, you take a probe and place it on the protruding part of the peritoneum and press it into the depth of the abdomen, then you sew the two protruding parts of the peritoneum to the end of the probe and join one to the other with the suture.
Then you remove the probe and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle nor anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal hernia, then you treat it with the treatment of wounds.
If the threads are cut, I clean them and dress the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the protrusion of the hernia does not increase.
And cauterization is better in this place because it is closer to safety.
Chapter sixty-eight.
On the relaxation of the skin of the scrotum.
Often the skin of the scrotum relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and its appearance is unsightly.  He who wishes to treat it should place the patient on his back.
And he cuts all the relaxed skin on the thick skin, then brings the lips together. And if you wish, first sew the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, and make sure of the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not have mentioned it in this book. However, I mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it may be in the knowledge of the physician if he is asked about it, and so that he may know the treatment of one whom it has affected; and the other, that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, goats, cats, and the like of animals.  So I say that castration is of two types: either by crushing or by cutting.  That which is by crushing, its method is that we seat the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are not perceptible to the touch. As for castration by cutting, it is necessary that you hold the animal and squeeze the skin of its testicles with the left hand, then tie the suspensory ligaments and make one incision on each testicle.
Until, when the testicles appear, cut them after you have skinned them, and do not leave on them any of the membranes except the thin membrane which is on the vessels. This type of castration is better than that which is by crushing.
Because crushing may leave something of the testicles, so the animal desires coitus. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of the hermaphrodite.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types. One is that which appears in the area following the scrotum, or in the skin of the scrotum between the testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva, with hair, and urine may flow from that which is in the scrotum's skin.
But in women, there is one type, and it is above the vulva on the pubis like small male genitalia protruding outward; one of them is like a man's penis and the two are like testicles.  And the treatment of the three types.
The two types in men and the one type in women: it is necessary to cut away that excess flesh until its trace is removed, then treat it with the treatment of other wounds until it heals.  But as for the second type in men, from which urine flows that is in the scrotum's skin, there is no action in it, nor is there any benefit from it at all.
Chapter seventy-one.
On the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may exceed the natural amount until it becomes large and its appearance is ugly, and it may become large in some women until it is spread like that of men and becomes suitable for intercourse.
Then you should grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or with a hook and cut it, and do not exaggerate in the cutting, especially in the depth of the root, lest bleeding occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals. As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And it may protrude outwards like a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the "tail disease."  Then you should cut it as you cut the clitoris, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter seventy-two.
In the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vagina is imperforate or the opening is small, and this is either natural, being present from birth, or accidental.  The accidental kind results from a previously existing condition, and it may be from an excess of growing flesh.
Or a thin or thick membrane, and it may be in the depth of the uterus, or in its sides, or above or below it.  It prevents intercourse, conception, and childbirth, and sometimes menstruation.  Its identification is by the observation of the midwife.
If the obstruction is apparent and near, but if it is not apparent, then she should examine it with her fingers or with a probe. If the obstruction is from a thin membrane and is close to the labia, then proceed quickly to break it. This is done by placing something like pledgets on the labia, then placing both thumbs together.
The woman is on her back with her legs spread apart. Then she forcefully stretches the labia until the thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened. Then she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and places it on the location. The woman is to have intercourse every day so that the location does not close again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, then it should be incised with a broad scalpel resembling an aspen leaf. If the obstruction is from excess growing flesh, then suspend it with hooks and cut it.  Have with you styptics that do not sting, such as acacia, quince, and frankincense, mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not close quickly. Keep it in place for several days and use a dry linen wick. Then treat it with the usual treatment for healing until it is healed.  There may be another growth of flesh in the uterus; it should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor.  A cancerous tumor in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red sores that appear in the vulva of women.
As for hemorrhoids, it is a swelling of the mouths of the veins until much blood constantly flows from them. If the hemorrhoids persist, they become warts.  I have already mentioned their types and symptoms in the classification, and we will mention them here.
What accepts treatment and what does not accept treatment. I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are deep within the uterus and do not appear to the touch, cannot be treated with iron.  Those that are at the mouth of the uterus and can be felt are those that are treated.
Then the woman should enter a cool room, and then stretch the warts with a scraper or a rough cloth and cut them from their roots. Then apply to them, when the bleeding occurs, one of the styptics that stops the blood without causing injury.
Such as acacia,  [a plant whose name is similar to the word for]  "shiyan,"  frankincense, and the like. Then she should raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding continues, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, gallnuts, and the like. Then take barley flour, and knead it with honey and vinegar.
And apply it as a compress to her back. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, apply cupping glasses to the loins and breasts without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of  tharthith, or the juice of  lamb's tongue, or the juice of the branches of al-illiq with a gallnut decoction, and apply it to the area.
When the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of entrails, rough in appearance.  What appears of it should be cut as I mentioned regarding warts, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the suppuration which occurs in the uterus.
Many types of tumors may occur in the uterus, such as cancer, the stony tumor, ulcers, the polyp, the phagedenic ulcer, fistulas, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and the hot tumor. I have described all these diseases, their types, and their signs in the classification.
It is necessary to mention in this chapter the hot tumor which occurs in the uterus, if it is among the tumors which collect for a time, how its suppuration is with iron.  One should look; if the pain of the tumor is initially severe, with throbbing, inflammation, and fever,
and you see it red when touched, do not hasten to its suppuration, and treat it with what helps in maturation until these symptoms subside. Then it is necessary that the woman sit on a chair with two seats and lean on its back.
and bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, and her thighs should be spread apart, and her arms should be under her knees, and tie them with a suitable ligature.
Then the midwife sits on the right side and uses the instrument with which the mouth of the uterus is opened, the image of which comes later.  If you want to use this instrument, you should estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's uterus, lest what enters from the instrument be more than the depth of the uterus.
This will harm the patient. If the instrument is longer than the depth, it is necessary to place pledgets on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the depth of the uterus. The screw which runs in the instrument should be from the upper side.
And you hold the instrument, and the attendant turns the screw until the cervix is opened. When the abscess appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and thin, you should incise it at its head with a broad scalpel. Then, when all the matter is evacuated,
a soft tent soaked in rose oil or green oil containing some astringent should be placed in the wound. The tent should protrude from the incision in the cervix, and clean wool soaked in water in which althaea has been boiled should be placed externally on the uterus and pubis.
Then, after two days, treat with the ointment which is suitable for that until it heals.
And the uterus and wound may be washed by injecting with water and honey, then with water in which liquorice root or long zedoary has been boiled, then return to the ointment, if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Then you should refrain from treating it with iron, but it should be treated as we mentioned in the section.
Chapter Seventy-Five.
In teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses if they emerge in an unnatural way.
The midwife should first know the form of natural childbirth; its signs are: if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to breathe the air, and what she is suffering from childbirth is eased.
And the emergence of the fetus is quick; know that this birth is in the natural way, and its emergence is head first with the placenta attached to it or hanging from its navel. If you see these signs, then you should press her belly so that the fetus emerges quickly.
For if it descends head first, the placenta descends with it and cleanses from those impurities a good cleansing. And other than this birth is unnatural and blameworthy, for the fetus may emerge on its feet and its hands may emerge before its head and feet.
And one hand or one foot, or its head and hand emerge, or it emerges folded, and perhaps it turns on its back, and similar blameworthy forms.  Therefore, the midwife should be skillful and gentle with all these things and beware of mistakes and errors.
And I am explaining each form and how to manage it so that you may be guided by that and understand it. If the fetus emerges head first  the natural emergence  and this is difficult for the woman and the labor is hard on her, and you see that her strength has weakened, then seat her on a chair and instruct women to hold her.
And pack her womb with cooked fenugreek and moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers and cuts the placenta with it, or cuts it with her fingernail, until the moisture within flows out, and she presses the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then she should inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with shirj ointment, then after the injection, she should instruct her to strain and make her sneeze with a feather, holding her mouth and nose; then the fetus will emerge immediately. If the hands of the fetus emerge first, then they should be gently returned little by little.
If it does not manage to enter, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs upwards, then shake the stool on the ground while the woman holds on so that she does not fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, then cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them moderately; then he will come out.  The emergence of the fetus with its feet: If the fetus emerges with its feet, then they should be pushed upwards, then the fetus should be turned little by little until it is straightened, then one of its legs should be taken and extended little by little.
If they come out to the thighs, then instruct her to strain and make her sneeze with a feather; then it will come out. If it does not come out as described, then return the fetus little by little until it is in its natural position; then it will come out easily. If it is prevented by all that we have described,
Take some henbane saliva, fenugreek saliva, shirj ointment, and dissolved gum, and pound them all well in a mortar, then apply it to the vulva of the woman and the lower part of her abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the sharaf.
If you see that her lower parts have softened, then make a pessary for her from myrrh and have her hold it.
