If the pessary remains for an hour, then seat her on a chair, then make her sneeze and close her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen; then the fetus will come out immediately. The emergence of the fetus on its knees and hands:
If it comes out in this manner, then gradually insert your hands, then the woman should lie on her back and let her hips hang down while lying on a bed, and clasp her hands, then gently squeeze above her abdomen. Then remove the cushions from under her legs so that her lower parts are elevated. If the fetus does not come out, then take both her legs and move them vigorously, then gently squeeze above the sharaf until the fetus ascends upwards, then the midwife inserts her hand and straightens the fetus little by little.
And tell the woman to strain until the fetus comes out.  The emergence of the fetus sideways, hanging by one of its hands:  gradually work to return its hand. If you are not able to return it, then have the woman stand and walk; if she is unable to walk, then have her lie down...
...on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice that I described from the saliva. If labor pains seize her, then seat her on a chair, then work to return the hand and straighten the fetus into its natural form, and make the woman sneeze and tell her to strain until it comes out.
The emergence of the fetus on its back, extending its hands, and its face towards its mother's back: the midwife should grasp the hand of the fetus, then turn it little by little and straighten it gently.  When it is straight, then apply to its head what we described of the fat and poultices.
Then tell her to move little by little. If she does that, then seat her on a chair and tell her to lean to her right side. Then tell her to strain and make her sneeze with the [nasal] speculum, for then it will come out.  The emergence of the fetus upright on its side.
If it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the placenta, then tear the placenta with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.  If it is attached to the face and neck of the fetus, then cut it from the umbilical cord lest it be strangled and die; then push the fetus inwards until it is as it should be.
And tell the woman to strain repeatedly, for then it will come out easily. The emergence of twins or many fetuses: know that twins are frequently born, and three or four may be born and live, although that is rare; as for five, it is something outside of nature, they do not live at all.
And they are surrounded, even if numerous, by a single placenta, and they are separated by a membrane-like partition between them, connected to the umbilical cord of each one. And they may emerge like a single fetus, in a natural and easy manner; and they may emerge in a blameworthy manner, as we have described.
If some of them are difficult at birth and come to what we have mentioned of unnatural forms, then treat them as we have described.  And let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness and slowness, and contrive for every form with whatever means are available to her that lead to safety.
Mentioning what is conceived in the womb of fetuses that are aborted: one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten may be conceived in the womb. It has been confirmed to me that a woman aborted seven, and another fifteen, all conceived.
By the estimation of the All-Knowing and All-Mighty, all of this should be in the knowledge of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing may occur to her one day, and she may deal with it as is appropriate.
Chapter Seventy-Six.
On the extraction of the dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the division of treatment and it does not come out, and the matter necessitates using the hand, then you should look: if the woman is strong and healthy and does not have any of the symptoms from which death is feared, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downwards and her legs raised, and a servant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, lest her body be pulled when the fetus is extracted.
Then moisten her vagina with moist ointments along with mallow and fenugreek saliva and flaxseed, then the midwife should anoint her hand with these ointments and saliva, then gently insert it into the uterus, then search with it for a place to hook the fetus with the hooks, and the suitable places for that.
Look, if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, then insert the hooks into its eyes or the back of its head or its mouth or its palate or under its chin or its clavicle or in the places near the ribs or under the cartilages.
But if its descent is feet first, then in the pubis and the middle ribs.  The hook should be held in the right hand, its bend between the fingers of the left hand, and the hand should be gently inserted, and a hook inserted into some of those places we mentioned.
Until you reach something empty, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if needed, so that the traction is even and does not lean to one side, then pull with a steady pull, and the pull should not be straight only, but moved to each side.
So that its exit is facilitated, as is done with a tooth when it is extracted.  Between these actions, the pull should be relaxed. If it is obstructed from one side, the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them from that side to move what is obstructed. If part of the fetus emerges, then move the hooks to other places.
It is slightly raised, doing this until the entire fetus emerges. If a hand emerges before anything else and cannot be returned because of compression, then a cloth should be wrapped around it and pulled until, when it has entirely emerged, it is cut.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow.  Thus should you act with the other hand and with the feet. If the head of the fetus is large and experiences pressure during its exit, or if there is collected water in its head, then a hooked scalpel should be inserted between the fingers.
And the head is incised with it so that the water emerges, or it is drawn out with the instrument called the *mishdaq*, the image of which, along with the images of the other instruments, comes in the chapter after this one.
Similarly, if the fetus is naturally large-headed, then the skull should be incised or crushed with the *mishdaq*, as we said, then the bones removed with the hooks. If the head emerges and is compressed at the clavicle, then it should be incised until the moisture in the chest flows out.
Then the chest will contract. If the chest does not contract and egress is not achieved, then cut as much as you are able. If the lower abdomen is swollen or has a tumor, then it should be incised so that the moisture flows out.
If the fetus is in a breech position, then pulling it out is easy, and straightening it towards the uterine os is simple. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, then it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be incised so that what is inside flows out.
If all the members are extracted and the head is retracted and retained, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the uterine os is open, let her insert her hand into the cervix and seek the head with it, and pull it with her fingers to the uterine os. Then she inserts one or two hooks and pulls it out with them.
If the uterine os is constricted by a hot tumor that has occurred, then it should not be forced.  Rather, then it is necessary to use the pouring of moist, fatty substances and to use poultices, and to sit in waters that relax and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if it can be straightened, then what we mentioned for the living fetus should be used. If this is not possible, then it should be cut and removed.  Nothing of the placenta should be left inside at all. When you have finished your treatment, use what we mentioned concerning the treatment of hot tumors that occur in the uteri.
If hemorrhage occurs, let her descend into astringent waters and use all other treatments. I have witnessed a woman who had conceived, and the fetus died in her womb, then she conceived again upon it.
Then the other fetus also died. After a long time, a tumor appeared in her uterus, and it swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close. Then I applied some strong, drawing ointments to it.
Then a bone came out of the place. Then days passed, and another bone came out. I was amazed by this, since the abdomen is not a place where bones are found. I judged that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.  So I examined the wound and removed many bones from the skull of the fetus from it.
I continued doing this until I removed many bones from her, and the woman was in the best of health.  And she lived like that for a time, discharging a little pus from the place. And I have presented here this rare case.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skillful physician attempts with his hand in treatment.
Chapter seventy-seven.
In it are images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a screw to open the mouth of the womb.
This is the instrument which is used to set books straight; it has two screws at the ends of the two pieces of wood, except that this screw should be finer than the instrument and should be either of ebony or boxwood, and the width of each piece of wood should be the thickness of two fingers.
And their width is about a finger and their length is a span and a half, and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two runners of the same kind of wood which are fastened in them; their length is half a span or a little more, and their width is about two fingers or a little more, and these two runners...
are those which enter the mouth of the womb to open it when you turn the screws; an image of another instrument for the same purpose, finer and lighter.
It is made of ebony or boxwood in the shape of hooks, except that it has two runners at its end, as you see; the length of each runner is about a span, and their width is two fingers; and if you wish to open the womb with it,
then seat the woman on a bed with her legs hanging down, spread apart between her thighs, then insert these two runners gently into the mouth of the womb, while you hold the end of the instrument below between her thighs, then open your hand with the instrument as you do with hooks, as much as you want to open the womb. until the midwife does what she wants.  An image of another screw mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument which pushes the fetus.
Image of the forceps with which the head of the fetus is grasped.
It resembles scissors, it has teeth at the end as you see, and it may be made rectangular like hooks, in this image as you see, it has teeth like the teeth of a saw, it cuts with it and crushes.
Image of a pusher also.
Image of a hook.
This hook should be somewhat thick so that it does not break when the fetus is pulled with it. Image of a hook with two barbs.
Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these instruments, the more numerous their types and the better prepared by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his reputation among people. Therefore, do not disdain any instrument to have ready, for there is bound to be need for it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
On the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold herself, then cause her to sneeze with a sternutatory [powder] and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out in this way, well and good; otherwise, take a measure.
And pierce a hole in the lid and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as rue, henbane, dill, chamomile, wormwood, marshmallow, and centauryall these medicaments or some of themand immerse them in water and place them on the fire. Then you place a silver tube on the hole in the lid of the pot, and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the womb, then cause her to sneeze as we said. Then the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in goose grease or mallow juice.
Then she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out little by little. If it is attached deep in the womb, insert the hand as we described, until you find the placenta, then pull it little by little towards the vulva.
Lest the womb fall out with strong pulling, but it should be moved gently to the sides, right and left, then the amount of pulling is increased. Then it will yield and be freed from adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is constricted, we have described the treatment with sneezing and the treatment with the pot and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, beware of violence upon it in pulling again.  But it is necessary to tie what has come out of it to the mouth of the womb to the woman's thigh, then inject it with the quaternary ointment, for it will cause it to rot after a few days.
And it will dissolve and come out.  However, if it becomes putrid, a bad smell will rise from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient, so it is necessary to use suitable fumigation.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried the fumigation of rue and dried figs.  The image of the instrument with which the woman is fumigated when menstruation and the placenta are retained, and so on:
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper. The thin end is placed in the vagina, and the wide end over the fire, and the incense is placed on the coals.  It is held until that incense is gone, and then another is added.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of the non-perforated rectum.
Many boys are born with their rectums imperforate; a thin membrane closes it.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger; otherwise, let her incise it with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then she should apply wool soaked in wine and oil, and then treat it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it will close, she should place a lead tube in the opening for many days and remove it whenever the child wants to defecate.
The rectum may also become obstructed by the healing of a wound or a tumor; this adhesion should be incised, and then treated as previously described, with the lead tube.
Chapter Eighty.
On the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower parts.
Fistulas that occur in the lower parts are a complication and thickening that occurs near the anus, externally or internally, from one side or another. The fistula may be single or multiple.
If this complication becomes chronic, it opens and a watery white fluid or thin pus discharges from it.  Some of these fistulas may penetrate to the bladder and to the intestine, while others may not. Penetrating fistulas can be identified by the passage of feces and flatus from them when the patient defecates.
Worms may also emerge from them.  There may be fistulas, if they are internal, that penetrate to the bladder and to the urethra.  Some may penetrate to the hip joint and to the sacrum. A fistula penetrating to the rectum can also be distinguished from one that does not.
You should insert your index finger into the rectum and insert a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula, if the fistula is not tortuous. If it is tortuous, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair into it.
Until you feel the probe or hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all, and nothingfeces, flatus, or wormsemerges from the opening as we have said, then know that it is not penetrating; proceed to treatment.
If the fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary passage, the sign is the passage of urine from it, and the inability of the site to heal with medication.  If it penetrates to the hip joint or to the sacrum, the sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no tortuosity to the bone, and pain is present in that area, and pus constantly emerges from it, and treatment is ineffective and it does not heal with ointment, these penetrating fistulas are incurable.
And their treatment is a vain effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt it. As for those that are not penetrating and are not chronic, recovery is hoped for with cautery, as I will describe and as I have tested.  This is that the patient should lie on his back between your hands.
And he should raise his legs, and his thighs should be inclined towards his abdomen. Then insert the copper or lead probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel with your finger that the probe has penetrated by itself, uncovered, without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrating, so do not exert yourself on it, for there is no cure for it, as we said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely. And of the treatments which are hoped to be effective is that you heat a thin cautery iron according to the size of the fistula, as mentioned before, and insert it hot into the fistula until it reaches towards the anus, then repeat it two or three times.
Until you know that all that excess, matted flesh resembling a bird's quill has been burnt. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter until the burnt flesh comes out. Then treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment. But if you insert the probe and it does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and there was a thick partition of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the fistula near the surface of the skin, then incise the skin at the beginning of the fistula. And you pass with the incision with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach with the incision where the end of the probe ends, and the probe becomes free and falls out. Then you clean out that matted flesh which resembles a bird's quill tube and leave nothing of it.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the blood overwhelms you and prevents you from cutting that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is cauterization with fire or a strong medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good things: burning that excess flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture. Then treat it with lint soaked in butter or sulfur powdered with oil until the area suppurates and that burnt flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that grow firm flesh.
These are medicines that constrict and dry, for if it is closed, it has healed and there is no fear of recurrence. If the fistula has reached the depth of the anus and is distant from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the anus and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it a membrane of peritoneum or flesh and it is near to penetration, then the action in it is only by way of hope, that is, that you use one of three methods: either cauterize it as we said.
Or incise it until you reach its bottom so that you are able to insert the lint and treat it from near. Do not go too far with the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus and cause the patient to involuntarily pass stool. Then treat it as we mentioned, and perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or penetrate that membrane and pierce it after incising it near the anus as we said, either with a probe or another sharp-pointed instrument. Then clean all the adherent flesh in the incision as far as you can thoroughly.
Then sew up the whole wound, including the incision and the upper opening, until it closes, leaving the fistula open inside the anus, which will be easier for the patient.  The fistula may be perforated in this manner.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is on a side of the anus toward the surface of the body with the skin and the anus's ring, then take a probe with a perforated tip like a shoemaker's needle, in this manner:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five or so threads, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom, if it is penetrating into the margin of the anus from the inside nearby.
Then bring out the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and bringing out the end of the thread. Bring the two ends together and tighten them, and leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads is severed and falls off. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, penetrate it however you can, except that it should not be too deep, for you should not do that because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then do with it as we have mentioned until it heals.
The image of the hooked scalpel with which fistulas are incised: its curvature is very sharp, and the other side is not sharp, lest you cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter eighty-one.
Concerning the opening of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling red bladders or like grapes, and there are small and large ones, and blood constantly flows from them.
And they may be one or many, and they may be outside the rectum and at its edges, except that those which are outside the rectum are, in most cases, less moist, with a yellowish fluid or a little blood constantly flowing from them, and they are the color of the body.
The treatment of those which are inside the rectum is that you order the patient to strain and bear down until the rectum protrudes and the growths appear to you. Then you quickly grasp them with hooks or seize them with your fingernail and then cut them at their bases. If the hooks do not hold because of their moisture and laxity,
then rub them with a rough cloth or a piece of a rasping file, or pull them with your fingers, then cut them, and then sprinkle on them after cutting some sharp powders to act as a cautery, or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with the other treatments until it heals. If the rectum does not protrude, then inject the patient with an enema containing a little stimulant to wash what is in the rectum and facilitate its rapid protrusion when the patient strains. As for the growths outside the rectum, their treatment is easy and simple.
That is, you grasp it with your fingernail or hook it with a hook and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron, then he should use a ligature in this manner: take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upward and pass the needle through its base from the other side, and twist the ends of the thread below the needle while it is crosswise, and pull the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the warts, leaving one unligated.
so that the excess blood flows from them, then place on the rectum a cloth soaked in rose oil or kypriot oil and place it on the rectum, and tell the patient to be still, then leave him until they fall. When the warts fall, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, they often result from dryness of the stool and retention of the temperament. If it becomes chronic and medicine does not work, then it should be scraped with a lancet or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper crust that prevents its healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more forcefully than the first time until it bleeds well and peels, then treat it; for it will heal.
Chapter eighty-two.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, dry warts and the ant.
A nail is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail; it occurs on the whole body, especially on the soles of the feet and the toes, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise around the nail, grasp it with a hook or a forceps, and remove it from its root, then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it as was previously described in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, matted, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
They often appear on the hands of children.  Its treatment is to stretch the wart and cut it, or pierce it with a silk thread or a hair until it falls off; and if you wish, cauterize it as previously described with fire or a caustic medicine.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts may be removed with the instrument which I have described in the treatment of the ant. As for the ant, it is also a small, matted, thick protrusion on the surface of the body, going very deep; and if it is exposed to cold, one feels in it something similar to the sting of ants.
It occurs on the whole body, but most often on the hands.  Its treatment is to take a tube made of a strong goose feather or eagle feather, and place this tube on the ant until the circumference of the tube surrounds it on all sides, then rotate your hand with the tube until you incise around the ant.
Then push it into the depth of the flesh, and the ant will be removed from its root. And if you wish, do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The upper part of the tube should be solid and twisted to facilitate gripping and twisting it with the fingers. And if you wish, cauterize the ant as previously described in the cauterization of warts in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
On the forms of instruments used for injections in cases of rectal disorders, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, china, or cast or beaten copper.  These instruments may be made small or large, according to the users. Those used in the treatment of small children should be small, and those for patients with narrow or painful rectums should be very gentle. Image of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is tied, is wide in this manner, having a partition where the bladder is tied, as you see, and its lower end, which enters the rectum, is solid, very smooth, and thin; on one side are two holes, and on the other, one hole.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a gimlet, or a little thicker. The bladder, in which the medicine is placed, is made from an animal's bladder or sheepskin, shaped like a small flask, its diameter being a cubit.
It is pierced with many holes, in a circular pattern, the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger. Then a tight thread of twisted yarn, made of ten threads or so, is inserted into these holes, and the bladder is gathered together like a flask. Then, when the medicine is placed inside, it is tightly bound in the head of the syringe between two stones, and then injected. This is an image of the circle of the bladder and the hole:
The method of injection is to fill the skin or bladder with warmed medicine, to a degree the patient can tolerate. Beware of it being excessively hot or cold.  Then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs upwards.
A cloth or blanket is placed beneath him. Then the end of the syringe is lubricated with oil, egg white, fenugreek saliva, or the like. Then it is gently inserted into the rectum. Then the attendant takes the bladder in both hands and squeezes it firmly until all that is in the syringe is emptied into the intestine.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient raises his legs to the wall. If the medicine stimulates evacuation, let him restrain himself as much as possible. If he sleeps with it all night, it will be more effective. If it is injected for diarrhea and its exit is hastened, then repeat the injection until he recovers.
If there are painful hemorrhoids or tumors in the rectum, let the syringe be very delicate and smooth, smoother than those used to inject children.  This is an image of a delicate syringe with skin stretched over it:
Chapter Eighty-Four
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of things similar to cauterizations, and I now mention here their treatment by hand and by medicines completely. I say that wounds differ according to the thing causing the wound and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause wounds are many, such as the blow of a stone, or a cut by a sword or knife, or a stab by a lance, or a stick, or an arrow, and so on from many things.
And as for wounds according to the locations on the body, such as the wound that occurs on the head, or the neck, or the chest, or the abdomen, or the liver, and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds especially because the compound ones will be mentioned in the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
I say that when a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no fracture of the bone, I look; if it is from the blow of a stone or the like, and only the skin has been split, and the wound is large, and I fear for the patient the occurrence of hot inflammation, then I hasten and incise the scalp in the appropriate place. And do not delay that, and let blood be drawn from him according to his strength if nothing prevents bloodletting, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is little, and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
And warn the patient against fullness, and thick drinks and foods; if something prevents his bloodletting, then let him reduce food and not approach meats or drinks, and apply to the wound, if hot inflammation occurs in it, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with a drink that contains astringency.
And if you are safe from hot inflammation, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and the air has not altered it, the  powder which I describe to you shortly; but if the air has altered it, then apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals; if the wound is large and is from the cut of a sword or the like, and its edges do not come together with plasters, then join it with sutures as I describe in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin exposes the bone and hangs down, and its attachment is only in a small ligament, then cut it, then treat the wound with medicines that produce solid flesh in place of the skin, and order the patient to nourish himself with foods that contain strength, such as heads and pastes, and the like.
If decay occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all that is decayed and throw it away, then treat it; if in these wounds there is an artery or vein that bleeds, and the bleeding is not stopped by medicines, then search for the artery.
If you find it and it is not severed, then sever it with a scalpel and tie it; and if necessity requires it, if what we mentioned does not work, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, then their treatment is easy; it suffices to treat them by sprinkling on them this powder while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes it. The preparation of the powder is that one part of frankincense, two parts of acacia, and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime are taken; all are ground, sifted, and the wound is packed with it and bound tightly until it adheres well.
It congeals with the blood, then bind it from above with bandages and leave it; do not unbind it as long as it does not swell, for the medicine does not separate from it until it heals.  The lime alone may do this if you do not have frankincense and acacia.
This action may also be done with alum in many large wounds if its application and binding are expertly done, and the wounds are fresh with blood, before the air changes them, but if the air has changed them somewhat, especially in summer.
Then there is no point in applying this powder; treat it with what we have mentioned, by applying some ripening ointments or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until pus forms, then treat it with other treatments until it heals.
But if a fracture occurs with the wound, and it is slight, then draw it with a splint.  I have described the treatment of head fractures in what follows, in the surgery of the neck; if the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in treatment between it and a head wound if it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with things that cause strong constriction, such as verdigris, vitriol, and the like, because they injure the nerve and cause it to spasm.
Nor should anything cold be placed on the wound, because the substance of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs; and what you treat it with should be soft medicines, such as finely ground litharge washed with fresh water several times, moistened with oil or rose ointment and tutty.
If it is also washed with fresh water and other minerals in this manner, and moist ointments, but if the wound is large, use stitching or bind its edges with compresses, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and a cavity forms at its bottom where pus collects, lance it in its lowest part; if an artery has been severed in the wound and you see its blood flowing, ligate it or cauterize it, or if necessary, amputate it.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, but the windpipe is unharmed, join the edges of the skin by stitching over the windpipe; do not touch the windpipe itself, but smooth it and restore it to its natural shape, then tie it tightly and leave it for some days.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals; if the wound has affected the bone, explore it; if there are bone splinters, extract them all, or as many as possible; what you cannot reach, leave until the wound suppurates and heals, making their removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the powder we have described; if a bone becomes carious in the wound and forms a fistula, treat it according to what will be mentioned in its chapter, in the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or knife, and you see it has depth, observe it; if air comes out of it when the patient breathes, know that it is a mortal wound; if it does not have depth and is fresh, bleeding, do not initially apply powder to it, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depth.
It will then retreat to the heart and kill the patient; but place on it an attractive ointment, and dress it twice a day; if you do not have ointment, put old cotton in the mouth of the wound to absorb the moisture that comes from it, and have the patient sleep on the wound.
so that what collects in it may flow out; and if three days or more have passed since the infliction of the wound, and the patient has not suffered convulsions, a bad pulse, or shortness of breath, and you see that his other conditions are good, know that the wound is safe, so treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it heals.
If its visibility is difficult and the discharge is continuous, know that it has become a fistula, and treat it in its chapter; the patient should lie on the wound so that what collects in it may flow out, and if the wound is in the chest or back, treat it with the stitching described above.
If it is large, cauterize it; if it is small, use powders.  If it has affected the bone and broken off pieces of it, explore the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are free; if they are not free, leave them until...
the wound becomes foul, for then it will be easy to remove them. As for other wounds occurring in other members, their treatment is governed by what we have mentioned.
Chapter eighty-five.
On wounds of the abdomen, the protrusion of the intestines, and their suturing.
The rupture which occurs in the abdomen may be large, or it may be small or medium.  A large rupture may allow the exit of one or several intestines, and their replacement and suturing will be more difficult and arduous.
A small rupture may also be difficult in another way, and its reduction may be more arduous.  If the intestine is not immediately returned to its place, it swells and thickens, making its replacement difficult. For this reason, the medium-sized rupture is preferable, because its reduction is not as difficult as in the other two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is of four types. Two sutures are general, suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other wounds of the body. The other two sutures are special, applicable to all wounds, but they are particularly suitable for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation and clarification. I say that if the wound is small and some of the intestine has protruded and its replacement is difficult, this is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the rupture, as we said, or because the intestine has been previously inflated by the entry of air.
If this is so, then it should be warmed by immersing a sponge or a wet cloth in lukewarm water alone, or in water in which dill, rue, and spikenard have been boiled, and applying it to the intestine until the inflation subsides.
A styptic drink may also do this, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.  When you see the inflation has subsided, moisten the intestine with water in which either quince or borage has been boiled, for this will make its entry easier.
If its return is still impossible after this treatment, make a small incision in the rupture with the instrument used to incise fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than the incision.  Recourse to incision is only taken after necessity and failure of other methods. This is the image of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and pointed, the other side is not pointed. The thin end is not as sharp as a scalpel, but is slightly blunt. It is an instrument similar to a small forceps, as you see. If the wound is wide and the intestine has entered, then it should be returned to its natural shape.
Its proper place, if you can manage it, is best. But if the rupture is wide and is in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs higher than his head; if it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the rupture is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the surgery higher than the other. This should be done in major and moderate surgeries.
As for minor surgeries, place him as you are able. Then have a skilled assistant ready to hold the entire rupture with both hands, bringing its edges together, then revealing it to the suture-worker, little by little.
This is the description of a single general suture:  Take a needle, or several needles, according to the size of the wound. Then leave a space at the end of the rupture equal to the thickness of the little finger, and pierce with a single needle, without thread, through both edges of the skin. along with the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin from the inside, until you pass through on that side.  Having brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, making four layers, then tie a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the edges of the wound are firmly joined.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger, and insert another needle, then fasten it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as you need, until you complete the entire circumference of the wound. Let the needles be of moderate thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin, therefore they should be of moderate thinness and thickness.  And the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close to the seam.
lest the flesh quickly tear and the wound open before it heals; nor should you be too far apart with the stitches lest the wound be prevented from uniting. Then cut off the ends of the needles lest they harm the patient when he sleeps, and make him pads of linen scraps on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of stitching with needles is best suited for small wounds, because one or two needles, or thereabouts, may suffice for stitching them.
As for the method of stitching for a second type of wound, it is to gather together with the stitching the four edges, namely the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, all at once with a needle threaded with a twisted thread of moderate thinness and thickness. Then, when you have passed the needle through these four edges, return the needle to the side from which you began.
So that the thread falls interwoven from the top of the wound, so that the stitching is according to the stitching of a purse which is tightened with it, making the space between each stitch the thickness of the little finger.  This stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching, I stitched a wound which appeared on a man in his abdomen; he had been wounded with a knife, and the opening of the wound was more than a span, and about two spans of the middle intestine had protruded, and the opening was in the middle of the abdomen.
I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he was cured, and he lived many years thereafter, managing all his affairs.  The physicians had judged that he would never recover at all.
And it is remarkable that I did not treat him with ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medicines. I used to place worn cotton on the wound twice a day and attend to washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special stitching, I mention it according to the words of Galen: that you begin the stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside; then, when the needle has passed through the skin and the muscle, going straight along the whole length of the abdomen...
...leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side into which you inserted the needle, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, into the other edge of the peritoneum. Then, when you have passed it through, pass it again through this same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside, and as you pass it through, pass it also through the edge of the peritoneum on its side, until you pass it through all of it.
Then begin again from this same side and sew it together with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out of the skin near it; then return it into that skin and sew the edge of the peritoneum on the other side together with this edge of the peritoneum.
And bring it out of the skin on its side, and do that again and again, one time after another, until you sew up the whole wound in that way. The general description of this stitch is that it is sewn like the sewing of furs for furriers.
That you sew the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, and leave it once from the other side until you finish. As for the second type of special stitch which Galen also mentioned, this is his exact wording:
It is that you sew it in the manner that some practitioners sew it, by bringing together each part to its counterpart which naturally matches it; thus they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and this is done as I describe to you.
It is necessary that you insert the needle into the edge of the peritoneum nearest to you from the outside, and pass it to the inside in it alone, and leave the edges of the peritoneum; then return the needle and pass it from the outside to the inside in both edges of the peritoneum; then return it again.
And pass it from the inside to the outside in the other edge of the peritoneum which is on the opposite side. This type of stitch is better than the easy common stitch, which is the stitch previously mentioned. This is Galen's exact wording.  Some have said that the suturing of the abdomen may be straightened with two needles. That is, you insert into them one thread, and begin by inserting the needle from your side and pass it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side, and pass it from your side, according to the stitching of the saddle-bags equally.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then its stitching is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its rule in its treatment is the rule of all other wounds; that is, if you find the wound fresh with its blood before the air changes it, and you return the intestines and sew it up and tighten it, then apply to it the medicated pledgets.
If the wound has been changed by the air, then apply to it some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the stitches fall out and the peritoneum and the mesentery unite; then treat it as you treat all other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medicines, apply to it from the time it begins to suppurate, old cotton, and change it twice a day as I have taught you, until it heals. For you do not need other treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple.  If you fear that the wound will involve the principal organs in pain,
Then it is necessary to dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and the armpit. If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines  for this often happens  then inject him with a lukewarm, astringent, black drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into its cavity. And know that what is thick of the intestines is easier to heal, and what is thin of them is more difficult to heal.
And as for the intestine known as the jejunum, it does not at all accept healing from a wound that occurs in it, because of the multitude of its veins and their size, and the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nature of the nerve. But if what emerged from the wound is omentum and you find it moist, then return it in the same way that you return the intestine.
If a period of time passes and it has turned green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the place that has blackened, lest bleeding occur, for in the omentum are veins and arteries; then cut below that ligature.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound, outside of it, so that it is easy for you to remove and extract it when the omentum falls off and the wound suppurates.  [Description of a wound occurring in the intestine:] But if a perforation occurs in the intestine and it is small
it is possible that it will heal in some people; for I saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach.  The wound festered and became a fistula from which feces and wind emerged; so I treated him, although I did not hope for his healing. And I continued to treat him until he healed and the place joined. When I saw that the place had joined, I feared for the patient that a bad incident would occur in his abdomen, but no bad incident whatsoever occurred to him, and he remained in the best of conditions. He rightly eats, drinks, copulates, enters the bath, and exerts himself in his service.  And some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner.
And it is that large-headed ants are taken, then the edges of the wound are brought together, and then one of them, with its mouth open, is placed on the edges of the wound.  When it grasps and tightens its mouth, its head is cut off, for it adheres and does not come undone. Then another ant is placed near the first.
And you continue to do this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you return it and sew the wound, for those heads remain adhering in the intestine until the intestine heals and recovers, and no harm whatsoever befalls the patient. And it is also possible to sew the intestine with fine thread.
Which is drawn from the intestine of an animal adhering to it after it is inserted into a needle; and that is that the end of this thread is taken from the intestine, then it is smoothed, then a thin twisted linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is inserted into the needle. And in it is the intestinal thread, and with it the intestine is sewn, then it is returned to the abdomen. And this type of suturing with ants and intestine is only a matter of hope and expectation.
But if the perforation is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, then there is no remedy and no healing at all.
Chapter eighty-six.
In the treatment of catarrh and fistula.
Know that every wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and prolonged and becomes an ulcer and does not heal, and constantly discharges pus that does not stop, is generally called, in whatever member it may be, a fistula; and we call it a catarrh, and a fistula.
It is in reality a hardened, matted, white mass, with no pain associated with it; it has a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some have called it a feather; and it is sometimes moist, constantly discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture may cease; and this moisture may be abundant or scanty, thick or thin.
And I have already mentioned in the classification, when describing wounds, that every ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow, is so because of one of nine reasons; one of these is either because of a deficiency of blood in the body, or because of its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its edges, preventing the growth of good flesh. Or because it is very painful and dirty; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a corruption that has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a peculiarity of the region.
Or because it is very painful and dirty; or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter it discharges is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because of a corruption that has occurred in the region, of the nature of an epidemic; or because of a peculiarity of the region. As was evident in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is naturally slow; or because there is one or several bones in it. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the classification.
It is necessary for me here to inform you of the treatment of fistulas and abscesses which are treated by hand surgery. Know that fistulas may occur in all parts of the body; some of them reach large veins and arteries, or a nerve.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the composite joints, such as the hand and foot; and some lead to the vicinity of a principal organ, and so on.
Then, those fistulas of this kind are among the difficult-to-cure diseases which do not accept treatment, and working on them is laborious and ignorant.  If there are fistulas which have not reached these places which I have mentioned, I hope and trust in their treatment.
And if you wish to arrive at the knowledge of that, take a probe of copper or iron. If the fistula is straight, examine it with it. If the fistula is tortuous, examine it with a probe of thin lead, for lead is soft in its body.
It will pass easily upon entering and will bend towards the tortuosity. If the fistula has many openings and you cannot discover them with the probe, inject one of its openings; then the moisture which you inject will pass to the other openings and will flow out.
Then thoroughly investigate in any way you can to find out if there is a bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a single fistula with many openings. Ascertain all this to the extent of your ability by questioning the patient and by his experience of pain when you touch the place.
And similar indications and causes of the swelling. When you have ascertained all this accurately, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  That is, you should look to see if
the fistula is apparent and near, or in a safe place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the places I mentioned to you, then incise the fistula as I previously described and remove what is in it of the clot, the putrefied flesh, and the excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its opening of flesh or wart, and so on, and treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep-seated and straight, you should incise it deeply as much as you can, then cleanse it of all its putrefied flesh.
Then use a pledget soaked in strong medicines and insert it to the bottom of the fistula which you can reach with an instrument. Do this repeatedly until the strong medicine eats away all that remains of corruption in the bottom of the fistula.
Then dress it with ointments that produce healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal by this, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is confirmed, then investigate and incise it as I have told you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
a sinew, nerve, or main organ, as we said. If the bone is exposed and there is some corruption and blackness in it, debride it until its corruption is gone, then treat it with what will heal it until it is cured. If it does not heal and pus continues as before,
know that you have not completely removed all the corruption.  Expose everything again and thoroughly debride and cleanse it to the best of your ability, then dress it. If it heals, well and good; otherwise, know that the corruption is in a deep recess. Expose everything again.
and thoroughly debride and cleanse it to the best of your ability. If the corrupted bone is small and you can remove it, then extract it with fine forceps suitable for this.
If there are many bones, thoroughly extract them all and do not leave any behind to the best of your ability. If something from the ligaments obstructs you, and there is no danger in cutting them, then cut those ligaments and flesh, if present, and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones at once, apply something to the wound that will cause it to rot, and leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones rot.  Keep the wound from closing the incision you made during your work.
Rather, if you fear that, place on it cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For when it has caused those ligaments to rot and the bones to become brittle, it will be easy to extract and remove them.
If it is a single large bone, such as a bone of the leg or thigh, and similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then debride it thoroughly until that blackness and corruption disappears, then dress the wound. If a large part of it is corrupted and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be sawn.
And cut it all away to where the corruption ends. Then treat it until it heals. And I will tell you about a case of gangrene that occurred in a man's leg, to serve as an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a cause within the body that spread to the leg, causing great swelling; there was no external cause. Then time passed, along with the mistakes of the physicians, until the swelling opened.
And much material flowed from it, and he was treated poorly, until the leg became gangrenous and many openings appeared in it, all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A number of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skillful in the craft of surgery.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg and the material flowing from those openings in great quantity, and the man's body had become thin and his color yellow.  Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings; the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
And I found that some led to others from all sides of the leg. So I began by cutting open one of those openings until I uncovered some of the bone. I found it corrupted, eaten away, blackened, putrid, and perforated, so that the perforation reached the marrow.  Then I spread open what was revealed to me.
And I removed the corrupted bone. I thought that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I had cut and spread open, and that I had excised it. Then I began to dress the wound with healing medicines for a longer time, but it did not heal. Then I returned and uncovered the bone again, beyond the first uncovering.
And I found the corruption connected to the bone.  So I spread open what appeared to me also of that corruption. Then I attempted to dress it, but it did not heal nor unite. Then I uncovered it again, and I continued to cut away the bone piece by piece and attempt to heal it, but it would not heal, until I had cut away about a span of bone.
And I removed it with its marrow. Then I dressed it with medicines, and it healed quickly and completely. This repetition in the operation and its incision was necessary because of the weakness of the patient and his inability to endure, and my fear for his death, because he suffered at all times from excessive vomiting and a serious faint.  Then he recovered completely.
And in the place of the bone, firm flesh grew, and his condition in his body improved, and his strength returned, and he managed his affairs, and in walking he suffered no harm whatsoever. If a protruding bone in any part of the body is broken
Then it should be spread open in this manner: you take a ligature and tie it around the end of the protruding bone, and you instruct someone to pull it upwards, and make another ligature of thicker wool than the first ligature.
Then tie it to the flesh under the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, and you expose the flesh from the area you want to spread open, lest the saw injure the flesh, and place a piece of wood or a board firmly under the bone from below.
Because if you do that, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupt bone.  The sawing should be slightly above the area of corruption, lest there be corruption in the cavity of the bone which does not appear on its surface to the senses, thus forcing you to saw it again.
If the bone is corrupt and not protruding, but its parts are connected to each other, and the corruption is in its middle or in some part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides completely, then place the wood underneath, then saw it from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the corruption, as we have said. If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupt joint itself and saw the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy other than excision. If the corruption is in the metacarpus of the hand or foot, the matter of its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel away the corruption however it appears to you, and excise it and clean it in any way you can.
And by whatever method it is straightened for you, provided that a vein or nerve does not obstruct you. And know that the instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, their arrangement, their thickness and thinness, their size and smallness, their hardness and porosity. Therefore, you should prepare for each type of work an instrument similar to that work.
Know that the works themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument you need, if you have long experience and knowledge of the arts of this profession, for it is from the mastery of the craft and witnessing many types of diseases. He may devise for himself instruments similar to those for each disease, and I have depicted for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.
Making them examples to be imitated and a measure by which you may measure others, God willing. Image of a saw:

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the amputation of limbs and the sawing of bones.
Limbs may become putrefied, either from an external cause or from an internal cause. If you treat this corruption with medicinal treatment and the treatment is not effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the limb, nothing hindering it,
then it is necessary to amputate that member to where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or from a great calamity which is greater than the loss of the member.  A sign of this occurring in someone is that the member turns black, until you would think that fire had burned it.
Or it may fester after turning black, until that festering spreads to what is next to that member and attacks the whole body; then hasten to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some venomous creature, such as a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a tarantula, and the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, amputate the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.  Likewise, if it has taken hold in the hand, amputate it from the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread until it takes hold in the forearm bones.
And likewise, if it takes hold in the arm, amputate the arm at the elbow in the joint itself.  If the corruption progresses and you see it taking hold toward the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder at all, for in that is the death of the patient; use other treatments to the best of your ability.
Do the same with the leg: if the corruption takes hold in a toe, amputate the toe at one of the joints; if it takes hold in the metatarsus, amputate the whole foot; if it ascends to the knee, amputate the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no recourse but to abandon it and leave the patient to death. The method of amputating or dissecting a member is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut off, and tie another ligature above the place.
One assistant pulls the lower ligature downwards, and another assistant pulls the upper ligature upwards; you yourself dissect the flesh between the ligatures with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or dissect. You should place linen cloths on all sides.
Lest the saw touch the healthy tissue, causing the patient additional pain and hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, staunch the place quickly or apply some styptic powder, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will tell you about an example of a man who experienced this very condition in his leg; a blackness appeared in his leg, accompanied by burning like fire.
This corruption first appeared in his toe, until it consumed the whole leg.  The man, seeing the corruption spreading through his limb and the intense pain and burning, of his own accord amputated it at the joint and recovered.
After a long time, the same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand. He came to me, and I tried to stop the corruption with the medications I applied to his hand, after cleansing his body. But the corruption did not stop and continued to spread into the next finger.
Until the corruption consumed the hand, and he asked me to amputate it. I refused, hoping to stop the corruption, and also fearing death for him if I amputated his hand, for the man was weak.  When he despaired of me, he returned to his town.
Then word reached me that he had amputated his entire hand and recovered. I have recounted this story to help with similar diseases and to serve as a guide to be followed and acted upon.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On the treatment of fistulas and how to inject them with medication.
If a swelling occurs in one of the fleshy organs, and the duration of the swelling is prolonged until it gathers pus, then bursts or is lanced, and all the pus within it comes out, leaving the place empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a thin rag, not excessively decayed so as to affect bone, nerve or ligament.  Therefore it deserves to be called a fistula, and not a sinus, unless its duration is prolonged until the decay affects any of these organs.
Then it is called a sinus or fistula, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin as it extends, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the place because of its decay.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere, and the decay has not reached that point, and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. This is to look at the fistula; if it is large and the pus that flows from it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and verdigris, equal parts, gathered in a vessel and cooked on the fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey.  Then take what you need, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the fistula, then bind its opening and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then remove it by squeezing. Do this for several days until the fistula is cleansed and the foul smell is gone.  You may inject it with ash water if this medicine is not available to you. This is to take the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood and pour water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the fistula has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, inject it with water and honey in which some powdered verdigris has been mixed.
Or inject it with honey and wine mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, and wine closes the fistula, especially if the wine has a binding and drying quality.  When you have done this several times and are certain that the fistulas decay is gone, then inject it with something that will cause flesh to grow there.
Such as taking some palm ointment and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent wine, or injecting it with some other medicines and ointments whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the fistula is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to pass,
widen it slightly with an iron instrument or place in it a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or theriac ointment until it widens.  Similarly, if the opening is too wide, bring its edges together with sutures, leaving only enough space for the injection tube to enter.
Similarly, if the opening from which the pus flows is high up, make an incision in the lowest part so that the pus may flow downwards, for if pus collects in the depths of the fistula, it prevents the growth of flesh there.
If you cannot incise the fistula downwards as desired, then position the member so that the pus flows easily, as you see fit, so that nothing of the pus remains retained within.
As for the cohesive medicines which are applied externally to the fistula, and the method of their application: take some of the cohesive ointments similar to what you desire, and spread it on a linen cloth, the cloth being large enough to cover the whole fistula. Then, with scissors, cut a hole opposite the mouth of the fistula, slightly larger than the mouth of the fistula. Then spread some soft ointment on another cloth, to the size of the hole, and place it there, spaced away from the mouth of the fistula so that the pus may easily escape.  Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
several days; but that on the mouth of the fistula, you remove at all times, so that what has collected there of pus may come out, and so you may know what flows from it of pus, whether it is much or little, healthy or unhealthy.
Along with this also, examine the fistula itself: does its owner feel pain in it, or not, and is there swelling in it, or not? If your treatment continues in this way, and you see that the material which comes out is slight at the mouth of the fistula...
know that the fistula has adhered or is near to adhering. Then, resolve it on the morrow or on the third day. And when you have opened the wound, replace the cloths with others and renew the ointment. If, after a long time, thin pus comes out from the fistula, do not despair of its healing.
Its healing is near, for some people are slow in the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others are the opposite. If, after many days, unhealthy pus comes out from the fistula, know that the fistula has not healed. If the healing of the fistula is slow
and its matter is prolonged, increase the drying medicines, and let them be medicines which are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, like palm ointment if an excess of litharge has been added to it. And among the most effective things to treat it with is that palm ointment be taken
and it is dissolved with rose oil and sprinkled with old wine, moderate in consistency, then you knead it with it and use it.  He may do something similar if you do not have palm ointment: honey, if you cook it until it thickens and use it, or take myrrh, aloes, and mastic, crush them all together, and sprinkle them on the honey while it is on the fire. Then you apply it on a cloth and bind it on the fistula, or it is applied with the honey which you have cooked until it thickens, and you sprinkle the medicines on it and place the cloth on it and bind it.
And long rhubarb, the root of the asphodel, the powder of  cistus, and centaury may be used in such a case, singly or mixed together; crush and sift them and sprinkle them on the honey that was described, and use it. If the matter of the fistula is prolonged and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And if it has affected the bones or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it like a fistula, as previously described.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
On the treatment of the felon, the diseased nail, the excision of the excess of a finger, and the separation of adherent fingers.
A felon is an excessive amount of flesh which grows under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot, and it may grow in the other fingers. If its matter is prolonged and its treatment neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it becomes putrid and foul until it eats the root of the nail, and perhaps it destroys it entirely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul smell, and the tip of the finger becomes broad and its color livid.  If you treat it with what we mentioned in the classification and your treatment is not effective,
it is necessary to cut away with an instrument all the excess which grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterwards, for cauterization is very beneficial in this.  And as for if the bone is sound and the nail also sound,
and the corner containing the nail has constricted the flesh inward and is causing it to ulcerate and injure it, it is necessary to place a thin pledget under the corner of the nail which is ulcerating the flesh and raise it upwards and cut that flesh gently.
And you should apply to what remains of the flesh of the cauterizing escharotics until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals. And as for if the corruption has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut that bone and remove it, for it will not heal at all as long as there is a corrupted bone in it.
If you see the erosion and corruption spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of the phalanges as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or bruise and severe pain occurs in it, it is necessary to bleed the patient first.
Then incise the nail with a sharp scalpel, an oblique incision from above to below, and be careful not to reach with the incision to the flesh beneath the nail, for by that you will cause the patient severe pain and it will be a cause for the growth of excess flesh in the place, then treat the area with that which soothes pain.
As for the supernumerary finger which is produced in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, and it may have bones in some of it, and it may have a nail, and the growth of some of them is at the root of the joint of some of the fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the phalanges of the finger. Those which grow in some of the phalanges of the finger do not move, and those which grow at the joint of the finger may move.  As for that which is only fleshy, its cutting is easy, and that is to cut it at its origin with a wide scalpel.
As for that whose growth is at the root of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so avoid cutting it. As for that which grows in the finger at one of the phalanges, it is necessary to first cut its flesh in a circular cut to the bone, then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion which occurs to the fingers, some to others, this often occurs, and it is from that with which a person is born, and it is at the healing of a wound, or a burn of fire, and the like. Then it is necessary to incise that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural form, then place between them a pledget or a piece of cloth soaked in rose oil, lest it adhere quickly, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until the place heals as it should.
Similarly, if adhesion occurs to some of the fingers with the palm, incise that adhesion according to what is suitable and improves the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the excision of polyps.
Polyps are twisted, thick veins filled with dark, stagnant blood, which occur in most parts of the body, most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of those who carry burdens and those who work. First, it is necessary to rub the body with black hellebore several times with a strong rubbing.
Then, bleed the patient in the basilica vein. As for its treatment with the iron, it is of two kinds: one is to incise and remove the black blood, and the other is to extract the vein and remove it entirely. As for its incision, it is in this manner:  first bathe the leg well with warm water.
until the thick, turbid blood is dissolved. Then, tie the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a bandage, then incise the vein in one place, or in two places, or in three, with a wide incision.
Then, draw out the black blood with your hand from below the leg upwards, and from above downwards, until you remove the amount of blood that you see as sufficient or what the patient's strength can bear. Then bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that generate black bile, and repeat the purging and bleeding.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient. As for its extraction, it is done in this manner: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then take him to the bathhouse and bathe his leg with hot water until it becomes red and sweats, or he performs strenuous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available, until the limb is warmed, then incise the skin opposite the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or at its bottom near the heel. Then open the skin with hooks and dissect the vein from every side until it is perceptible to the touch.
And when it appears, you will see it as dark red.  When it is freed from the skin, you will also see it as if it were a tendon. Then insert a probe under it, and when it is raised and emerges from the skin, suspend it with a smooth, blunt hook.  Then make another incision near that incision, about three fingers wide.
Then dissect the skin from over the vein until it appears, then raise it with the probe as you did before and suspend it with another hook as you did initially. Then make another incision, or many incisions if necessary.
Then extract it and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull and extract it until it emerges from the second incision. Then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, above all the incisions, until when it is all extracted, cut it.
If it does not yield to pulling and extraction, then insert a needle with strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull it. Insert the probe underneath and twist it with your hand to every side until it emerges.  Take care that it does not break, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to extract it.
And harm will befall the patient from it.  When you have extracted it all, place wool soaked in a potion and rose oil or olive oil on the wounds and treat it until it heals. If the varix is constricted, with convolutions and torsion in various directions,
and is not straight as we said, then it is necessary to make an incision on it at each convolution and place of torsion, then suspend it with hooks until you extract it completely.  Take care when incising it that you do not cut or injure the vein, for it will make its extraction difficult for you; therefore, exert yourself carefully.
Image of the incision made in the varix:
Image of the blind hook:
It should not be hooked like other hooks, nor should it have a sharp point lest it injure the vein. It should be thick, smoothly curved, for if it is thin it will cut the vein with its thinness; rather, it should have a greater thickness, as we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
On the removal of a varicose vein.
This vein develops in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, barren countries with little fertility.  It may also develop in other parts of the body besides the legs, and its development is due to an infection that occurs under the skin.
Just as living creatures and worms occur inside the body, and gourd and worms develop between the skin and flesh, the sign of the beginning of this vein is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the place becomes swollen, and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that swelling.
It is as if it were the root of a plant or animal; when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it.
