If it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the remainder; do not cut it from its root before it all comes out, for if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing swelling, infection in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it should be treated carefully and pulled out little by little until it is all out and nothing remains of it in the body.  From this vein, in some people, there may emerge something that is five or ten cubits in length.
It has been reported to me that it came out of a man to the length of twenty cubits. If it breaks off while you are treating it, insert a probe into its hole and press it firmly against the body until all its matter is emptied. Then treat the place with medicines for several days.
Then treat it as you would treat a tumor. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, and it may have many openings from each of which a branch emerges. Treat it as we have mentioned in the classification and as previously described.
Chapter ninety-two.
Concerning the incision for the worm that develops under the armpit and is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries the cow's disease because it frequently affects cows. It is a single small worm that develops between the skin and the flesh and moves throughout the body, ascending and descending.
Its movement from one member to another is perceptible to the touch until it pierces the skin at some point and emerges.  It is formed from the corruption of some humors, as is the case with worms, snakes, and the gourd-like growth in the abdomen.
Its harm is expected to be that if it moves in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and emerge, destroying the eye. This happens frequently. If you wish to treat it and remove it, this should be done when it moves and becomes perceptible to the touch.
It is necessary to firmly tie above and below it with a strong ligature, then incise over it and remove it. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, cauterize the area with fire until you burn it. The greatest harm it is expected to cause is to the eye, as we have said.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, tie firmly below it on the forehead, then incise over it and remove it. The patient should be treated by purifying his body with purgative medicines for bad, corrupt humors, and should avoid foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
Concerning the incision for the disease known as the gnawing ulcer.
This disease, which in our country is called *nafir*, is a pain that appears in some members and then moves from one member to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in some of the deserts. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in a vein of the forearm.  After a while I saw that swelling move with the ulna, like a worm, ascending to her shoulder as quickly as possible, like mercury.
When it moved from one place to another, the pain disappeared from that place and settled in the shoulder. Then I waited an hour, and it ran throughout the body until it was in the other arm.  Then she told me that it went around her whole body, as I witnessed.
So I marveled at the speed of its movement from one member to another.  I had never before seen this disease with my own eyes in this way, except that I had seen a number of people whose pain moved from one member to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
And I judged that this was only because the woman was from the desert people, dry of body and with visible veins. Therefore, that moving wind appeared to the senses, and it should not appear in this way in people of comfort and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and its sufferer feels pain, and if it appears to you visibly as we have mentioned, then bind above and below it with a bandage and incise it until that congested wind emerges and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it by cleansing the body and with things that expel and disperse winds, such as the seeds of rue and the seeds of *skibin* and similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows may differ according to their types and according to the places in the body where they are located. As for their differences according to their types, there are large ones and small ones, hollow glass ones and solid glass ones, and some have three points and some have four points.
Some have barbs and some have splinters. As for those according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two kinds: either the organs are hollow principal organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow has entered one of these organs and you see the signs of death which I will describe to you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from it, for in most cases death will overtake its owner. And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, then there will result from that a severe headache, drowsiness and dizziness.
And if these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow has not disappeared into the depth of the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And of the signs of the brain, if an arrow strikes it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane covering the brain, then there will result from that a severe headache, drowsiness and dizziness. And redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting, and perhaps blood will come from the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech will cease and the voice will be lost, and a white moisture resembling gruel will come from the place of the wound, and watery fluid from it will flow.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and from extracting the arrow if it has not been extracted. As for the signs of the arrow if it strikes the heart and is near the left breast, and one feels it as if it has been driven into something solid and not into something empty,
and perhaps the arrow will have a movement resembling the beat of the pulse, and black blood will flow from the wound, and this will be followed by coldness of the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting; then know that death is inevitably approaching. And a sign of the arrow if it wounds the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels near the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily and seeks to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it will be near the smaller ribs, and the breathing will be great with severe pain.
And he will sigh and all the members of the shoulders will move. And if the arrow strikes the liver, severe pain will follow, and blood resembling the liver in its redness will come from the wound. And if the arrow strikes the stomach, perhaps something undigested from the food will come from the wound.
And its condition is apparent. And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and is embedded in it, and something of the feces, or fat, or intestine which has been perforated comes from the wound, then there is no hope of treatment or of extracting the arrow. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine comes out,
and something protrudes from it to the outside and the pain intensifies for the patient, then know that he is doomed. As for the other members, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar members, they are more often saved.
When the arrow does not encounter an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned. And I tell you of some of what I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may thereby be guided in your treatment. And that is that an arrow once struck a man in the corner of his eye at the root of the nose.
Then I extracted it for him from the other side, under the lobe of the ear, and he recovered, and no harm befell his eye. And I extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the lobe of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had hidden itself, and I grasped only its small tip which is attached to the wood.
And it was a large arrow from the composite bows, square-headed, smooth, without barbs.  The Jew recovered and no ill event occurred in his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and the arrow was Arabic.
And it was one with barbs; so I made an incision for him between the jugular veins, and it had sunk into his throat, so I gently manipulated it until I extracted it. The Christian was saved and recovered.  And I extracted another arrow for a man which had struck him in the abdomen, and we judged that he would die from it.
But when thirty days or so had passed, and nothing in his condition had changed, I made an incision over the arrow, and I manipulated it and extracted it, and he recovered, and no ill event befell him. And I saw a man whom an arrow had struck in his back, and the wound had closed over it.
Then after seven years the arrow emerged at the root of her thigh. And I saw a woman who had been struck in her abdomen by an arrow, and the wound had closed, and the arrow remained. Nothing in her condition changed, nor did she find any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man whom an arrow had struck in the face, and the wound had closed, and he felt little pain from it. And there are many like this. And I extracted an arrow for a man, one of the Sultan's guards, which had struck him in the middle of his nose, leaning slightly to the right.
And the entire arrow was hidden. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I explored it with a thin probe, but I did not feel it. And he felt a stinging pain under his ear, on the right side.
I hoped that this stinging pain was from the tip of the arrow, so I applied a dressing with drawing and ripening power, hoping that the area would swell and the arrow's location would be revealed so that I could incise it. But nothing happened in the area to indicate that the arrow had reached that point.
So I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  Then, during that time, the wound healed, and the patient despaired of its removal for a time, until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me. I then applied a strong caustic medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, and I felt the thin tip of the arrow which adheres to the wood. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared to us visibly.  And it had been with him for about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to removal.  I continued to gently manipulate it and devise methods with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with forceps, as its image is shown at the end of the chapter.
And the physicians judged that the cartilage of his nose would not heal, but I healed it, and the wound closed, and the patient recovered completely without any further harm. And I tell you the method of removing some arrows so that you may make that a measure and example for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skillful craftsman judges by the little from the much, and by what is present from what is absent, and deduces a new operation and a new instrument in strange emergencies.
If we descend to this craft, then I say that arrows are removed from the members in which they are fixed in two ways: either by pulling from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction, and those that are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in a fleshy place, so it is pulled and removed.  If it does not yield to removal from the place where it entered, then it is necessary for you to leave it for several days until the flesh around it putrefies, so that its extraction becomes easier; and so it is if it is embedded in bone.
And if it does not yield to removal, then leave it also for several days and return to pulling and moving it each day, for it will come out. If it does not yield to removal after several days, then you should pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill until you widen the space for the arrow, then pull it out and remove it.
If the arrow embedded in the bone of the head has penetrated deeply into one of the ventricles of the brain and some of those symptoms which I have mentioned to you appear in the patient, then desist from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after several days.
then persevere in pulling out the arrow; and if it is very firmly embedded and does not yield to pulling, then use the drills around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the wound until it heals.  And as for if the arrow has disappeared into a place in the body, and is hidden from sensation, then probe for it with a probe; and if you feel it, then pull it out with some of the instruments suitable for pulling it out; and if you cannot reach it because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow, and there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein there,
then incise it until the wound is widened and you can reach the arrow until you extract it.  If it has barbs, grasp them and free the flesh entangled in them from every side by every method you can; and persevere, if you are unable to free the flesh, in breaking the barbs and twisting them until you are freed.
And when you attempt to extract the arrow in whatever place it may be, use the twisting of your hand with forceps in all directions until you free it, and use the utmost gentleness lest the arrow break and it becomes difficult for you to pull it out and extract it; and if you cannot manage it at your first attempt,
leave it for some days until the flesh around it putrefies, then try again; for it will then be easier. And if bleeding interferes with you, use what we have mentioned of treatment in its chapter; and guard carefully against cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon, and use ingenuity.
In every way you can free the arrow, and let it be gently and slowly and steadily as I have described to you; and it is necessary for you, when pulling out the arrow, to place the patient in the same position he was in when the arrow entered, for this is more suitable.
If you cannot do this, use whatever position you can; but as for the arrow which emerges from the opposite side, either something of it will have emerged outwards, or you will find the tip of the arrow by touch just under the skin, and see it protruding.
Then make an incision upon it, and let the incision be large enough to admit the forceps, then pull it out, for it will easily come out.  If it sticks in a bone, twist your hand round and round until the arrow affects the bone and makes room for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for some days, then try again until it comes out. If the arrowhead is in it, push it with it. If the arrowhead has fallen off and you wish to use pushing, then insert the hollow instrument so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters that hollow, for the arrow will easily come out in this way.  If the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary to cut away all the flesh in which the poison has spread, if you are able to do so, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow which has fallen into the chest, or into the belly, or into the bladder, or into the flank is near what can be felt with a probe, and you are able to make an incision upon it, then make an incision and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and take it out.
Then stitch the wound if it needs stitching, then treat it until it heals.  A picture of the forceps with which arrows are pulled out:
Their ends are like the beak of a bird; they are made like a file, so that when they grasp an arrow or anything, they do not let go.  And various kinds are made, large, small, and medium-sized, all according to the size of the arrow and the width or narrowness of the wound.
A picture of the hollowed-out pusher:
A picture of the solid pusher:
This has a solid end like an awl, to make it easier to enter the hollowed-out arrow and push it.
Chapter ninety-five.
On the incision of veins.
The veins which it is customary to bleed in the body are thirty in number; of these, sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the whispering veins, and the two arteries in the temples which are visible, and the two veins at the inner canthus of the eyes known as the seeing veins.
And the vein erect in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lipthese veins are known as the veins of the jawand the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins which are bled in the arm and hand, they are five veins: one is the cephalic, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head; and the median cubital, which is the middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the basilic, which is situated on the inner side, and it is also called the axillary, and the common people call it the vein of the belly; and the vein of the forearm, which is situated on the ulna, and to which it adheres, and which appears very clearly above the thumb; and the azygos.
And this is the vein which is between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one is that which is under the popliteal space on the outer side; and the second is the saphenous, and its place is at the heel on the inner side; and the vein of the sciatic nerve, and its place is at the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to them. As for the two veins which are behind the ears, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic colds, migraine, ringworm, and chronic bad sores of the head, and the manner of bleeding them is as I describe.
And that is to shave the head of the patient and rub his scalp in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well; then the patients neck is constricted with a turban until the two veins and their position appear behind the ears in the two low places of the head; then you search for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulsation under your finger, there you mark with ink.
Then you take a lancet, which is what is known as the *nashshal*, then you insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone; then you raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin, a severed cut, and the length of the cut is the amount of two closed fingers.
Or thereabouts, and you let out the amount of blood you want, then you bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  And it was mentioned earlier in the book, their cutting and cauterization. As for the two arteries which are in the temples, the benefit of bleeding them is for chronic migraine, severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and sharp, profuse discharges into the eyes.
And the manner of bleeding them is as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the veins appear clearly to the touch, and their pulsation is apparent under your finger; then you mark with ink. Then you raise the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
And you insert the lancet, the *nashshal*, from below and raise the vein upwards and sever it as you did with the other two veins, and you let out blood according to your need; then you loosen the constriction of the patients neck and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and bind it tightly from above and leave it until it heals. And their mention, cutting, and cauterization were mentioned earlier in the book.  As for bleeding the vein of the forehead, its benefit, after bleeding the cephalic vein, is for chronic facial ailments such as ringworm, ulcers, and erysipelas.
And the manner of bleeding it, as I describe to you: the patient constricts his neck with a turban until the vein appears; then you take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image:
You place the projecting spike which is in the head of the axe on the vein itself and strike from above with a comb or something similar, and you let the blood flow to the extent you desire; then you loosen the constriction of the patients neck and bind it until it heals.
And it may be opened with a wide lancet, but it should not be sharp-pointed like other lancets, but rather slightly wide-ended, and you bleed it obliquely, because the bone is near, and the lancet may break in it if it is thin.
As for the two veins which are at the temples, their benefit is for eye ailments such as ringworm, redness, and blepharitis, and facial diseases. As for the manner of bleeding them, it is that the patient constricts his neck with a turban, then you bleed them while you are standing over his head, and let the bleeding be slightly obliquely lengthwise with a small, slightly wide lancet.
For the place has no flesh in it; if the lancet is thin-pointed, it may break. Then you let out the amount of blood you need and place cotton on them and bind them for one night, then unbind them. As for bleeding the vein of the nose, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
And severe headache and facial diseases like erysipelas which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic.  As for the manner of bleeding it, the patient constricts his neck, then you grasp his nose with your left hand and take a thin, long lancet.
And you insert it into the middle of the nasal bone itself between the nasal septum in a straight line, for the vein does not appear to the touch there; then the blood will emerge immediately. It is necessary to move your hand with the lancet a little and let out the amount of blood you need, then bind it for a night, for it clots quickly.
As for the jugular veins, the benefit of bleeding them is for shortness of breath, the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the surface of the skin, such as black vitiligo, impetigo, bad ulcers, and sores. As for the manner of bleeding them, the patient constricts under them in his neck with a bandage, and the surgeon stands at the head of the patient.
And the patient sits on a chair, then you bleed the vein lengthwise with a somewhat wide incision, then you let out a moderate amount of blood, or according to what you see is needed. Then you do likewise with the other vein, then you untie the bandage.
And you constrict the two veins moderately, lest the patient suffocate, and you leave him until the next day, for the wound will heal. As for the veins of the temples, the benefit of bleeding them after bleeding the cephalic vein is that it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, cracking of the lips, and bad ulcers.
which are in and around the nose.  The manner of bleeding them is that you seat the patient before you and constrict his neck with a turban, then you part his lips and look at the two veins, one of which you see to the right of the lip and the other to the left, and you recognize them by their blackness.
And that is also because around them are small, black veins; so cut them with a cut that severs them. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, then aim to cut the largest and most apparent ones.  Likewise, do this for the two veins which are in the lower lip.
As for the two veins which are under the tongue, the benefit of bleeding them after bleeding the cephalic vein is for the inflammations which are in the throat from disease of the uvula and diseases of the mouth. The manner of bleeding them is that you seat the patient between your hands, facing the sun, and you raise his tongue and look under the tongue, on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, and their color is toward blackness.
Then you bleed them, and be careful not to go too far in cutting them, for beneath them are arteries, and perhaps there may result bleeding of blood from those arteries. As for the three veins which are bled in the elbow, these are those which customarily are bled in all people, and their bleeding is done in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, rounded or olive-shaped lancet to the depth, or by incising with a scalpel, which is the <i>nashal</i> [and this is its image], image of the wide, rounded lancet:
It is wide as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins which contain thick, turbid blood. And this is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
And this lancet is less wide and thinner at the tip, suitable for bleeding thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is the image of the <i>nashal</i> lancet:
This is the <i>nashal</i> which is suitable for incision, and there are types of it, wide and narrow, according to the width and narrowness of the veins also. And by this one may infer others, and it is well-known among the craftsmen.
And as for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from what is near the chest and abdomen.  And it is necessary for the phlebotomist, when bleeding it, to beware of it and be careful with it, for beneath it is an artery. If he makes a mistake and goes too deep with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
Then bleeding will occur, therefore it is necessary that its bleeding should not be with the lancet for puncturing, but its bleeding should be by incision with the <i>nashal</i>. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to avoid it and turn to another, or seek one of its branches.
Or bleed in its place the vein of the arm, for it is clear, and incise it with the <i>nashal</i> lancet as we said. If you wish to bleed it itself, then before tightening the arm it is necessary to feel the place until you recognize the place of the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then you tie the arm and incise the vein with a sharp incision with the *nashal* lancet as we said, and take care that the incision is far from the place of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, swelling in the place which you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood, upon bleeding, spurt like the urine of a boy, and the blood is thin and red, then know that it is from the artery.  Then immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, and often it will stop, then tighten the arm.
And leave it, and warn the patient against neglecting it, and let him be on bed rest and not move for several days until it heals. If the blood does not stop and it overcomes you and you do not have medicine at hand, then amputate the artery if it appears to you, for its ends will contract and the blood will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell and split it and take one half and tie it tightly on the place of the vein with a ligature and bandages until the next day. If the blood stops, otherwise treat it with what was mentioned before of placing styptics, and stopping its blood is not difficult in most cases.
Because of the smallness of the wound and the tightness of the ligature in the arm, know this. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
And it is necessary for the one who bleeds it to be careful when bleeding it, for under it is a nerve. If he increases the insertion of the lancet and injures the nerve, numbness will occur in it which is difficult to cure, and perhaps it will not heal at all. And this nerve often appears to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is thin and cannot be distinguished, then you should make your incision in it lengthwise and avoid the nerve as much as you can. If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of bleeding it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for diseases of the eyes, and it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish to bleed it, to make the incision with the olive lancet or with the broad, basil-shaped lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery or nerve under it.
However, when bleeding, you should avoid the head of the muscle with the lancet and seek the soft place. It does not harm if it is not struck with the first blow to be bled again several times, except that it may swell in some people if it is not bled with the first blow.
But that swelling does not harm it at all. As for the method of bleeding, its complications, and what should be done to remedy it, the first thing is that you should know that bleeding is used either only to preserve health.
Or it is used in diseases. That which is used to preserve health and maintain it and to guard against the occurrence of diseases is that the bleeding should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely, the cephalic, the basilic, and the median, and that the bleeding should be at the beginning of spring if signs of plethora appear.
And the bleeding should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours of the day have passed. As for the bleeding that is used in diseases, it does not have a limited time, but whenever necessity and need require it, day or night.
And at every hour and at every time. Children should not be bled until they are fourteen years old, nor should the elderly who have passed sixty years be bled. If someone intends to bleed for any reason whatsoever.
He should cleanse his bowels before bleeding with a gentle enema if there is much retained stool, lest putrid impurities be drawn into the veins from the intestines during bleeding, harming the principal organs.  Nor should one bleed the consumptive, the drunkard, or the intoxicated until that has passed from them.
Bleeding should also be avoided after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive coitus, fatigue, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and everything that weakens the strength, whether physical or mental. Then, look to the thinning of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
With food, drink, and medicine if he can do so. Then he should enter the bath unless something prevents him, or he should engage in some exercise so that the blood is thinned, and the bleeding should be done in the middle of the day as we said. He should aim to free his chest that day from all bad psychological conditions.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical conditions such as excessive fatigue and exertion, coitus, and the like. And he should prepare for his session the things which people customarily use, such as various perfumes and scents, entertainment and the like, each person according to his ability.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion higher than the cushion on which the patient sits. Then he extends his arm and the phlebotomist rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the ligature with the bandage and twists it two or three times. Let the tightening be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is towards relaxation it also prevents the flow of blood. Then after the tightening, it is necessary for the patient to rub both his hands together.
Until the veins swell and become perceptible to the touch. Then the phlebotomist wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, then he places the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself below the place which he wants to bleed a little, lest the vein slip and avoid the incision. For some veins you find to be like a tendon, slipping away during phlebotomy, and some are full of wind, so when the lancet is placed on them, they sink beneath the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein, or if it opens it, it is only a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be careful and deliberate in all these matters. Then he lowers the lancet. If the vein opens at that time, otherwise he repeats it again a little below or above that place quickly if the place is not swollen.
If the patient swells or is frightened, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, for it might cause a hot swelling, and do not let him enter the bath. Then he can repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes.  If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the flow of blood is thin,
and you fear that the desired amount of blood will not come out, then return the lancet to the same hole gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly, and do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat it at all, for it will not help you. Put something of the dregs of oil on it, for it facilitates the flow of blood, and it is better in this place than the oil itself and other ointments as well.
Therefore use the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult.  The theriac Pharomach and the shakhzanaja may also do this, if one of them is placed on the place, for the blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the phlebotomyand this often happens, especially to someone who has been bled only once or whose vein opening was smallact quickly and place a sponge soaked in slightly warmed salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour, for it will resolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has completely left the vein itself or another vein. If something black or green remains in the place after days, it does no harm. If you wish, apply something of aloe and bitter substances dissolved.
Or something of the juice of the  foudanj and the like.  Often a swelling and a node occur when bleeding the basil vein; place your hand upon it. If you find it yielding when you prod it, then that is a bad node, and beware of putting on it anything of what we have mentioned.
For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it; but bind it with something astringent to solidify the place, then treat it with the other treatments until it heals. And it is necessary that you let out for each person blood according to his strength and what appears of the color predominant in the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow until it reddens; and likewise, if you see it thick, let it flow until it thins; and likewise if it is acrid, until its acridity departs.  It is necessary for someone who is full-bodied and strong and needs to have blood removed all at once
to widen the vein opening and the lancet to be wide. And for someone who is weak, the opposite of that. And it is necessary that you let it out in stages, and that the opening be narrow. And the best thing to use in opening a vein is something sharp, well-honed, incised, not punctured.
And this type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and from cutting a nerve; and it is better and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise. And whoever is accustomed to fainting during bloodletting, it is necessary that you give him before the bloodletting something of bread soaked in pomegranate juice or oxymel.
If he is feverish and you let blood out for him in three or four times, then if he is cold in temperament, let him take before the bloodletting bread soaked in hydromel or in spiced honey-wine or in pleasantly-scented wine.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and its cause is the exit of much blood, then it is necessary that he be given meat broth and thin, pleasantly-scented wine, and he should use fragrant substances with aloeswood, and rub his chest with it, and use all that we mentioned in the section on fainting.
which comes from vomiting. And as for whoever wants to relieve his arm and let his blood out again, it is necessary for whoever had his bloodletting for much vomiting and whose strength is weak that you let the blood out little by little according to his strength in successive days.
And as for whoever wants to relieve his arm and let his blood out again and his body is strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting. And as for whoever wants to draw the blood from his body to the opposite side to which it inclined, it is necessary that you relieve him on the second or third day.
And as for whoever has much blood in his body which has become hot and sharp and caused a fever, it is necessary that you let blood out from him in one go, and let out from him a large quantity, and widen the opening until fainting appears, after you have checked all the conditions of bloodletting. And that you put your hand on his pulse when the blood flows, lest death occur instead of fainting, for that often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs. And it is not necessary, if you want to open the arm and let the blood out again.
And if the mouth of the vein has closed and the exit of blood is difficult, do not prick it forcefully or twist it strongly, for that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until you bleed it again, or remove with the blade of the lancet what has solidified of the blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply to it something of salt dissolved in water, or apply to it something of theriac faruq or shkhznaya, and prick it delicately until the blood comes out.  If the vein has swollen, then leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If the need requires letting blood again and it is necessary, then either bleed him above that place, or bleed him in the other arm or in another vein.
As for bleeding the arm vein, it is bled instead of the akhl and basillic veins if they are not found or are hidden, because it is composed of them.  Its method of bleeding is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the vein appears clearly, then it is tied above it with a ligature slightly, a moderate tightening.
Then bleed the vein at a slight angle, neither across nor lengthwise, and let the opening be wide, and let your bleeding be a little above the joint of the hand. If the exit of the blood is difficult, return the hand to the vessel with hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until it reaches your need. If you are in the summer days, then you may dispense with returning the hand to the hot water.  The use of hot water to make the blood flow is most often in the winter time, and bleeding this vein is safer than all veins. Because there is no underlying striking vein or nerve. As for bleeding the cephalic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  Its method of bleeding is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no underlying striking vein or nerve. As for bleeding the cephalic vein of the right hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments.  Its method of bleeding is that you tie the wrist with a ligature or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the vein swells. And it becomes very apparent to the touch, then bleed it at a slight angle. And even if you completely cut it, that will not harm him at all. Be careful not to push your hand deeply with the lancet, for beneath it is the nerve of the fingers and the place is bare of flesh. Then return the hand to the hot water.
And leave it to flow. For if you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and be prevented from flowing. When you have removed the amount of blood needed, place fat and salt on the vein lest it close quickly.
And similarly you should do with every narrow branch. As for the benefit of bleeding it from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen.  And you do the same in bleeding it as you did in the second, exactly. As for bleeding the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower part of the body, such as uterine ailments.
And retention of menstruation, and kidney diseases, and chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar diseases.  Its method of bleeding is that he puts his leg in hot water and has it rubbed until the veins swell, then you tie above the joint of the leg with a bandage, and the vein is located at the heel, on the surface, towards the great toe.
And many branches extend from it on the surface of the foot. So bleed it in the widest branch of it, or at the heel where it converges; this is better and safer.  If you bleed it on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves which are underneath on the surface of the foot.
And make your incision in a twisting manner, as if you intend to amputate it, and let the lancet be sharp. If the blood does not come out, then let him return his leg to the hot water and leave the blood to flow until it is emptied. If the phlebotomist misses the vein on the first attempt...
let him return to bleeding a little higher, for the place is safe and there is no fear of harm from it if you are careful of the nerve, as we said. And you do the same with the saphenous vein of the other leg, exactly. As for the sciatic vein, its location is, as we said, at the heel from the lateral side.
And the benefit of bleeding it is for hip pain, if that is from hot blood. And its method of bleeding is that you put the patient in the bath and hasten and tie his leg from the hip to above the heel with four fingers' width of a thin, long bandage, for it will not appear except by that.
Then, if it appears, bleed it in whichever way you are most comfortable, either by twisting, which is better, or by amputating it or cutting it; for its location is safe. And it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it...
and it does not appear at all to the touch, then bleed some of its branches, which are those that appear on the back of the foot toward the little finger and ring finger, and be careful of the nerves, and release the amount of blood that you want.
Then untie the bandage and place cotton on the place of bleeding and bind the place, for it will quickly heal.
Chapter ninety-six.
Concerning cupping and its method of use.
Cupping instruments may be made of horns, wood, brass, or glass, and cupping is of two types: one is cupping with incision and the extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is of two types.
And the cupping of the sides of the neck on both sides, and the cupping of the chin, which is below the lower jaw of the mouth, and the cupping of the shoulders, and the cupping of the coccyx at the base of the spine, and the cupping of the forearms, which are the middles of the arms, and the cupping of the legs, and the cupping of the heels.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins spread throughout the flesh, and for that reason it does not weaken the body as much as bloodletting, and cupping, whether with fire or without, should not be used in any illness that is caused by fullness until the whole body is purged.
If the need for cupping arises from illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle of it, or at any time.
That is because some people, when their blood increases to the point that it needs to be removed by cupping, feel heaviness and headache in their heads, and some feel fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck, and some feel itching in their face and sides, darkness and pain in their eyes, and some itch in the place of their cupping.
And some laugh a lot, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, their gums swell, and they spit blood, and some sleep a lot, and some see blood, redness, killing, and wounds in their sleep, and things like that.  When we see any of that
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping then after two or three hours of the day have passed. As for the benefit of cupping the temples, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water pouring into the eyes.
But it should be after emptying the whole body. This cupping may be a substitute for phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.  It is warned against using it for someone who is cold-brained or has a cold, for it will harm him greatly.
Therefore, the elderly and those with cold diseases in their heads should not use it, and those who habitually use it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should be ordered to lower his hand with the cupping glass a little lower, for fear of producing forgetfulness. As for cupping the nape of the neck, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic and cephalic veins.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, rupture of the respiratory apparatus, cough, and fullness.  The cupping of the nape of the neck should be raised a little, for if it is lowered, it will produce weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the mastoid processes, it benefits from pains occurring in the head, ophthalmia, migraine, angina, and toothache. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein. The cupper should be ordered not to deepen his hand with the lancet, lest he cut a vein and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it benefits from thrush in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases in the mouth. It may replace phlebotomy of the labial veins.  As for cupping the shoulders, it benefits from palpitations that result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the insides of the forearms, it benefits from what benefits phlebotomy of the three veinsthe basilic, cephalic, and medianbecause it draws blood from all those small veins in the flesh and draws those small veins from other thicker veins, until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be ordered not to go too deep with the lancet, because the area is devoid of flesh and underneath are nerves and arteries. As for the single cupping performed on the coccyx, it benefits from hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
The cupper should be ordered to use a large cupping glass, and it should be made of copper, because the area needs strong suction, and a glass cupping glass might break and require many incisions.  As for cupping the legs, it noticeably reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the whole body, it benefits from chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; it promotes menstruation; it benefits from boils and abscesses; and it replaces phlebotomy of the saphenous veins and the veins of the ankles. However, it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people. The benefit of cupping the ankles is similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping glasses is that the cupping glass is first placed empty and moderate suction is applied. Do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses, but rather apply and remove them quickly so that the humors are drawn to the area evenly. Continue to repeat this until you see that the area has reddened and opened.
When the redness of blood appears, then make an incision and resume suction slowly. Then consider the state of the bodies. For those whose flesh is loose and porous, it should be incised only once, lest the area ulcerate.
Order the cupper to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, and to adjust the suction gently and with delicate movement. If the blood is thick, it should be incised twice; the first time to open a path for the thin, watery blood, and the second time to completely remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, the incision should be repeated a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to remove a small amount of blood, we are content with one incision; if we want to remove a large amount of blood, we make more incisions, especially if we estimate that the blood is thick.
Then it is necessary to make a deep incision; the moderate limit of the incision is only the depth of the skin.  What should be used of ointments when applying cupping glasses, and of waters, and what the cupped person should avoid. As for someone whose skin is thick, hard, dry and whose pores are tight,
the places for the cupping glasses should be anointed with opening, softening, and resolving ointments.  If it is in the summer, then such as rose ointment, violet ointment, sweet almond oil, or pumpkin seed oil.
And if it is in the winter, then such as daffodil ointment, iris ointment, chamomile ointment, or lily and the like. If the waste matter is thick and cold, let the ointment be marjoram ointment, or balm ointment, or benzoin ointment, or storax ointment, and the like.
If the cupped person has wide pores and soft flesh, then the ointment should be avoided. These should wash their cupping places after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with gooseberry water, or with gourd water, or with chickweed water, and the like. As for someone whose blood is very moist,
he should wash his cupping places with vinegar, or with water of spikenard and sumac, and the like. As for someone whose waste matter is thick, he should wash his cupping places with old wine, or with marjoram water, or a decoction of storax, or chamomile, and the like.  Cupping should be avoided in the bathhouse and immediately after the bathhouse.
Rather, it should be used after leaving the bathhouse after an hour or two. No one should sleep after cupping.  What the cupped and bled person should manage before and after cupping.
First, it is necessary to look. If the cupped or bled person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation prevail in his blood, then he should take refrigerants such as pomegranate and dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, oxymel, and sherbet, and the like.  And he should make his foods from poultry and lamb stews, and sour things and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, then he should be given honeyed drinks and quince drinks or seed oxymel, and he should consume moderately fragrant wine, that which is between old and new. He should be ordered to eat little, and his food should be poultry, partridges, sparrows, and pigeon squabs, stewed.  And the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting should be more than the food.
And it may be necessary to give some people, at certain times, some of the antidote of Faruq, or musk medicine, or Theriac, before cupping and before or after bloodletting, to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood.  It should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for cuppings which are without a condition, they are the cuppings which are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, the region of the kidneys, and the hip joint, for these organs do not tolerate a condition being applied to them. Rather, what is intended by them is either drawing blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping glass on the breasts in the case of epistaxis, or we use it to remove cold wind which has become lodged in an organ, such as placing the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel, for it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes the pain by resolving that wind.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if an obstruction or stone appears in them, for by the force of its drawing it may perhaps open the obstruction or dislodge the stone from its place.  And so it acts if placed on the liver and spleen in the case of wind that disturbs them. And these cuppings may be used empty, with only suction.
It may be used with fire, and it may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of inflammation, so that the cupping glass, and let it be large, is filled with hot water alone, or with water in which some herbs suitable for this have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the place, held, and removed, and repeated several times according to need. And this is the image of the cupping glass which is used with fire:
Its mouth should be the width of two open fingers, as we have depicted, and its depth about half a span, and in its side, about halfway up, a small hole, about the size that a needle will enter, made of Chinese copper or yellow copper, with a thick rim.
Smooth, even, and polished, lest it injure the member when placed upon it, and in its middle a transverse groove of copper or iron, where the lighted candle is placed. And this cupping glass may be made larger than we have described, or smaller, according to the illnesses.
And the manner of its use: for the cupping glasses of boys and thin people are not the cupping glasses of men and those with full bodies.  As for the manner of placing this cupping glass with fire on the member, it is that you prepare a lighted wick of tightly-woven linen or a small candle of pitch and place it on the middle of the solidified rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass.
So that the ascent of the fire will be upwards towards the bottom of the cupping glass, lest the body of the patient be burned. Then it is placed on the member, and the finger on the hole which we mentioned, so that when we have held the cupping glass as long as we need, the finger is removed.
And the vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will adhere to the place. Then you prepare the wick in the same way and reapply it if you need to. As for the cupping glass which is used in the treatment of *shousha* with water, it does not have a solidified rod nor a hole.
Rather, it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the member only. And this is its image:
And this cupping glass, the larger it is to contain much water, the better it is.
Chapter ninety-seven.
On the suspension of the leech.
Leeches are used mostly in cases where cupping glasses cannot be applied, either because of smallness, like the lip and gum and the like, or because the member is devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose and the like.
And the method of using them is that you choose leeches which are in pure fresh water, free from impurities, then leave them a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body is first purged by bloodletting or cupping, then the affected member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed upon it.  When it is full, it falls off. And if it is possible to suck the place with a cupping glass, it is more effective; otherwise, wash the place with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and rub and squeeze it. If the bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and it is a trickle, then let a linen cloth be dipped in cold water.
And place it on top until the trickle stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle on it powdered vitriol or gallnut or similar astringents until the bleeding stops, or place on the place halves of peeled lupins.
And leave it until the lupin sticks to the place, for then the bleeding will stop. And it is preferable, if it is necessary to reapply leeches, not to reapply those leeches if others are available. If the leeches refuse to attach, let the place be wiped with fresh blood.
Or a needle is inserted into the place until some blood comes out, then it is placed, for when it senses some blood it will stick in place.  If you want it to fall off, sprinkle on it some aloe, or salt, or ash, then it will fall off in place.
Chapter Three.
On setting (bones).
This chapter also deals with what is needed in the art of medicine, namely the setting of fractures and dislocations occurring in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common people, and by those who have never looked at a book of the ancients on it, nor read a letter of it.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become nonexistent in our country, and I have never encountered anyone at all who was skilled in it. And I have only acquired what I have acquired from my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them, until I extracted knowledge of this from them; then I adhered to experience and practice throughout my life.
And I have drawn for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge encompasses. And my experience has passed over it after I have brought it closer to you and freed it from the branches of prolixity, and shortened it to the utmost, and clarified it to the utmost, and depicted for you in it many images of the instruments which are used in it.
For it is from an increase in explanation, as I did in the preceding two chapters, and there is no strength except in God, the Most High, the Magnificent.
The first chapter.
In it are summaries and compendia concerning the matter of fractured bones, and it is necessary to present them.  And before we begin to mention the fractured and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter summaries of speech and sections. It compels you first to understand them and to stand upon their reality, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble craft.
So I say that when a fracture, dislocation, wound, or fall occurs to someone, it is necessary to hasten first to bloodletting or purging, or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness, or the one to whom this has happened is a child or a very old man, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then he should restrict his diet to cold legumes and the meat of birds and gazelles, and he should avoid drink, strong meats, and overeating, and every food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is safe and the flow of material to the location is not expected.
Then let the patient return to his first regimen, which was his custom.  When the broken bone begins to knit, then the patient should be nourished with foods that provide abundant, strong, and firm nourishment, which has viscosity, such as porridges, rice, heads, necks and cow stomachs. And eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and the like, for by this treatment the consolidation of the fracture will be quicker and better. And know that broken bones, if they are in strong men and old men, cannot ever unite and join in their first nature.
Because of the dryness and hardness of their bones. And what was of bones in the utmost softness, like the bones of small children, may unite and join together; but nature grows on the broken bone from all its sides something resembling glue, thick, which adheres to it and tightens it until some of it adheres to the other.
And it binds some of it to some of it until it comes to the utmost strength and firmness as it was at first, so that nothing hinders him from his actions.
For this reason it is necessary to give the patient food which has firmness, viscosity and thickness, as we said. And know that fractures differ in their kinds according to the difference of the members, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the breast bone is different from a fracture of the back bone.
And so also all the other members, each differing from the other. And we shall come to the mention of each kind of fracture, explained in its chapter, detailed from the others. And the kind of fracture of the bone itself may also differ.
For its fracture may be a splintering without any fragments being produced, and its fracture may be along the length of the bone, and its fracture may have fragments and excrescences, separated and unseparated. And the fracture may be with a wound and perforation in the skin, and the fracture may be a slight crack.
And for each kind there is a special method in its setting, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. And among the things by which a fracture of the bone is recognized are its crookedness, its swelling, its appearance to the touch, and its roughness when you probe it with your hand.
Then when there is neither apparent crookedness nor roughness in the place, nor do you feel any disturbance in the bone when you feel it, and the patient does not feel much pain, then there is no fracture, but it may be a sprain, or a slight fracture, or a slight crack.  Therefore you should not move it at all by pulling and probing.
But apply to it of the medicaments, the mention of which will come after a while, what suits the place, then bind it gently. And know that if the bone is broken and cracked into two without any fragments being produced, except that each part has separated from its fellow.
Then it is necessary for you to hasten at once to set it and straighten it before a hot swelling occurs to it.  If a hot swelling occurs to it, then leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then straighten it in any way you are able with gentleness and skill.
And know that its setting and straightening is easier than a bone in which fragments have occurred. Then it is bound as will be mentioned.  If there are fragments in the bone, then it is necessary to pull the broken limb from both sides, whether it be hand or foot.
Either with your hands, if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. And let your placing of the limb be on a level surface in its natural shape, so that when the broken part of the bone is extended, then replace those fragments in their places in any way you are able with skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause the patient pain or suffering by your actions, and strive with all your effort to join one bone to the other in the best way. It is necessary at that time to touch them and feel them with your hand, and if you see something irregular, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
And beware of strong pulling and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do, and they often cause by their actions a hot swelling or stiffness in the limb, as I have witnessed many times from their actions.
Then, after the setting, perfection, and binding, keep that limb still and quiet, and warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, and when turning and restlessness, and when defecating, and in all his movements as much as possible, and let him be careful that the position of the limb is a position where he will be safe from pain.
And that is because whenever he feels pain or suffering in the position of the limb, he should move it to another position where he does not feel pain, and he should be careful that this position is level and straight.
Lest there be a deformity in the limb when it heals. As for the method of binding the broken limb, it is as I describe to you. Know that broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and their forms. Those that are small, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and the like.
Then the bandages should be soft and gentle; and those that are thick, like the thigh, back, and chest, then the bandages should be wide and firm, because a wide bandage is necessary for a large limb and binds it from every side with equal tightness, so that no defect interferes.
Then, after you have finished the setting, you begin to apply the appropriate plaster. In a soft manner on the place of the fracture, then you begin to wrap the bandage on the place of the fracture itself three or four times, according to what the limb requires, and you tighten your hand slightly with the bandage, then you take it to the upper side of the place of the fracture.
With less tightening than your tightening of the broken area, then you spread the wrapping away from the place of the fracture a little and loosen the tightening little by little until you take from the correct place something suitable, then you take another bandage and wrap it also on the broken place.
wraps, then you take the wrap to the lower side of the fracture, and let your action in tightening and loosening the wrap be as we mentioned in the first upper wrap, then you place between the bandages from the soft padding or rags what straightens the crookedness of the fracture.
If there is crookedness, otherwise do not put anything in it, then wrap another bandage around it, then straighten on these bandages the firm splints immediately if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, then apply what calms that inflammation and removes the swelling, and leave it for days, then tighten the splints on it then, and let the splints be from broad, carved, skillfully prepared halves of reeds, or let the splints be from sieve wood.
which are made from pine or palm stalks or  boxwood or similar things that are available, and let the width of each splint be in this form and this shape exactly.
Except that the splint which is placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, and as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb, in its size and smallness, the image of the splint:
Then tighten the splints with another bandage, according to your first tightening exactly, then tie from above with firm threads according to what we mentioned of the tightening, which is that your tightening on the place of the fracture should be more. And the farther it is from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and thinness, and let them be of wet linen especially, because if the threads are thick, as I have seen done by the ignorant, who make their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them is excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable, because you do not achieve with them the tightening you want, and there should not be less than a finger between the splint and the splint.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the splints after tightening in the healthy places, place under them soft tow or fluffy wool so that nothing harms him from that; and as for if the fracture is with a wound and a rupture occurs in the skin, we will mention it separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, and that if the limb is large, the splints should not be placed on it except after five or seven days or more, according to your certainty that hot swelling will not occur.
And this is a description of the bandages with which the ancients used to bind fractures, dislocations, and sprains; a description of a general, brief bandage that binds fractures and is suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, because it is something that is not overcome by heat or cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill, which is the essence of the flour that adheres to the walls of the mill during the movement of the millstone, then knead it as it is without sifting it, with egg white, and make its kneading neither thick nor thin, then apply it.
A description of another bandage that sets fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of mastic, leadwort, acacia, resin, gum tragacanth, and sugar, ten drachms of each; and of myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; and of the lote tree, twenty drachms; and of Armenian or Roman clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything and mix it with lote water or with egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then use this bandage, for it is good in its composition, quickly sets broken bones, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
A description of another bandage that is also useful for fractures and sprains: take gum tragacanth, mastic, and white leadwort, ten drachms of each; myrrh and aloes, five drachms of each; acacia, six drachms; Armenian clay, twenty drachms. Pound everything finely, sift it, and knead it with water or egg white and use it.
A description of a bandage for joints and bones that have shifted from their places, and it soothes the pain that occurs in them, and for bone cracks and fractures: take purified wool and dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the affected area. This bandage does not have bone-setting power.
But it is excellent in soothing hot swelling and relieving pain especially. A description of another bandage that sets broken bones: take the leaves of the black mulberry and the leaves of wild poppy, and pound them together, and bandage with them while moist. A description of another brief bandage used when a bone fracture is healing.
And if you want to resolve the rest of the swelling, take of the root of leadwort, chamomile, violet flowers, and crushed acorns, one part of each. Pound everything together and knead it with white of egg if the member is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, knead it with coriander water or water and use it.
A description of another bandage stronger in resolving than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs during bone healing: take of the root of leadwort, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, marjoram, violet flowers, and chamomile, one part of each.
Pound everything together and knead with vinegar water, or with fresh water, or with white of egg, all according to the heat of the member and the stillness of its inflammation.  As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken member when setting it.
Except for the cerate made only from wax and oil, and he described it as being of a consistency midway between thick and thin. As for Galen, he was of the opinion that things containing drying agents with some heat should be placed on the broken member when setting it, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and similar things that we mentioned.
A description of a bandage beneficial for weakness and pain: take of alum, chickpea flour, human hair that is shorn or bird feathers, leadwort, and salt, equal parts. Pound, sieve, knead, and bandage with it. As for the amount that the bandage should remain...
Then it is to be loosened; that is to say, you should observe: if no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place occurs in the patient, do not loosen it for many days. If, however, intense itching, worrying pain, or swelling occurs in the place
then hasten to loosen it at once and do not delay that at all, and remove the bandage from it. Then take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it until the itching and pain subside. Then let the member rest for an hour.
Then apply pledgets of carded wool dipped in vinegar and oil or rose oil and bind it on for a night, until you are sure the hot swelling has subsided, the swelling of the member has ceased, and its pain has gone. Then return it to gentle traction and light bandaging, but do not bind it as tightly as at first.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and inflammation, and all the symptoms have completely disappeared, and you need bandaging and traction, then reapply it as you did at first, equally. If nothing of what we have mentioned occurs in the member
then do not loosen it except after three, four, five, or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days; all this according to what appears to you of the condition of the member, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh begins to consolidate upon it.
Then also increase the traction more than your first traction, and also increase the nourishment of the patient beyond what has been mentioned. If you see the place of the fracture has dried and atrophied more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.
Then bathe it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, and let this be every three days, and lessen the traction a little. For by this action nourishment will flow to the member and it will heal quickly.  As for what ignorant bone-setters do, of breaking the member again
if it is not set properly at first, and is set crookedly, that is an error in their action and a great deception. And if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and acted upon it, and I have seen no trace of it at all from any of them. And the correct thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
Concerning accidental fractures in the head.
The types of accidental fractures in the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and this may either sever the bone entirely to the point of reaching the membrane beneath the bone. Just as an axe does to wood.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called axe-like.  Alternatively, the sword may cut some of the bone and sever only its surface, without the cut penetrating to the end; this type of fracture is called an absolute extraction. The wound in these two fractures may be either large or small.
And there are fractures which are comminuted or contused; their cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone or something similar. This fracture may be penetrating, nearly reaching the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be only on the surface of the bone.
The wound in these two fractures is also either large or small. And there are fractures that are hidden in the bone, as fine as a hair; this is a slight crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called hair-like. There is also a fracture caused by a fall or a blow from a stone or the like, which drives a section of the bone inwards, and the area becomes concave, as happens to a copper pot when struck, a part of it being driven inwards.
This most often occurs in the moist bones of the head, such as the heads of children. All these types of fracture may have loose and non-loose fragments.  We will describe the treatment of all of them in their proper place.  We recognize all these types of fracture by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh from them. As for the hair-like type, it is recognized by examining the bone, wiping it and smearing it with ink; the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of fractures, first look at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see among his symptoms those which clearly indicate danger, such as vomiting bile, distension, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, sunken and reddened eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death is inevitable with these symptoms in most cases.
But if you see symptoms that do not alarm you and you hope for his safety, then begin his treatment.  And that is, if the wounded man comes to you immediately after the injury, and this is in the winter months.
Then you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case.  And if it is in the summer months, then you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane under the bone spoils and those symptoms which we described appear.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and contusion, then you should cut away the crushed and contused part as I describe to you; that is, shave the head of the wounded patient.
And uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, when uncovering the bone, you find bleeding or hot swelling, treat that as is appropriate.
That is, pack the place with cloths soaked in a potion and rose oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to chisel away the bone and extract it; this can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first method, it is to cut the bone with a fine saw with a narrow blade.  And this is its image:
Then use another saw after this one, a little wider. And this is its image:
Then also use another saw, wider still than the second. It is necessary that you have a number of saws of different sizes, some wider than others and some shorter than others, and that they be extremely sharp at their edges. Let them be made of Indian iron or good steel.
And use gentleness in striking the saw, lest you shake the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, then it is necessary that you bore around it before using the saws, with the borers which are called non-immersive borers.  They are called non-immersive borers.
Because they do not go beyond the limit of the skull bone to what is behind it, for the borer has a round edge below its sharp head, similar to a small ring or circle, preventing it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to have many of these borers, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for every skull a borer whose sharp end is suitable in length and shortness for the thickness of that skull.  And this is an image of three types. Of the borers: large, medium, and small.
As for the method of boring around the fractured bone, it is that you place the borer on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then you move the borer to another place, and you make the distance between each hole the size of the thickness of an awl or similar.
Then you cut between each two holes with the cutters, and you do this with the utmost gentleness as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with something else from some of the instruments prepared for that.
Such as forceps and delicate hooks. And you should be very careful that the borer or cutter does not touch anything of the peritoneum. If the bone is cleaned and freed from the peritoneum, if it was adhering to it, and you remove it, then you should dress it.
And smooth the roughness of what remains of the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter, except that it should be thinner and more delicate than the other cutters.  If anything remains of the small bones and splinters, then take them gently with the instruments you have.
Then you treat the wound afterwards with cauterization and the ointments which will be mentioned. As for the other method, it is a very easy method, far from danger; Galen mentioned it and praised it greatly, and this is what he said. First, you should begin by uncovering the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and evident, until, when you have uncovered that part, you place the tip of this lenticular cutter underneath it, and this is its image:
Its lenticular part should be smooth so that it does not cut anything, and its sharp part should be on its sides extending lengthwise, as you see, so that the lenticular part rests against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp cutter on the bone. Then you strike the cutter from one side.
With a small hammer until you cut all the bone gently as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, with no fear of an accident whatsoever, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were sleepy.
If anything of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some parts of the bone, then scrape it off with the tip of the lenticular cutter itself, and carefully detach it, for it will detach without harm or fear.
