But if the bone fracture did not penetrate to the membrane, and what was broken of the bone was its surface, and roughness and small fragments remained in it, then you should remove that roughness and extract those fragments with fine forceps, of which various sizes have been made, so that you can use the most suitable and appropriate ones in each place, according to what the work requires.
And the same broken bone and its shape. You should use the widest of those forceps first to remove from the bone, then use a finer one after that, and continue to do so successively, until you come to use the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hair-like fissures and slight fractures, you should use for each one a treatment according to what leads to its improvement; this is something not hidden from anyone who has the least experience in this art and has understood what we have written and explained about large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, then you should take a linen cloth the size of the wound and dip it in rose oil and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular cloth. And dip it in syrup and rose oil and place it on the first cloth, and do this as gently as you can, lest you weigh down the peritoneum. Then use a wide bandage on top and do not tighten it except to the extent that it only holds the wound.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure that the hot swelling has subsided. Then remove it and use afterwards drugs with drying properties, such as the roots of iris, crushed cyclamen, crushed myrrh, and ziraund, and the like. You make suppositories from these drugs.
And sprinkle them on the wound as they are dry. In general, use a medicine that cleanses and does not sting.  In treating the wound, strive to keep it clean, free from grease and dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate in it at all.
Because if pus collects on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and festers it, causing great harm to the patient.  This can occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If blackness appears on its surface, and the patient exhibits the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he will inevitably die. However, if the blackness is caused by a medicine applied to it, and it was in the nature of that medicine to cause that blackness.
It is necessary to take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, smear a cloth with them, and apply it to the meninges. Then treat it with the appropriate types of treatment until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the reduction of a fractured nose.
Know that only the two upper parts of the nose, or one of them, can be fractured, because they are bone;  the lower part is cartilaginous and does not fracture, but rather is subject to contusion, crookedness, and flattening.
If one of its parts is fractured, then you should insert your little finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until you return the nose to its natural shape. Let this be done gently, being careful not to cause the patient pain.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger cannot reach it, then it should be straightened with the end of a slightly thick probe. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same.  And let it be reduced on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, otherwise after seven or ten days, when the acute inflammation has subsided. Then insert a pledget of linen into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two pledgets if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the pledget be of a thickness to fill the nostril. Some of the early bone-setters mentioned that the pledgets should be moistened with fat and changed every day, but I do not think this is necessary; rather, the pledgets should be moistened with egg white mixed with finely ground flour.
Then leave the pledgets until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens. Goose quills, wrapped in soft linen, may be inserted into the nose at the site of the pledgets; this will hold the fractured nose more firmly and prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not essential; you may do it or you may make pledgets. If acute inflammation occurs in the nose during your treatment, apply a compress of quince or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or some diachylon ointment.
If no acute inflammation occurs, you should dress it externally with semolina flour and finely ground myrrh mixed with egg white, then apply a soft bandage, but do not bind the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured slightly.
Or if they are splintered, you should make an incision and remove them with a suitable instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with appropriate ointments that promote healing. If a wound occurs inside the nose, you should treat it with pledgets and use lead tubes. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of lower jaws if fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then if the fracture is only external and it is not fractured in two and is not depressed inwards, then its identification is easy.
Then it is necessary, if the fracture is in the right side, that you insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient, and similarly if the fracture is in the left side, then insert the index finger of the right hand and gently raise the edge of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, you control it to straighten it. If the fracture of the jaw has been broken into two, then it is necessary to use traction from both sides until its straightening is achieved. If it has happened in the teeth, [a] protrusion.
Or if they are separated, then tie what you hope will remain with a gold, silver or silk thread, then place on the fractured jaw a pledget, then place on it a folded cloth, and place on the cloth a large, tight splint or a piece of leather the length of the jaw. Then tie it from above according to what seems suitable to you to tie it and agrees with its binding so that it does not become undone, and instruct the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft drinks. If you think that something of the shape has changed in any way, then hasten to unbind it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what seems suitable to you to tie it and agrees with its binding so that it does not become undone, and instruct the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft drinks. If you think that something of the shape has changed in any way, then hasten to unbind it on the third day. Then repair what you have bandaged with mill dust mixed with egg white or with semolina flour after removing the pledget from it, and place a soft compress on the bandage. As long as that bandage adheres to it and the condition of the bone does not change, leave it.
Do not unbind it until it heals and the fracture hardens. Often this fracture hardens in three weeks. If during that time a hot swelling appears, then use what we have repeatedly mentioned to calm it until that swelling goes away.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, look: if a splinter or splinters have become separated from the bone, then be gentle in extracting those splinters with whatever instrument is suitable for you to extract them. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, then widen it with the scalpel to the extent of your need.
Then, when you have extracted those splinters and nothing remains of them, stitch the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that. And suture the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
In the reduction of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured in front, at the end of the shoulder, and its fracture is in one of three ways: either it is fractured and broken into two without splinters, which is easiest to set.
Or splinters occur in the fracture, which is more difficult to set; or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  The procedure, if the fracture is without a wound, is that you prepare two assistants, one to hold the arm next to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck toward the other side.
Then you set the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is no protrusion or irregularity. If you need more extension, then you should place under the armpit of the patient a ball of rags or wool.
And its size should be according to your need.  You extend and raise the clavicle and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set as it should be. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has gone inwards, then the patient should lie on his back.
And a moderately sized pillow is placed under his shoulder, and the assistant presses his shoulder downwards until the clavicle which is deep rises upwards. Then you correct the fracture and set it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the clavicle has broken and is moving, then you should incise over it and remove that splinter gently. If the splinter is embedded in the bone, then you should attempt to cut it with one of the instruments prepared for that purpose. After placing under the clavicle the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a bend. Its width should be according to the size of the bone, large or small, and its length also according to what is convenient for the work, and let it have two ends, as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound opening which you incised when removing the bone splinter is wide and you are safe from hot inflammation, then join the edges of the wound with sutures.  If the opening is small or you fear hot inflammation, then dress the wound with lint and pledgets according to the size of the incision.
If hot inflammation occurs, moisten the lint in rose oil and vinegar or syrup and apply it. As for setting a bone if it is without a wound or incision, it is to apply to the bone a plaster made from mill dust with egg white. And you place the soft splint upon it, then you place the ball under his armpit if he needs that. Then take a bandage which is very long, and its width is a cubit or thereabouts, then apply a folded pledget over the splint and the plaster, then take a splint of thin wood. Let its width be three fingers and its length likewise, then insert it in a cloth, then insert that cloth with the splint in the part of the bandage which falls on the place of the fracture, then tighten the bandage on the fracture as you wind it. And pass it over his neck and under his sound armpit and under his affected armpit and pass it repeatedly on each side, and as you see that the tightening firmly fixes the fracture, which is not hidden from you.
And the whole point is that the splint does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day; whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the splint has moved, correct that and tighten the bandage. Then let the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm may be raised from his side, and the clavicle fracture may be raised with the raising of the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck, and do not untie the bandage unless something happens in the area such as itching or swelling, until twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a reason for it. Then return to the binding and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle heals and consolidates, and it strengthens and becomes more solid in twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter Six.
Concerning the setting of a fracture of the humerus.  The humerus rarely fractures in its wide part; rather, it fractures at its extremities.  Whenever a part of it is fractured, or it is fractured in its middle, this is known by touch.  Therefore, depending on the shape of the fracture, treat it accordingly.
Straighten it and restore it to its natural shape by any means you can, then apply to the place mill dust with egg white and soft lint, and place on top a pad of folded cloth, then apply a wide splint of wood.
Thin enough to cover the whole shoulder or slightly wider. If, under the splint, there is a prominence in any part of the shoulder, smooth out that prominence with soft lint until the splint lies evenly, then bandage firmly from above with a long bandage.
Bind it tightly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and straighten the splint if it moves from its place. Let the patient lie on his sound side. The shoulder heals in twenty [days].
days or twenty-five days.  When this period is complete, remove the bandage safely, for it is among the bones whose shattering or displacement is not feared. If a fragment of bone protrudes and is pressing under the skin, incise over it and remove it. And do what was mentioned in the fracture of the clavicle to reduce the hot swelling if anything of that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of a fracture of the chest.
The chest may be fractured in its middle, though this rarely happens; but its extremities are more frequently fractured and shattered.  Among its symptoms, if its middle is fractured, is that it inclines downwards, and there is intense pain, difficulty in breathing and coughing.
And perhaps blood is expelled, and the depression in the fractured bone is evident and not hidden from the sense of touch.  Its treatment is that the patient lies on his back, and a pillow is placed between his shoulders; then his shoulders are compressed and the ribs are brought together by the hands from both sides, gently smoothing it in every way that is possible.
And continue until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be. Then apply a bandage and a splint, and place over it a splint of thin wood from willow or similar light wood, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then gently tie it over the fractured bone lest it shift; pass the bandage around to the back several times, and tighten it firmly. Then examine the bandage frequently, and whenever it loosens, tighten it.
And if necessity demands its removal due to an abscess appearing at the site, or pain or swelling, then proceed to remove it, and take off the bandage, and treat whatever has occurred in accordance with its treatment; then reapply the bandage if you see fit. And dress it and keep it firmly bound until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
Concerning the setting of fractured ribs: Know that fractures of the ribs occur in the thick parts adjacent to the back, and their extremities in front are subject to contusion because they are cartilaginous.  The knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense when examined with the fingers.
And set it by straightening the fracture with the fingers in a way that is possible, until the shape is as it should be; then bandage and bind the fractured bone with a splint if it needs it.  If the fracture of the ribs is inclined inwards, then severe pain will result for the patient.
And it will penetrate like the penetration which occurs to one with a chest wound, because the bone penetrates the diaphragm; and there also occurs for him shortness of breath, coughing, and the expulsion of much blood. This is difficult to treat, and the ancients devised many methods for it.
And some of them said that it is necessary to give the patient foods which produce flatulence and wind, so that the belly may swell and expand, and the fracture may be driven outwards. And we dislike this, lest it confirm the occurrence of hot inflammation if it has not occurred; and if it has occurred, then it increases it and confirms it.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on the place, then strongly sucked. This is more similar to reason, except that it is feared that the cupping glass will draw impurities to the place in its weakness. And some of them said that it is necessary to cover the place with wool which has been dipped in hot oil.
And make a bandage between the ribs until it is full, so that the binding is even when you wrap it around; then treat the patient with the treatment of a chest wound, as regards food and medicine. If the patient is burdened by a severe matter which he cannot endure, And the bone penetrates the diaphragm with a harmful penetration, and we feared for the patient. Therefore, it is necessary to incise the place and uncover the fractured rib, then place beneath it the instrument which protects the peritoneum, which has been described previously; and we cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If a hot inflammation appears in the meantime, then hasten and moisten bandages in rose oil, and place them on the place, and treat the patient internally with what calms the inflammation as well.
And he lies on the side on which sleep is easiest for him until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture affects themand this rarely happens to them, and contusions happen to them more often, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back as wellif this happens to someone and you want to know whether he will recover or not. Then look; and if you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and dead, and he is not able to move them, nor extend them, nor flex them, and if you pinch them or prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, nor does he find any pain in them, then know that he will not recover in most instances; he is dying.
And if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking in them, then know that the marrow of the bone has been saved and that the patient will recover with treatment. If the vertebrae of the back are similarly affected and you want to know whether he will recover or not,
look at his legs; and if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has happened in them, then if he lies on his back, wind and feces come out involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily, and if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, then know that he is dying; do not trouble yourself with his treatment.  If none of this happens to him, the matter is less serious. The treatment for what has happened is that you aim to alleviate the hot swelling.
by placing on the injured vertebra rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks; apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply to the place one of the strengthening, drying compresses, and bind it tightly, and order him to remain still and quiet.
And let him not sleep except on the side where he feels no pain until he recovers. If, at the time of the injury to the bone, a splinter or something has broken off from it, then it is necessary to incise the skin and extract that piece of bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures, and then treat it with cicatrizant ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx, which is the tailbone, is broken, then you should insert the thumb of the left hand into the rectum.
and set the broken bone with the other hand as far as you are able to achieve a setting; then apply a compress and splint if necessary, and then bind it.
If you feel a broken splinter in it, then incise it and remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment previously described until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; but if they do fracture, it is in such a way that their extremities are shattered, they split lengthwise, and they incline inwards. The patient experiences pain at the site, numbness, and paralysis of the leg on the side of the fracture.
Its treatment is that you pass your hand over it until you ascertain the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only in its extremities, then set that fracture according to what seems best to you for its reduction, until its shape resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise, or if it has inclined inwards, lay the patient on his stomach so that you may be able to set the fracture.  When you have set it, apply a bandage, and then apply a splint of wood or leather and bind it tightly.
Do not fear for him that the fracture will shift or the splint will come loose. Fill the hollow of the loins with something that will make it level, so that the binding may be even. Instruct the patient to lie on his back or his sound side. If a hot swelling occurs...
...then desist from setting and binding it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply something to calm it, as described above, then return to setting and binding it as is appropriate. If fragments or shattered pieces of bone remain, they should not be removed.
They should not be touched but smoothed from the outside, as we have said, and the binding should be left until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it in one of two ways: one is to take a smooth, curved piece of wood of medium thickness, in this manner:
and you tie two bandages to its ends, then it is hung from a high place and the patient sits on a chair, then his broken arm is placed on the wood until his armpit is touching the middle of the curve of the wood, then something heavy is hung from above it.
Or a servant stretches it downwards, then the physician sets the fracture with both his hands until he returns the fracture to what is appropriate.  The other way is for the patient to lie on his back and his hand is hung from his neck with a bandage.
Then you order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and each stretches to his side. And if you wish the stretching to be stronger, then tighten below the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and each of the servants stretches to his side.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, then the middle of the bandage should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then you set the fracture gently without violence, until the fracture is set as it should be and unites well. Then you bandage it if no hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling appears, then leave bandaging it until the seventh day and place on it a moistened soft wool compress soaked in vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then you bandage it.  The method of bandaging is to apply the compress to the fracture, then apply a bandage of new cloth over the compress, then gather the arm to the upper arm itself and place his hand open on his shoulder.
And you apply the cloth and bandage to the upper arm and forearm so that the forearm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from this and nothing changes in the broken bone. If you fear that something might change, then use splints.
This is to place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than the other splints, and let there be the width of a finger between each splint.  Let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your tightening on the site of the fracture should be tighter.
And the farther from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you see that the placement of the splints and the tightening, as we said, is at the time of your setting the limb immediately, then do so.
And if you fear hot swelling, leave off the tightening and the splints until the seventh day, as we said. Then examine the bandage every three days, lest there occur in the place chafing or swelling, or the nourishment be prevented from reaching the limb by reason of excessive tightening.  Amend all this as we have mentioned.
But if you are confident that none of that will occur, then do not loosen the bandage except after many days. And let the patient lie on his back, his hand on his stomach, and let a moderately full cushion of wool be placed under the armpit.
And examine at every time, night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be disarranged, or the bandage become loose. Amend all this with your effort. And make the patient's food in the order we have presented, that the food be initially light, until the bone begins to consolidate.
Then it is necessary to increase his food. For it is the custom of the arm and leg to consolidate in forty days. Then it is necessary to loosen [the bandage], and he should use the bath and be treated with the ointments which are suitable for this. If the fracture is serious and comminuted,
then do not loosen the bandage from him until fifty days or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, and they are called the ulna; one of them is small, and it is that which is next to the thumb, and the other is large, and it is situated under the small one from below.  So perhaps the larger ulna is broken alone, or the smaller one alone, and perhaps they are broken together.
And when the smaller upper ulna is broken, then its setting is easy and its healing is quicker. And when the lower ulna is broken, then its fracture is bad and its healing is difficult. And the worst is when the two bones are broken together.  If the bone which is broken is the smaller upper ulna,
then it is necessary for the physician, when setting it, to make its extension easy gently until he straightens it. And if the large ulna is that which is broken, then it is necessary to make its extension stronger. And if the two ulnas are both broken, then it is necessary to make the extension very much stronger.
And it is necessary that the shape of the hand, when it is being set and extended, be extended on a pillow, and the thumb of the hand should be above, higher than all the fingers, and the little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should sit squarely on himself.
And let the pillow be in front of him at a height so that the patient does not suffer difficulty.
Then a servant extends the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends from above likewise. Then the physician straightens the bone until he returns it to the best shape he can.  If there are fragments in the fracture of the bone, then they are removed.
Return each fragment to its place to the best of your ability. If a fragment appears that is detached and is piercing the skin, and there is no hope of setting it, then make an incision upon it and extract it in the manner we described previously. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then I have dedicated a chapter to that.
Then take the treatment for that from there. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of your setting, then apply a cloth with a poultice made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be moderate between thickness and thinness, and bind the cloth on it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the poultice and apply the prepared bandage of mill dust with egg white. Then apply the splints; and let the splint that is placed on the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger. And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one forearm bone or in both forearm bones together, then make your binding at the site of the fracture stronger and tighter. And the farther you move the binding upwards or downwards, make the binding a little looser, as mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not very hard. And let the thread with which it is bound be of special linen, moderate between thinness and thickness, as we described. And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected, such as an itching that appears in the limb, then the limb should be washed with warm water until that itching subsides, and the limb should be left unbound for a night so that it rests, then the binding should be resumed.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something like that, then correct all of that to the best of your ability. And also look, if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, then it should be loosened slightly and left for a few days until nourishment flows to it, then tighten it.
If nothing of what we mentioned happens to the patient, then it should not be unbound except after twenty days or so. Then, hang the patient's hand to his neck, and let his arm be straight, and he should carefully avoid turbulent movements, and let his sleep be on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all that depends on the conditions of the temperaments and the conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the setting of a fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpus of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers rarely suffer fracture, but rather they often suffer contusion.  So when a fracture or contusion happens to the hand,
the patient should sit squarely, and before him a chair levelly.
Then he places his hand upon it outstretched, then the surgeon extends the broken bones and sets them, until when they are well-set, then it is necessary to apply a bandage and a splint, if a hot inflammation does not occur; then a splint is applied from above according to the place.
And it is inserted into a soft rag. If the fracture is downwards towards the palm of the hand, then make a kind of sphere from a rag and instruct the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then it is tightened with a long rag.
And let the splint be of soft leather so that it conforms to the skin with the bending of the whole hand, and tighten it as necessary. If the fracture is outwards, then it is necessary to place a splint above and another splint below in the hand so that the hand is open and straight.
Then the bandage is applied as the hand turns and interlaces between the fingers with the ligature. If the fracture occurs in one of the finger joints, if it is the thumb, then let it be set as it should be, then it is bandaged with the hand; and if you wish to make for it a small, upright splint to support the fracture.
And it does not move. If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, little or ring finger, then let them be set and bound with the adjacent sound finger, or bind them all together, this is better, or place a small upright splint upon them.
As we said in the thumb, and examine during your setting and afterwards for hot swelling, then treat it as necessary whenever anything of that occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the setting of a fractured thigh.
The thigh bone frequently fractures and is apparent to the touch because it moves forwards and backwards.  Its setting is by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, the patient lying on his face.
Then each bandage is extended to its side evenly. This is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone; but if it is near the head of the femur, then it is necessary to tighten a soft bandage, either of wool or similar.
At the head of the femur towards the pubis so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension is downwards. Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until he returns it to its natural form.
And the bone unites well. Then it is necessary to maintain the bandage and tightening, unless a hot inflammation occurs in the limb. If inflammation occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot inflammation subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for its tightening, it is necessary to wrap a firm, wide bandage around the fracture two or three times, and what remains is left. Then the leg is bent until the heel reaches the ischium, and a long thread is passed between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, the ends of the thread being above on both sides.
Then wrap around the leg and thigh what remains of the bandage. Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and place one splint on the leg bone.  Then stuff the space between the thigh and leg with soft rags so that the tightening is even.
Then begin with strong tightening from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns.  The farther the bandage is from the site of the fracture, the less strong, softer, and looser your tightening should be.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie with it what is next to it of the splints from above. Then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the heel of the foot, and also tighten with them the ends of the splints from the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place. Then leave the tightening on it as long as no pain, swelling, or puffiness, and the like, occurs in the limb. If any of that happens, quickly untie it and correct whatever occurred, as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a protruding bone splinter, you should smooth it if you can. Otherwise, incise over it, remove it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may be set without adding the leg with splints.
As we mentioned in the humerus and forearm, except that its setting in this way does not cause lameness for the patient.  But if you set it alone without adding the leg, it is inevitable that its owner will limp forever.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days, or a little more or less; all that depends on the differences in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the fracture of the kneecap.
Fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but it is often bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a shattering into fragments; and this happens with or without a wound.
You will ascertain all this by touch, and you will treat it by aligning the separated fragments with your fingers until they are joined and united, as far as careful and gentle manipulation allows; then apply a dressing, and a circular splint if necessary.
Then tighten the appropriate bandage over it, and treat all the conditions we described in the case of other fractures, such as hot swelling and the like, counteracting each symptom with its appropriate remedy until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
On the treatment of leg fractures.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula; they are subject to the same types of fracture as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical. If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured lower down, the result is obvious.
Therefore use extension and reduction, and apply splints accordingly, except that if the leg fracture is severe with many fragments, extension should be gentler and lighter, and you should take great care in its treatment.
In treating the leg there is something additional to the treatment of the arm: when you have set the splints and completed all your work, take two splints of pine wood, such as are used in smoothing walls, which are placed between cracks in the planks, or they may be made from palm leaves or similar material.
Choose those of moderate thickness, not too thin, and let their length be that of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one twice lengthwise with a cloth, and place one on each side of the leg.
Let them extend from the knee to the lower part of the foot, then bind the splints in three places, at the ends and in the middle. By this means the leg is prevented from tilting to either side and is well supported; a wooden gutter may also be used along the length of the leg.
It is placed in this to keep it from moving, and it is especially necessary to do this in fractures accompanied by a wound. Then examine the leg every day and treat it with the greatest care against swelling, inflammation, or other complications. Whenever any such complication arises, treat it accordingly until it heals.  A leg fracture consolidates in about thirty days, or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the bones of the leg and toes.
As for the heel, it is never fractured; as for the bones of the leg, they may be fractured, and the toes also are rarely fractured, but rather are more often bruised.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the leg and you see that some of those bones are overriding others, then let the patient place his foot flat on the ground as if walking, then you put your foot on the elevated part of those bones, and then press down and set them.
until they return to their places, then apply a bandage and splint from above, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot, then bind the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloths and whatever else is needed.
Then if three or four days have passed, untie the bandage, for you will find the bones straight whether they were fractured or dislocated. The procedure is as you see.  As for if some of the toes are fractured, set them and align them according to what I described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured toe along the length of the toe, and let it be a little wider than it, then place under the foot the board that I described to you and bind it firmly. If what was fractured of the bones was two or three or more,
then make for each toe a splint of appropriate size, wrapped in soft cloth, and bind the board to the bottom of the foot, and let each end of the board rest outside the sole of the foot so that it is well secured.
And you should not forget to attend to all that we mentioned to you in other fractures, from the symptoms that we mentioned, and treat each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pubis and the man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her cross-legged, then slowly bend her slightly backwards, and let her be supported from behind; then let the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full and forms a ball within her vulva.
Then the woman is shaken and her hip is raised little by little; then that cotton will come out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the fracture of the bone will be reduced. Then place a pad on her back; and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she may urinate.
And return it in the manner in which it was first inserted, then return to her first pad. Do this for seven days or so, then it will heal. And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a tube to its mouth, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; then the fracture will be reduced. Then pack it with cotton and leave it as described until it heals. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, the same treatment is used for its reduction.
And its setting is as we described for the hip bone.  The correct treatment of these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for one who has some experience and understands this book well, will not be hidden from you, for the fracture itself indicates the treatment in most cases.
Understand the correct way to set and bind it. As for a fractured penis, take a goose's gullet, insert the penis into it, then apply a bandage of cloth, bind it, and leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the reduction of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
Whoever suffers a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, should immediately be bled if the conditions for bleeding, as we have previously explained, are favorable.
If the wound bleeds, you should immediately stop it by applying powdered verdigris if nothing else is available, then proceed with setting the fracture that very day; do not delay it.
If no hot inflammation occurs, good. But if hot inflammation occurs, delay setting it until the ninth day, until the hot inflammation subsides. Do not touch it at all on the third and fourth days, for it will expose it to unfavorable conditions.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, you should try to gently reduce and align it with your hands, using slight traction. If you cannot reduce and align it with your hands, reduce it with this instrument, which is made of iron, about seven or eight fingers long. And its width should be according to the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, according to what he needs for treatment in each type of fracture. Let them be round, with slight thickness, so that they do not bend when force is applied during the procedure.
And let the end be sharp, with a curve at its tip, and let the upper part be thicker, and from its middle downwards, much thinner.  This is its image:
It is called in Greek *peron*, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, curved end should be placed on the end of the protruding bone and pushed gently until the bone returns and is somewhat aligned, then align the ends of the fracture with each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut until the instrument can grasp it. If you are still unable to reset the bone as we described, cut it with similar cuts to those we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the saws, however you can. Then, remove any roughness and thin scales remaining on the bone. If, after resetting it, the patient feels severe, persistent pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. When you have completed the setting of the bone, dip a cloth in a black astringent syrup, especially if it is summer, and do not apply cerates or anything containing fat to the wound.
lest putrefaction and corruption occur. Then, apply the splints when you have finished setting the bone, and leave the wound exposed by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors, the size of the wound.
And beware, with all caution, of tightening the wound with the fracture, for many ignorant physicians have done this, causing their patients either death, or gangrene, or tetanus. Let your binding be loose and relaxed, unlike the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or a large wound, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he feels disturbing pain in the place, you should not apply splints, but make bandages from firm cloths in the place of the splints, and bind it with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that you placed on it with the syrup, then use lint and ointments with which we customarily treat wounds, such as the tetrapharmacon and the like; and you should loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, evening and morning, until it heals and is cured.  It is necessary that you position the limb so that the pus flows downwards easily. If many days pass and the wound does not close, nor does the pus cease, then know that there are small bone splinters.
Then you should probe the wound; whatever of those splinters is loose, extract and remove it; and whatever is not loose, and is piercing the limb and causing pain, work at cutting and extracting it in every way you can.
If the wound suffers from inflammation, or gangrene, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should treat each symptom with its corresponding treatment, as described previously in its chapter.
And what you should pay attention to and keep in mind, if a large bone is broken and protrudes from a limb, such as the femur or humerus and similar large limbs, do not attempt to pull it or remove it. For death often results from this; rather, leave it until it putrefies, for perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complications which occur after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially one near the joints, and it makes the limb unsightly and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
Look; if the complication is fresh, use medicines that constrict, such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, cyprus, acacia, and the like. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent syrup, or with egg white, or with vinegar.
Apply it to the complication in a bandage and bind it tightly. Leave the bandage in place for many days, then remove it and reapply another until the complication disappears, God willing; or bind a tightly fitting lead plate onto it.
For lead has the property of removing everything that grows in the limbs. If the complication has become hardened and severe, and necessity compels its removal, then incise it from above and cut away the excess growth, or scrape it away with some instrument until it is gone, and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture, if it has been set and the limb afterwards remains thin and not as it was originally.
If a bone fracture has been set and the limb afterwards remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes. One of these is either due to excessive loosening and tightening of the bandages, or to excessive tightening of the bandages so that nourishment cannot reach the limb, or to excessive and repeated rubbing, or to excessive movement at the wrong time, or to a deficiency of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
The treatment of this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that his blood increases, and to use the bath and to make him happy and joyful, and so on.  Then, pitch should be applied to the limb to draw much nourishment to it, or it should be repeatedly rubbed with lukewarm water. until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-two.
In the treatment of fractured bones which have healed crookedly and are prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has healed, a crookedness or a projection of the fractured bone, or a complication appears, and the appearance of the limb is thereby disfigured, unless the limb is prevented from its natural function, then one should not accept the opinion of those who claim that the limb should be broken from the head, as many ignorant physicians and bone-setters in our country used to do.
And this action is very reprehensible, leading to great harm, the least of which is disability. But if the crookedness and complication are recent, then it should be bathed with water in which emollient herbs have been cooked, such as mallow leaves and root, and rosemary, and the like.
And it should be bandaged with emollient bandages, like well-made diachylon, or the sap of mallow root should be taken and beaten with chicken fat and lard and bandaged with it, or ripe figs should be taken and pounded with pigeon dung and the like of medicines which are called inhibitors of consolidation.
And sometimes the complication will be resolved by constant gentle rubbing with the hands, and the movement of the limb in every direction at all times should be used.  If the crookedness is old and severe and hardened, and treatment with iron is necessary,
then it should be incised above and the connection of the bone released, and what remains of the complication or bone should be cut with delicate cuts, and gentleness should be used with effort and care, then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
Dislocation of the jaw is the displacement of one of the joints from its place, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the patient severe pain and suffering.  If it happens to someone, he should immediately attempt to reduce it.
He should not delay at all, for if he delays, the place will swell and make the reduction of the jaw difficult. Therefore, he should not delay, nor move, nor stretch it while it is swollen, because it often causes the patient spasms and harmful pains.
But if that happens, he should immediately bleed the patient, then leave him until the swelling subsides somewhat, then bathe the limb with warm water and oil, then gently reduce it, and treat each limb as mentioned in its place.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged, as with fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
Rarely do both jaws become dislocated, and their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced, relaxing a little, or they are completely dislocated so that they relax. towards the chest, so that the saliva of the patient flows and he cannot retain it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue stutters in speech.  But if the dislocation is slight, in most cases it returns of its own accord very easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to hasten its reduction quickly and not delay it at all.  This is done by having an attendant hold the patient's head, while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or he inserts both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers from the outside to level it, and he orders the patient to relax his jaw and release it to go to every side, and the physician levels the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then use fomentations with hot water and oil until their reduction is facilitated, and do not delay their reduction at all, as we said.  When they have returned and become level, and the patient's mouth closes and they do not relax, then apply to them pledgets of cloth with a cataplasm made of wax, oil, and rosewater.
Then bind it gently with a loose bandage, and let the patient sleep on his back with his head elevated between two pillows, lest he move it to the right or left, and let him not strain to chew anything, but let his food be soft gruel.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw is set, then let him eat whatever he desires, and let him use it gently and not strain to open his mouth when eating, drinking, and yawning until the jaw is set and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws is difficult, if they become dislocated at some time and do not return to their places, then fevers and persistent headaches often result from this, and perhaps the patient's abdomen will become distended, and perhaps he will vomit pure bile. If you see this, then know that it is corrupted.
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and the tip of the shoulder.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection to the chest; it may dislocate outwards, and this is evident to the touch.  Its reduction is that the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the area with your hand strongly, and it will return. Then apply a bandage and supports and tighten them. As for its end which is next to the shoulder and connected to it, it does not dislocate except rarely; if it dislocates, then it should be reduced and straightened as we have mentioned and as is possible for you.
Then apply a bandage, supports, and tightening, and instruct the patient to observe rest and stillness until he is healed. With this same treatment, the tip of the shoulder is reduced if it also leaves its place.
Chapter Twenty-six.
In the reduction of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is that it dislocates towards the armpit downwards; the second is that it dislocates towards the chest; and it may dislocate above the shoulder, and this is rare.
It does not dislocate to the rear because of the shoulder blade, nor does it dislocate to the front because of the nerve.  Most often it dislocates and goes downwards towards the armpit, especially in those who have little flesh, because it goes out quickly in them and goes in quickly.
But as for those whose flesh is abundant, it is otherwise, that is to say, it goes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.  And it may happen to some people that from a blow or a fall, the shoulder swells with a hot swelling, so that they think it has dislocated. It is necessary to examine this until you are certain of its truth.
Then you proceed to its treatment.  The dislocation is recognized if it is downwards towards the armpit: if you compare the dislocated shoulder with the sound shoulder, you will find a clear difference between them, and you will find the head of the shoulder twisted, and under the armpit, on touching, the head of the shoulder like an egg.
And the patient is not able to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that apparent to the touch, not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced if it is recent or if the patient is a child.  Its reduction is that a servant raises his hand upwards, then you place the thumbs of your hands under his armpit and raise the joint strongly upwards to its place, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards, and it returns quickly. If it does not return by what we have mentioned, and the dislocation has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use things which relax and soften, such as cooking marshmallow root, fenugreek, and rosemary in water and using it.
Then the patient lies on his back, and you place under his armpit a ball of wool which is moderate between softness and firmness. Then the physician places his heel on the ball and grasps the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, and it returns to its place. And if you wish, reduce it in this way: you bring a taller man than the patient and position him at the side, and he puts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his abdomen. If the patient is light, then something else should be hung on him to weigh him down, and the jaw will return immediately. And it may also be reduced in another way.
And that is to fix in the ground a long piece of wood, the head of which is round in shape like the pestle of a mortar, neither thick nor thin, then it is placed under the patient's armpit, after placing a soft cloth on the head of the wood, and the patient standing along the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand downwards from the other side, and you extend his body also from the other side forcefully, and the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce it by all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: you take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width is about four fingers, and its thickness about two fingers; it has a round head to facilitate its entry into the depth of the armpit, in this manner:
Then you tie a soft cloth on the round head so that the wood does not hurt the patient; then it is placed under the armpit, and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbeam, level with the width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side; then the joint enters immediately. When it has entered in any way possible, a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it goes around from above; then the ball is tightly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is wound from above, and his hand is suspended from his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days, and the patient's food should be light so that the limb may be strengthened, for this is quicker for its healing. Then, after seven or five days, it is untied and exercised; if it is firm and does not relax, then it is healed.
If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture or some other cause, cauterization with three cautery irons should be used as described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not stable,
the bandage and tightening are repeated several times and it does not stabilize, and it falls and relaxes, and he cannot raise it upwards, then know that the nerve in the head of the humerus is severed, stretched, or relaxed; then know that the joint will never stabilize in its place.
As for a dislocation that is towards the chest and breast and backwards, its reduction is by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns.  The rest of the bandaging and treatment are used until it heals. If, after healing, there is stiffness in the limb and slowness of movement,
the patient should use the bath repeatedly until that stiffness softens and returns to its former nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and likewise returns with difficulty also. It dislocates in all directions, especially forwards or backwards.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is visible and palpable, whichever way it is dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one, that will be clearly apparent to you by the depression of the joint. He cannot bend his arm nor touch his shoulder with it. And you should hasten to reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot swelling occurs.
For if a hot swelling occurs, reduction is difficult, and perhaps it will never heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backwards; for this is the worst of all types of dislocation, the most painful, and often death ensues.
And its reduction, if it is of a kind that can be reduced, is that an attendant extends his hand with both his hands and his arm extended, and the physician's hands above and below the elbow, and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both hands, or with the base of his palms, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is forward, it may be reduced by bending the arm sharply until the base of the hand strikes the corresponding shoulder. If the dislocation does not return, use very strong traction; that is, let two attendants extend the arm.
And let two attendants also hold the patient so that he does not move during traction. Then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or a wide bandage around his hand. And when the physician touches the joint with his hands, let him lubricate them with oil so that it will be easier to dislocate the joint.
Then he pushes the joint forcefully until it returns. After its return, a bandage containing astringents and desiccant, along with egg white, should be applied tightly and the arm suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is untied. If the joint is firm in its place, untie the bandage and leave it. But if you see that the joint is not yet firm, reapply the bandage and leave it for several days until it becomes firm, then untie it. If swelling occurs after the reduction and untying of the joint
and there is difficulty in movement, let him use fomentation in the bath and gentle massage and manipulation until it softens, or place a poultice of fat lamb on the joint, then bandage it and leave it on for a day and a night, then remove it and put him in the bath.  When he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then reapply the poultice once, twice, and three times with the bath until it softens. And if you wish to apply a moist, light poultice of cow dung with butter and bind it on, do that several times, for it will soften and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, except that it is necessary to hasten its reduction at the time it dislocates, before the place becomes swollen or a hot swelling appears.
Its reduction is to place the patient's wrist on a board and have an attendant extend his hand, and the physician places his hand on the prominence of the joint and pushes it until it returns. However, it is necessary to see if the dislocation has gone towards the palm of the hand.
Then let the patient place the back of his hand on the board during extension and reduction.  And if the dislocation is prominent towards the back of the hand, then let the palm of his hand be placed on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the prominence of the joint itself. If it returns immediately, otherwise, bind it with a bandage that calms the swelling.
And leave it; do not manipulate it again, for it does not tolerate it, and you will not be able to reduce it after a few days have passed, except that the joint remains crooked and does not harm the patient in any way, unless the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you know that the nerve is severed or bruised; there is no remedy for it except cauterization, which may or may not help. When the reduction of the wrist is complete, apply the bandage we described, then tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then it is untied, and the hand is exercised. If its movement is difficult, and something like stiffness appears in it, soften it with warm water and rub it repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may be dislocated in any direction. If a finger is dislocated towards the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns. Then, bandage the fingertip and suspend it towards the direction in which it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight that day. If it is at night, bandage it in the same way; continue to unbandage it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bandage it at night.  Do this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; bandage it towards that same direction and do as you did before until it heals.  Do likewise if it is dislocated in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the dorsal vertebrae.
If a vertebra of the dorsal or cervical spine is completely dislocated, or if many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death swiftly overtakes the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's stool passes involuntarily; he cannot retain it, and often some of his limbs, whether his legs or arms or one of them, become relaxed. But if only one vertebra is displaced, it often returns to its place.
And its displacement occurs in four directions. That which is displaced backwards is called a hump. As for its treatment, you should observe: if the hump has occurred from infancy, there is no treatment for it, and there is no cure at all. But that which has occurred from a fall or blow, and the like...
The ancients mentioned it with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which are not useful. I have shortened this, so that a little suffices instead of much of what they have described, by my approximation to the meaning and my explanation of it, and I have depicted the instrument differently from what they explained and described.
I say that the hump which occurs in the front of the chest is incurable, and so is that which occurs to the sides as well.  Only that which occurs in the back is treatable, in the way I describe. This is that you extend the patient on his face.
on a level bench near a wall, and spread a damp cloth beneath him so that his chest is not hurt. Then place an upright plank, fixed in a hole in the ground at one end of the bench towards his head, and another plank towards his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the plank  and let it not be firmly fixed in the hole  and another servant holds the other in that manner. Then wrap around the patient's chest and under his armpits with a soft, tight bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the plank at his head and tie it there.
Then tighten with another bandage above his hips, above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages and tie them to the other plank at his feet. Then each servant pulls his plank with the bandage, and the planks do not move from their fixed positions.
except that they are not fixed, as we said. And the physician places his hands on the hump with force until it returns, or he places a board on it, then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits.
and dig in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, a place into which the end of the board fits. Then place the middle of the board on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until he presses the hump.
and it returns to its place. And if you wish to make it with a hand-turned screw, it is that you fix in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the shop two planks whose length is a cubit, and at his feet at the end of the shop also two planks.
Let the distance between each plank be a span. And a hole is made in each plank through which the screw passes. And all the wood is firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all. And insert a round rod, which is the screw in which the bandage is twisted, into the holes of the two planks.
And at its end is a hole in which a rod a span long is fixed with what is twisted, and in the other two planks likewise. Then tighten the bandage which you have tightened on the chest of the patient in the screw which is at his head, and the bandages which you have tightened on his legs in the screw which is at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw, turning his hand with the wrench with which he twists the screw, and the physician straightens the hump as we have explained. And this is an image of the screw, the shop, and the patient:
Then, after the vertebra returns and the place is straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying compress with egg white, then lint, then place on top of the compress a splint of a plank whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length is as much as takes the place of the hump.
And on some sound beads, then it is bound with the appropriate bandage, and the patient uses light food until he recovers. If some swelling remains in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary to use medications that relax and soften, along with the use of the plank which we described for a long time.
A lead plate may be used in this, and a swelling may appear at the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it is thought to be a dislocation, and it is an extra bone which has grown; do not treat it with this treatment, for perhaps death will result.
Chapter thirty-one.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and are not subject to what happens to the other joints of slight displacement and depression, and the hip joint is dislocated in four ways.
That is to say, it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards and backwards, and it is most often dislocated inwards, and rarely forwards or backwards, and the sign of its dislocation inwards is that if you compare the sound leg of the patient with the affected one, it will be longer.
And the knee will be more prominent than the sound one, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg at the groin, and the place next to the groin will be clearly swollen because the head of the femur has gone there.
And the sign of that which is dislocated outwards is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms, and the sign of that which is dislocated forwards is that he extends his leg completely, except that he cannot bend it without pain being in the knee, and if he tries to walk he cannot do so forwards.
And he retains his urine, and his groin swells, and when walking he puts his weight on his heel, and the sign of that which is dislocated backwards is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he bend it before bending the groin, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly in the position of the loin, and as for the reduction of the types of this dislocation, you should look, for if the dislocation is old, it has become chronic with its owner, and he has not attempted to reduce it and it remains in its state, then there is absolutely no treatment.
Then you should not attempt it, and as for he whose dislocation is recent, and whatever type it is from the four types of dislocation, then hasten to twist the joint and stretch it inwards and outwards and move it to the right and left, perhaps...
it returned and did not need any other treatment, and if it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong, helpful servant who will stretch his leg from below, either with his hands or with a bandage tied around his leg above the knee, and another servant who will stretch him from above.
by placing his hands under his armpits, then tightening with a soft bandage on the root of the thigh, and a third servant holding the end of the bandage, and his stretching is either from the front from the groin towards the clavicle, or from behind towards the back.
And their stretching is all at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended, for this type of stretching is a common type for the four types, and if the dislocation returns as we said, otherwise, each type needs what I mention of specific treatment.
As for its specific reduction, if the dislocation is inwards, then the patient should lie on his sound side, then a bandage is applied to the root of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the place under the groin.
Then the bandage is stretched upwards from the groin to the upper body towards the clavicle, then another strong, helpful servant takes with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and pulls outwards with a strong pull, then it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than all the types of treatment by which this member is reduced, and if it is difficult for you and it does not respond to entering with this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary to bind the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage on the heels and on the knees.
And the distance between each one and its neighbour is four fingers, and the affected leg is extended more than the other by two fingers, then the patient is suspended by the head from a wooden beam which is in the house, and his distance from the ground is two cubits.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy hangs onto the patient, and the other boy embracing the thigh pushes forcefully, then the joint returns to its place quickly. As for its specific reduction if the dislocation is outwards.
Then the patient should lie down on the bench according to what we described in the case of the hunchback, and the bandage is tightened on his affected leg in particular and on his chest, then the two wooden pieces are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head, then an additional wooden piece is placed in the middle of the bench, very firmly secured.
A wet cloth has been wrapped around it so that it does not harm the patient, so that the wooden piece is between his thighs so that he does not be pulled downwards when he is stretched, then each servant pulls to his side and the physician adjusts the hip with his hands, and if it responds to reduction.
Otherwise, place the board on him and compress it as we mentioned in the case of the hunchback, except that the patient should lie on his sound side, and if the dislocation is forward, then the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this same position on the bench.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, then presses it with the other hand, and with that he makes the pressure extended downwards towards the knee, and if the dislocation is backwards, then the patient should not be extended downwards.
And it is raised above the ground, but rather it should be placed on something solid, just as it should also be from the dislocation of his hip outwards, according to what we mentioned of his lying on the bench and he is on his face and the bandages are tight.
As we said before, and the compression with the board should also be used on the place to which the joint has gone out, so when the hip joint has returned completely as it should, and the sign of its return will not be hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's legs.
Then if you see them even, and the patient contracts and extends the leg without difficulty, then know that the limb has returned as it should, then at that time join the thighs and apply the bandage and tighten it with a turban so tightly that the hip does not move in any direction.
And the patient must remain still for three or four days, then untie the bandage and compare the leg with the other; if you see them equal in size, then know that the reduction is firm, so release the patient to walk, and if you see in it something of relaxation.
Then return and bandage it and tighten it according to your first tightening, and leave it also for three days, then untie it, and let him walk slowly on it for days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter thirty-two.
In the treatment of the reduction of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards, I mean backwards, and it does not dislocate forwards at all. And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the patient to bring his leg to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. And the reduction of all types of its dislocation is that you seat the patient sitting, and he extends his legs if he is able to do that, and a servant sits behind him to hold his waist and twist him slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
And you place your back against his face and place his leg between your legs, then you grasp his knee with your hands and interlock them between your fingers on his knee, then you press with your hands on the sides of his knee forcefully, and another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh softly without constraint. Then bandage it and adhere the leg to the thigh, then bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. And he should only use a little walking for days until it strengthens.
If it is difficult for you to return it by what we described, then use strong traction with the bandages that I previously described in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be slightly dislocated, or it may be completely dislocated.  Its dislocation is either inwards or outwards, and the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and prominent towards the direction in which it dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight dislocation, it is easy to return it; this is done by gently pulling with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it is completely dislocated, then the patient should sit, and a strong servant should hold him from behind his back at his waist.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above, and with your left hand from below the foot at the point of the heel. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with the left twice, then straighten it with the left hand and push the front of the foot with the right hand towards the leg without force.
Do this twice as we described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg on the third attempt while you pull at the heel. If it returns in one or two attempts in this manner and you see the foot is straight, then it is done. Otherwise, repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If it refuses to return by what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and firmly fix a wedge in the ground so that it lies between his thighs, having wrapped a cloth around it so as not to hurt the patient. Then a servant should hold his thigh.
Then another servant stretches the leg, either with his hands or with a rope tied around the leg's neck. Then each servant stretches in the opposite direction to his companion, with the wedge remaining between the patient's thighs to hold him so that his body does not shift downwards during the stretching. Then the physician straightens the dislocation with his hands.
And another servant holds the healthy leg below, for the dislocation will return quickly.  When the dislocation has returned and you are sure of its correct return, apply the bandage and splint, and tighten it with firm bandages, and bind the foot to the splint below.  You should protect the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
Lest the bandage be too tight and hurt him. Then leave it for two or three days; if the bandage loosens, tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day. And the patient should abstain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be certain that the dislocation will not recur and be spoiled, and will not then accept treatment. If a hot inflammation occurs, then you should use what we previously described in another place concerning treatment and purification to alleviate it until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
You should set what is dislocated with a slight extension without force; this is not difficult, but easy. If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep, then the patient should place his foot on a level surface of the ground.
Or on a board while he is standing as if walking, then you stand and place your foot on the dislocated joint, and then press down on it firmly with your foot until it returns and you see that it is straight and no protrusion appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that takes the whole foot, having two ends, then bind it tightly and firmly for three days, then unbind it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you are safe from recurrence, God willing.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation which occurs with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When something like that happens and you attempt its treatment and setting, it often results in death.
Therefore, no one should undertake the treatment of such a thing except one who is skillful in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, deliberate, not reckless or rash, and that he use in the beginning only those medicines which calm hot swellings, and leave the patient alone.
By Gods power, except for what I hoped would be safe from harm, with the mildness of the disease and you saw in it some hope, then return it from its place at the first, before the hot swelling occurs.
If the limb returns to what you intended, then use the treatment which calms hot swellings and treat the wound with whatever is suitable for it from drying ointments; if the dislocation is with a fracture and there are loose fragments in the bone, then remove them. And follow in that what we mentioned in the simple diseases, what preceded in their places.
And strive your best and avoid entering the path of risk, as I have previously advised you; that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your standing, God willing.
